THE RED PAGE.
MON REVE FAMILIER.
Je fsis souvent ce reve Strange et penetrant
D’une femme inconnue, et que j’aime, et qui
m’aime,
Et qui n’est, chaque fois, ni tout it fait la m6me
Ni tout Si fait une autre, et m’aime et me comprend.
Car elle me comprend, et mon cceur, transparent
Pour elle seule, helas ! cesse d’etre un problkme
Pour elle seule, et les moiteurs demon front
bl£me
Elle seule les sait rafraichir, en pleurant.
Est-elle brune, blonde ou rousse?—Je l’ignore.
Son nom ? Je me souviens qu’il est doux et sonore
Comme ceux des aim<ss que la Vie exila.
Son regard est pareil au regard des statues,
Et, pour sa voix, lointaine, et calme, et grave,
elle a
L’inflexion des voix chores qui se sont tues.
PAUL VERLAINE.
THERE are still people wbo ask the steward
to call them early when the boat is crossing
the Equator. ‘ It’s our first voyage, you know,
and we don’t want to miss seeing it.’ And some
of them travel first-class, too.
Nor is it very uncommon to find in Australian
State schools children 12 years old or more, who
have ‘ done maps ’ to such purpose that they think
the parallels of latitude and longitude represent
actual lines drawn upon the Earth’s surface.
Possibly some readers will be slightly amused at
these instances of illusions from which they are
free. But knowledge is always relative. Many
persons who are quite aware that the Arctic Pole
is not something you can climb up and hang
your hat on are still of opinion that they them-
selves are divided into a ‘body.’ ‘soul,’ and
‘spirit ’ which really exist as separate entities.
Many who have laughed at the anxiety of Porthos,
in ‘Le Vicomte de Bragelonne,’ to comply with
Aramis’s wish and arrive before the Equinox
(‘Well, the fellow will have to be very well
mounted if he beats me ’) still believe that the
heart is the physiological seat of the emotions.
* * * *
The heart is a bit of muscle, like the biceps.
‘Body,’ ‘soul,’ ‘spirit,’ are merely labels of
different manifestations of the Ego. ‘ Body ’is
• well-understood term legitimately applied.
‘Soul’ is a confusion-breeding myth. ‘Spirit’
is little better. All that there is of conscious life
in any individual resides in the brain and attached
nervous system.
‘ Soul ’ is one of the words which, on O. W.
Holmes’s plan, need ‘depolarising.’ But it is so
olustered with false ideas handed down from
less enlightened days that attempts at depolarisa-
tion are hopeless. Its use should be restricted to
poetry and fiotion, and to religion, which is a
mixture of poetry and fiction : when dealing with
facts in unimaginative prose it should be elimi-
nated altogether.
There is a sense, of oourse, in which poetic allu-
sions such as—
John Brown’s body lie 3 mouldering in the grave,
But his soul goes marching on—
are scientifically correct. When John was in the
flesh his sayings and doings and the sayings and
doings concerning him impressed the brain-cells
of many persons in a certain definite way. When
Brown ceased to be in the flesh those impressions
remained as permanent sources of theught and
action.
But ‘ soul ’ is frequently used by children,
theologians and others as representing some
oonorete organ, and for this there is no justifica-
tion. When little Jessie, having been told by
her truthful and intelligent mother that good
people’s souls were white and bad people’s souls
were black, stayed out long after nursery tea to
watch the upward flight from the chimney of
Mrs. Jones’s soul, that lady’s decease being
hourly expected, and at last rushed in enthusias-
tically with * Mother ! mother ! I saw it ! I saw
it! I saw her soul fly out —and it’s piebald ! ” she
made an assertion hardly capable of proof. And
when Brother Wilkes, of the Genuine Methodist
Christians, meets Brother Bilkes, of a similar
species, and enquires—‘Brother! how is your
soul?’ in the same tones that he might employ in
asking ‘How is your liver?’ his solicitude, if it
be real, is probably based on complete ignorance
of physiology. And it is the knowledge of physi-
ology, not the fear of God, which is the beginning
of wisdom.
Although the use of the word ‘ soul ’ in a
poetic sense is conceded to those of religious
habit, it is suggested that in the interests of cor-
rect terminology ‘ brain ’ might be substituted
for * soul ’ in many places throughout religious
literature. In such spiritual songs, for example,
as that in which occur the stirring lines—
O, come and seek Salvation while you’re young !
O, come and seek Salvation while you’re young t
If you don't, the Devil will catch you—
Send a sooty imp to fetch you—
And your soul will burn in brimstone while you’re young—
the change from ‘ brain ’ to ‘ soul ’ would pro-
bably not decrease the elevating effect upon
habitual worshippers. The Brain can do every-
thing the Soul can, except live for ever—and the
Soul’s claims in this direction have never been
passed for payment by a recognised insurance
office. The most convinced advocate of the im-
mortality of the Soul has invariably terminated
his argument by losing consciousness and beginning
to decompose and smell; and though loss of con-
sciousness and decomposition and smell may really
be indications that a new life has begun, they can-
not, in the faoe of existing prejudices, be considered
humanly conclusive. Besides, all the arguments
advanced for the immortality of the Soul can be
advanced for the immortality of the Brain; and
as the Brain has to do the arguing in both cases,
it does not seem fair to let the Soul get the credit
of the immortality which is entirely of the Brain’s
creation.
If the Soul can be dispensed with, some of the
toughest theological problems will disappear along
with it, and the energy hitherto devoted to their
solution may be otherwise applied. Thus it
has always been a moot question where the
Soul came from, how it got into the body, and
where it was located. The antagonism of con-
flicting views has at times been productive of dis-
satisfaction, and there should need no demonstra-
tion of the advantage of substituting for a Soul of
uncertain genesis a Brain whose development can
be watched ab initio under the microscope.
* * • «
Nothing does humanity more credit than its
brain. True that hitherto there has been no con-
scious effort at brain-modification ; but there is
some negative merit in supplying a good instru-
ment, eagerly responsive to a wonderful range of
impressions from environing Nature. When a
cloud travels across the sun the human brain
registers its passage by a change in blood-pressure
—and the human brain has invented the instru-
ments which detect and record that very delicate
change. The brains of many animals would
achieve the one feat, but no brain but the human
brain could achieve the other.
How many million years the brain has taken to
reach its present stage no one can approximately
tell us. Probably it is some hundreds of millions
of years since life first appeared on the planet;
and ever since the brain has been persistently
working its way up through the infinite grada-
tion of species. At present good human brains,
with their attached spinal cords, contain some-
thinglike threethousand million pigeon-holes in the
shape of nerve-cells, in which all the remembered
sights, sounds, tastes, smells, touches, ideas that
have ever reached the individual are more or less
tidily stowed away in various states of preserva-
tion. All these cells are put in telephonic com-
munication by many millions of fibres, which
Vibrate messages from one to the other ‘ at
the speed of thought.’ Impressions derived from
sight are stowed away in one corner of the brain,
and from hearing in another, and so on. The
admirable telephone system enables you to mass
all your impressions on a single point. Thus
when anyone says—
Julius Caesar !
in your ear the vibration of the tympanum is con-
veyed to a certain cell, or cells, in the hearing-
centre of the br;dn —which might be called the
central telephone exchange for the ear. Central
ear exchange immediately rings up all the cells
with ‘ Julius Caesar ’ itnpressions in them, all
over the brain, with which it has telephone nerve-
fibres in good working order. Possibly you have
in your time received no impressions about
‘ Julius Caesar,’ though you may have many about
‘Great Scott.’ But the cells which have ‘Great
Scott’ in them won’t respond to the call for
‘ Julius Caesar ’ any more than the Lands Dept,
will answer for Redfern railway. Consequently
the vibrations from the central ear exchange
‘ ring off,’ as it were, and it is reported to a lot of
other cells, sitting up on a platform to superin-
tend the whole business, that ‘ Julius Caesar ’
isn’t known in the battalion. Consequently
you feel that ‘ your memory is a blank ’ on
the subject of ‘ Julius Caesar.’ All the
same, in one of the nerve-cells that the mes-
sage has been sent to some molecular disturbance
has been set up, and the cell isn’t quite the same
as it was before; and if to-morrow somebody
whispers ‘ Julius Caesar ’ in your ear that nerve-
cell will probably be able to tell you * a message
came here asking for that chap yesterday.’
[That is how memory begins—by a change in
the molecules of some particular cell or cells,
making them more liable to vibrate in the way in
which they have vibrated before. Then they give
forth the same or a similar note to consciousness.
Just in the same way a violin which has always
been played in tune tends to play itself in tune—
its atoms have been taught to swing together in a
particular rhythm by long training, and they op-
pose a distinct resistance to the beginner who tries
to get from them the different vibration which a
discord implies.]
Or, again, possibly you have heard or read of
‘Julius Caesar’ before, but the original impres-
sion left in your brain was, for some reason or
other, only a faint one. Perhaps you were in
poor health, or were cultivating a vegetarian-fad,
and the cell which should have thrown itself
about and remembered J.C. was half-starved for
want of good blood-food, and didn’t do its work
thoroughly. Or perhaps you were ‘attending to
something else ’ —‘ Great Scott,’ for example—and
nearly all the force of the brain was employed in
sending messages to or from * Great Scott,’ and
there was hardly any attention to spare for the
‘ Julius Caesar ’ messages —just as when a circus
comes to Narandera all the juvenile interest con-
centrates upon it, and the boy who ‘ does Blon-
din ’ on the fence cannot get an audience. Such a
faint impression may take some time to recall,
and you will have to ‘think a bit’ while the
nerve-vibrations look up and down the likely cell-
pigeon-holes trying to find the ‘Julius Caesar’
docket.
Or, again, you may have originally received a
good strong ‘Julius Caesar’ impression, but it
may have faded in the course of years; or the
joints of your cell-molecules may hare grown stiff
with age, so that they do not vibrate readily ; or
your original impression may have been derived
through the central eye exchange, and thus there
may be no direct wire from the central ear ex-
change to the cell where it is stored, so
that it takes a good while to establish connection.
In all these oases it is only by degrees, if at all,
that you are able to bring back the original
‘Julius Caesar ’ impression with something like
the old vividness.
But a normal person, when
Julios C.esar!
is shouted in his ear, might receive ‘in a flash
and almost all together mental pictures of—
1. A medallion of J.C. seen at a museum.
2. Caesar’s Commentaries, as studied at school.
3. Shakespeare’s play.
4. Personal conclusions on the life and character of
Caesar derived from a hundred sources.
And so on—resolving themselves into a definite
impression that Caesar was —so-and-so. Probably
these memories are stored in different groups of
cells at some little distance apart, according to
the sense through which they have been derived.
Hence it takes a little time for thought to pass
from one to the other, and if a clear picture
of any single memory is desired energy must be
temporarily concentrated upon that, the contents
of the other cells being successively brought up
and sorted out for comparison. Then at every
step in the process a fresh train of thought may
be started—you went to the museum under such
circumstances—in such company—met so-and-so
—whose book was published the other day —
open to criticism on such a point just
as the Sheffield life of Gibbon is some-
body quoted in a Sydney paper Gibbon’s reflec-
tions on finishing his history in the summer-
house in his garden—Lytton first emphasised the
passage—as far as you know—you remember
quoting it, with stress on its pathos and dignity,
to somebody who merely commented ‘ Summer-
house in his garden ? How ever did he keep the
wind from blowing his papers about? ’ . . .
and sO on, until death. (Poe has admirably
suggested some externals of the process in ‘ The
Purloined Letter.’)
» * * *
But the point is that every one of these thoughts
or impreosions represents a more or less perma-
nent change in the disposition of certain brain-
molecules. There are probably thousands of mole-
cules to a cell, and (as stated) some three thousand
millions of cells, so that the combinations are
practically infinite enough to represent even all
the thoughts which pass through the brain in a
life-time, and to register as ‘ memory ’ the most
vivid and definite of them.
And another point is that since the brain can
intentionally recall any image which it has
received, it is possible to crystallise the most
valuable impressions for permanent use. That is,
it is possible to more or less strengthen by exer-
cise the different sets of brain-cells in any indi-
vidual—just as Sandow deliberately strengthened
by exercise one set of muscles after another until
he reached something like all-round perfection.
The importance of this lies in the fact that in the
most cultured man’s brain there are groups of
cells which lie dormant and useless because they
have not been properly trained in youth. Stone-
wall Jackson only knew two tunes —one was
‘ Yankee Doodle ’ and the other wasn’t—simply
because the nerve-cells in the hearing centre of his
brain had not been trained to distinguish and
appreciate musical notes. And there is a Sydney
musician who boasts, probably with truth, that he
can detect when a single note is one-sixteenth of a
tone sharp or flat as compared with the correct
notes preceding or following it, yet who passes
paintings without a glance—because his dormant
sight-centres give no response to the delicate
stimuli of color.
And a third point is that most molecular changes,
once established, persist as ‘ habit.” ‘ Habit ’in a
brain-cell, or a spinal-cell, or a muscle-cell, simply
means that the cell-molecules through constant
repetition of any movement are disposed in a
particular position, and tend to vibrate in a par-
ticular manner which corresponds with a par-
ticular action ; and when you ask them to vibrate
in another manner it is like ‘rubbing a cat the
wrong way,’ when you ask them to remain in
another position it is like throwing a stone in
the air and telling it not to fall down again.
The brain-cells, for example, which controlled
Parkps’s speech had been accustomed in his
youth, when impressions are most deeply graven,
to send such a message along their motor-
fibres that his vocal organs said ‘the ’ouse.’ When
he grew older he learnt that he should say ‘ the
house,’ and the fact was so impressed on other
brain-cells that he tried to say * the house,’ and he
could say it when he concentrated his attention on
the disastrous h. But when he had to devote the
higher centres of his brain to thinking of his
speech, and deciding what words should comenext,
the lower centre escaped from his control, the
naughty molecules vibrated in the old familiar
way, and he said ‘ the ’ouse ’ as regularly as clock-
work. He knew he was wrong directly his ear
caught the sound and carried it back into his
brain—too late! Possibly, by very hard labor,
he might have done something analogous to cut-
ting a new channel through the nerve-matter of
the disobedient cell, but so rapidly does the
nerve-substance lose its plasticity that the enter-
prise was practically impossible. After middle
age, and probably for a good while before it, the
average man is a slave to the impressions that
early habits of thought have imprinted on his
brain.
Herein partly lies the physiological explanation
of conservatism. A man’s brain works along tha
furrows ploughed in it by youthful thoughts,
which generally reflect the prevalent temper of his
day and generation. As he grows older, another
generation is born, with new thoughts to which'
he has a mental repugnance—partly because the
molecular modifications of his brain absolutely
prevent him from understanding them, and his
nerve-cells no longer possess the energy to make
fresh modifications.
Then atavism, or heredity, comes into play as
well. A brain is modified by the environment into
which it is thrown during its own brief existence,
but the general lines of its status and character
are laid down by the whole pre-existing environ-
ment and reaction to environment of every life
lived on this planet. Any attempt to leave those
lines is made in defiance of the whole of the
world’s past history. Thus it is that progress is
slow and confined to Youth. The inertia of the
Race may be overcome for a while, but when it
adds to itself the inertia of Age the combination
is irresistible.
So it is that even in ‘ this enlightened day 1
comparatively few people retain in later life the
habits of reading acquired in youth. Instead of
a book a newspaper suffices them. Instead of a
book of history or philosophy a novel attract
them. This is partly because the older brain is
less capable of sustained attention. But it ig
partly also because the reading habit is still a
habit foreign to humanity—acquired too recently
to ‘come easy’ to the brain. A practised writer
rarely realises what blurred images the words
which are so clear to him produce on the brains of
most of his readers—with what difficulty they
gather the meaning from the symbol—and how
slight is the conscious impression produced upon
them by language which to him seems moßt
forcible.
This explains why, as remarked the other day
in The Bulletin, Boss Tweed, of the N.Y. Tam-
many ring, was accustomed to say of his oppo-
nents, ‘ I don’t care what they writes —it’s the
pictures as kills me.’ To begin with, the foreign
element in New York is so large that the percent-
age of illiteracy is unusually high for America;
and many of those on whom Boss Tweed relied
for support could not read tho proofs of his
knavery at all, while everyone with eyes could
understand a picture of Tweed with his hands
buried in the coin of the City Treasury.
But even to many of those who could read, the
printed assertion ‘Boss Tweed is a thief’ would
convey an impression nothing like so distinct aa
the picture, for word-symbols set in motion in tha
average individual brain-cells nothing like so
energetic or so well-developed as the cells which
the picture energises. In the same way, humanity
has long been used to receiving vivid impressions
through the centres in the brain affected by
speech, and these centres are exercised as continu-
ally by the individual as they have been by the
race, so that they are in the normal individual
exceptionally powerful and well-nourished. Hence
unaccustomed readers’ habit of repeating the
words aloud as they read —in order that the
impression made by the sound upon the brain
may reinforce the much weaker impression
made by the word-symbol. Hence, too, the habit
of oral instruction in schools; and of lecture-teach-
ing at the universities. Were the lesson or lecture
merely written on a blackboard the average pupil
would not assimilate it half as well. On the other
hand, trained readers, who have so exercised and
energised certain brain-cells that the printed
word yields its image without effort, and often a
more vivid image than the spoken word, fre-
quently look upon attendance at lectures as a
waste of time, since they can learn in ten minutes
from a book the message which it takes a lecturer
two hours to deliver.
• • • •
And so on, and so on. The study of the brain,
which has made immense strides in so short a
period as the last ten years, is one of the most
fascinating and important that modern science is
engaged in. There is, by the way, no precise
warrant for supposing that communication within
the brain proceeds in the same manner as tele-
phonic communication, or that the nerve-cells in
any literal way resemble pigeon-holes. The illus-
trations are good ones, however, and the telephonic
analogy is probably very close.
THE BURIAL OF MOLIERE.
Dead —he is dead ! The rouge has left a tra*e
On that thin cheek where shone, perchanae, a
tear.
• Even while the people laughed that held hm
dear
But yesterday. He died, —and not in grace,
And many a black-robed caitiff starts apace
To slander him whose Tartufife made them tear,
And gold must win a passage for his bier
And bribe the crowd that guards his resting-place.
Ah, Moliere, for that last time of all,
Man’s hatred broke upon thee, and went hy,
And did but make more fair thy funeral.
Though in the dark they hid thee
Thy coffin had the cope of night for pall.
For torch the stars along the windyjsky !
ANDREW LANG, from J.TRUPF[?].

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE BATTLING DAYS.
So, sit you down in a straight-backed chair, with
your pipe and your wife content,
And cross your knees with your wisest air, and
preach of the ‘ ‘ days mis-spent ” ;
Grown fat and moral apace, old man ! you prate
of the change “ since then
In spite of all, I'd as lief be back in those hard old
days again.
They were hard old days; they were battling
days ; they were cruel at times —but then,
In spite of all, I would rather be back in those
hard old days again.
The land was barren to sow wild oats in the days
when we sowed our own—
(’Twas little we thought or our friends believed
that ours would ever be sown)
But the wild oats wave on their stormy path, and
they speak of the hearts of men—
I would sow a crop if I had my time in those hard
old days again.
We travel first, or we go saloon—on the planned-
out trips we go,
With those who are neither rich nor poor, and we
find that the life is slow ;
It’s “a pleasant trip” where they cried “Good
luck ! ” There was fun in the steerage then—
In spite of all, I would fain be back in those vaga-
bond days again.
On Saturday night we’ve a pound to spare—a
pound for a trip down town —
We took more joy in those hard old days for a
hardly spared half-crown ;
We took more pride in the pants we patched than
the suits we have had since then—
In spite of all, I would father be back in those
comical days again.
’Twas We and the World—and the rest go hang—
as the Outside tracks we trod ;
Each thought of himself as a man and mate, and
not as a martyred god ;
The world goes wrong when your heart is strong,
and this is the way with men :
The world goes right when your liver is white, and
you preach of the change “ since then.”
They were hard old days; they were battling
days ; they were cruel times—but then,
In spite of all, we shall live to-night in those hard
old days again.
HENRY LAWSON.
M‘CARTHY THE TENDER-HEARTED.
McCarthy lived in Booligal.
A sandy-whiskered man, and tall,
Was he, with eyes that overran
At tale of wrong to beast or man.
When he th’ Armenian horrors read
About, he clenched his fist and said,
“ I’d make the Sultan look dam small
If he was here in Booligal! ”
Did he a wounded chicken see,
He’d take it up—so kind was he—
And see it got no further hurt,
And keep it warm inside his shirt;
And if an old stray horse he found
He’d, weeping, lead it to the pound—
So tender was his heart withal,
McCarthy’s heart, of Booligal.
No funeral was held complete
Unless M'Carthy had a seat
Behind the hearse. To see his grief—
To see his vast white handkerchief—
You’d think, till you were undeceived,
He was the Party most Bereaved —
Por he could weep enough for all
M'Carthy could, of Booligal.
They tell a tale—it may be true;
I hardly think it is ; do you?—
How teamsters when the track was dry
Would “ Gee up, Brimstone ! Polly ! ” cry,
And say —“ We have a tale of woe
To make McCarthy’s tears o’erflow,
And they will make a. river small
When we get into Booligal.”
A stranger to the township came.
They told him of M‘Carthy’s fame,
And much he longed the man to see—
There all at once “ Great Heav’ns ! ” said he,
“ What mean these cries and sounds of strife? ”
“M'Carthy’s hammerin’ his wife,”
Said they—“ It’s her_ you’re hearin’ squall,
M‘Carthy’s wife of Booligal.”
O.R.
BALLADE OF THE BUSH.
When you have spent lone, endless days
In tending sheep upon the plain,
Beneath a high sun’s streaming rays
That weave strange doubts if you are sane,
While sweat-drops make a changing stain
Upon the ground, which red heat frees
And makes the spot quite fair again—
It’s pleasant then to talk to trees.
The moon fantastic shadow lays
When summer nights hold cloudless reign,
Grim, ghostly shapes that work amaze,
And build queer fancies in your brain ;
And, too, the mournful wail gives pain
When far-off curlews sound their glees ;
All help your mind to crazy vein :
It’s pleasant then to talk to trees.
For slow-spun weeks no human phrase,
Or voice of man unlinks the train
Of curious thoughts on human ways,
That churn your mind until you’d fain
The stumps would dance to ease the strain—
Sometimes they do in your dis-ease,
And when they don’t, to make time wane
It’s pleasant then to talk to trees.
Oh, he who’s borne the heavy chain
Of long grey hours, nor mortal sees,
Will grant the truth of this refrain.
It’s pleasant then to talk to trees.
Vic.
H. A. CONANT.

SATURDAY, MARCH 20, 1897.
The Law and the Larrikin.
Within the last month or so the inevitable
“ larrikin outrage ” has been a little more
violent than it commonly is in and around
Sydney. It is always more or less violent ;
the “ push ” is always present, and the un-
provoked “ stoushing ” of somebody out of
pure cussedness is an event which happens
several times every week with varying
degrees of ferocity. But when there is more
unprovoked battery than usual, and the vic-
tim’s brains protrude further than they
generally do, and the accompanying language
is a little more horrifying than the ordinary
sample, and one or two deaths arise, the cir-
cumstance is called an ‘ ‘ Outbreak of Larri-
kinism,” and the daily paper wakes up to
sudden interest in the question. On
these occasions the daily paper and its in-
furiated correspondent, “Pro Bono Publico,”
invariably announce that, “ all other means
of repression having failed,” it is necessary
to resort to the lash for the suppression of
the native hoodlum. This stereotyped
fallacy is now going the rounds once more
with the usual want of any good result.
Nothing ever comes of the daily paper’s
periodical shriek for more flogging; it rises
after some especially awful slab of brutality,
and it dies away again when the event is
forgotten, and leaves things as they were.
None of its advocates advance any sugges-
tions for the prevention of larrikinism; their
one idea is to get level with the offender on
gory principles after he has done his outrage
—provided he can be caught. And if the
object of the law is merely to satisfy its
feelings with a bloodthirsty retribution after-
ivards, and to give an occasional brutal
offender—a very occasional one—what he
deserves, the lash is “all there.” If, on the
other hand, the idea is to suppress larrikin-
ism and prevent outrages and wipe out the
whole “ push ” organisation it is a miserable
failure—for reasons which are already suf-
ficiently distinct, but which it may be ivell
to explain over again for the benefit of those
who do not readily digest reasons.
The first obvious cause of larrikinism is
the woeful weakness of the police force in
all the Australian cities. The stereotyped
answer to this is always supplied by the Fat
Person, who writes to the papers pointing
out that the police force of Sydney, Mel-
bourne, Adelaide, and elsewhere in Aus-
tralia, is quite as strong in proportion to
population as that of London, Glasgow,
Paris, and other old-world cities. This
fact is commonly deemed to be conclusive.
In reality it has no earthly bearing on the
case. Sydney is vastly more extensive in
proportion to its population than Glasgow
and other cities which serve as favorite
examples. Being so much more scattered
it requires probably twice as many police in
order that the law-abiding citizen may have
as good a chance of finding a man in blue
when he needs one, and in order that an
equally efficient watch may be kept. More-
over, the organised “push” is practically
unknown out of Australia, and it requires
probably two or three or four times as many
police to make reasonably sure of arresting
the ruffian who is almost always backed up
by half-a-dozen or a dozen reliable sup-
porters, as to capture the one who is not so
supported. The quantity of police-power re-
quired in Sydney or Melbourne is to be judged
by their own circumstances; not by entirely
different circumstances in cities at the other
end of the globe. The fact that riotous out-
breaks are of daily occurrence and that they
occur almost always when there is no police-
man in sight, proves clearly that there
are not enough constables ; if there were,
they would be in sight much oftener. An
obvious cause of larrikin outrages is that the
person committing them has always, owing
to the scarcity of police, a three to one, or
possibly a ten to one, chance of not
being caught at all. The advocates of
the lash vociferate for power to do more
awful things to the offender when he is
caught, but they might almost as wisely
attempt to suppress the Armenian atrocities
by giving Jawbone Neild authority to do
something awful to Abdul-Hamid if he can
capture him. The chance that any parti-
cular larrikin may be arrested at the right
moment may be a little greater than the
chance that “Major” Neild will capture
Sultan Abdul-Hamid, but it isn’t so much
greater as to be worth betting upon.
* * * *
Even with the present miserably inefficient
system, however, the criminal larrikin is
caught now and then—and fined 40s. or so.
He is sometimes fined more than 405., but
the chance that any particular hoodlum will
be fined more than 40s. is another thing
which is not worth betting upon. Every
magistrate has very extensive powers for the
suppression of the evil; he can sentence the
offender to a long term of imprisonment with
or without the alternative of a very heavy
fine—one heavy enough to cover the expense
of catching him and leave a balance over to
aid the depleted State revenue. But he
doesn’t use his full powers in one case out of
a hundred, or anything distantly resembling
that. He has also very extensive powers for
dealing with cases of drunkenness, foul lan-
guage, and other minor but highly objection-
able offences, but he generally either releases
the prisoner gratuitously or charges him
something varying from ss. to £l. One
notable exception to this rule is the un-
speakable Sydney police-magistrate, who,
a little while ago, not only discharged
a person accused of disgusting language, but
severely reproved the arresting constable for
interfering with him at all, on the ground
that his disgusting language was quite
“customary.” If the magistracy won’t use
the powers it already possesses, it is obvi-
ously useless to entrust it with more powers ;
the only result will be that it won’t use them
either. Also, seeing that there has been no
systematic and continuous effort—in fact,
there has been hardly any effort at all—to
use the powers already in existence, no one
can tell whether these powers are sufficient
or not. The whole case for the lash rests on
the assertion that the existing powers are not
sufficient, which assertion is absolute assump-
tion and ignorance, and the ordinary or
garden variety of wooden-headedness.
* * * *
If the lash is requisitioned to suppress the
hoodlumism which disgraces Australian cities,
and the rest of the system is left as it stands,
the result will be something as follows :
About one brutal rowdy in ten will be cap-
tured, as heretofore ; if there are not enough
police to effect the captures it doesn’t matter
whether the possible penalty is 2s. 6d. fine,
or flogging, or crucifixion —-none of these
things will make the force any more
numerous than it is. Moreover, there is
often a lamentable uncertainty whether the
person so very occasionally captured is the
real offender. Owing to the scarcity of
police it is very rarely he is arrested on the
spot; in most cases he is run down after-
wards on the strength of a very loose and
unreliable description, and “ identified ” by
the victim as the man who kicked his head
in the dark—the victim, in his natural ex-
asperation, being prepared to identify almost
anybody rather than get no satisfaction at
all for his injuries. Assuming, however,
that the occasional arrestee is the right per-
son, he will be brought up before the regula-
tion weak-kneed magistrate, who will then
have power to award any sentence from ss.
fine up to a rousing and breezy episode
at the triangles instead of his powers
ranging from ss. fine to, say, six months’
hard labor. As that flabby person, with
his present authority, generally fines the
prisoner 40s. it may be presumed that—
being the same person and equally flabby
he will continue to do so. In about one case
in a hundred, perhaps, flogging will be
ordered, but, considering that the beak
doesn’t use the full powers he already pos-
sesses anything like once in a hundred times,
even this is very unlikely. Assuming, how-
ever, that it does happen, the total result
will be that once in a thousand times the
right or wrong person will be flogged, and
otherwise things will go on as usual.. The
idea that flogging would be largely availed of
arises from a wholly unreasonable view of
the average magistrate’s force of character.
About once a week, or oftener, when a notably
brutal offender is dragged up, the presiding
Draco almost weeps because he hasn t
authority to order the lash as the only
thing bad enough to meet the case. Then
he generally passes a sentence of 14 days at
the outside. The spectacle of a man too
weak to use the powers entrusted to him,
even in cases where he alleges that the case
calls for all of them and much more besides,
yet craving for more authority, is a pitiable
example of weakness.
1 * * *
The Bulletin is firmly of opinion that
larrikinism can be suppressed, but not by
the spasmodic shriek for an occasional horror
which is almost the only effort that ever is
made in this direction. The first essential is
to largely increase the police force —to mul-
tiply it by two or three or four if necessary ;
it certainly requires to be multiplied by two,
and even if it was increased fourfold it would
not be much larger, in proportion to the
area to be guarded in Sydney and Mel-
bourne, than the police strength of many
European cities. Then there would be a
decent chance of arresting the criminal
rowdy every time, and whether it is proposed
that his ultimate fate should be flogging,
imprisonment, summary execution with
horrors, or a half-crown fine, the first essen-
tial is to make his arrest a reasonable cer-
tainty. The next essential is to limit the
discretion of the weak-kneed magistrate,
whose ideas of the value of order, and the
objectionableness of brutal assault, obscene
language and the like, are so lamentably
mixed that he habitually assesses anything
short of murder at something not much
above 40s. If the lowest price of the
great Australian adjective were fixed
at £5, and that of the more objec-
tionable kinds of obscenity at £lO, and
the minimum for brutal assault was £2O, and
the corresponding terms of imprisonment
were lengthened in the same proportion, and
the maximum punishment for the same
offences was enlarged considerably, it would
at least provide a good amount of revenue to
pay for the extra cost of police supervision.
Also, though the magistracy might be as
cowardly as it now is, and as badly afraid to
inflict the maximum sentence in extreme
cases, and its ideas of varying degrees of
criminality might be as loose as they are at
present, still the limits within which it could
make a conspicuous ass of itself would be
somewhat narrowed. The average sentence
passed would still, almost certainly, be much
less than the law already allows, but the scope
afforded to the bench for not enforcing the
law would be somewhat lessened. In other
words, it is needful to greatly diminish the
discretion allowed to magistrates, the average
magistrate having conclusively demonstrated
that he has no discretion worth speaking of.
And if, when the magistracy has thus been
compelled to really administer the law,
larrikinism is not in a great measure sup-
pressed, it will be time enough to say that
the law is inefficient. At present no one
knows whether it is inefficient or not. It
Jiasn’t even had a trial.
Bowing to Baal.
Possibly Premier Reid had from the first made
up his mind to bow the knee to the Imperial
Baal. Possibly he hadn’t. The influence
restraining him from going down on his marrow-
bones quite so soon as the ruck of Premiers
may have been diplomacy, or it may have been
conscientiousness, or it may have been a blend
of both. Nobody knows exactly but Premier
Reid, and Premier Reid won’t tell. Anyway,
he will now have the pleasure of going, com-
bined with the credit of only going under
pressure. It isn’t much credit, but there is
possibly some political capital to be made from
it. That any Premier should leave Australia
at this time unquestionably involves a betrayal
of this country’s interests to the monarchical
fetish. We are borne on a tide of federal
enthusiasm whose strength has considerably
surprised the old hack politicians. If it is
taken at the flood by the men whose -position
makes them the natural agents and directors of
public opinion, Brunton Stephens’ “Not
Yet ” poem may lose all but an archaic and
literary significance. If we miss the current
when it serves the federal cause may be bound
once more in shallows and in miseries—and the
blame will lie largely at the door of the poli-
ticians who junketed to England because they
were ready to miss the chance of making a
nation, and averse from missing an opportunity
of cutting tenth-rate figures in an empty cere-
to°forgeT h ICh enli 6 lltened History will be eager
* * * •
No Premier has any right to leave this
country three weeks after the Federal Con-
vention meets. If he feels no duty to himself
he yet owes duty to Australia. Why should
our national interests be sacrificed because in
the ridiculous course of hereditary monarchy
a commonplace queen has vegetated for 60 years
upon a commonplace throne? In the eves of
the next century Queen Victoria will\ e a
cipher. Her predecessors were mostly odious:
she is useless. And Premier Reid, who has
done Ins best to damn Federation once now
does his best to damn Federation a second tim^
by deserting the national ship at the crisis of a
promising voyage. The Bulletin, for one
will make no pretence of regret if next time
Federation should damn Premier Reid. Yet it
is a pity. Premier Reid is an unreliable finan-
cier, and is objectionable as having travelled on
bogus Freetrade, but, so far as this paper
knows, he is personally a comparatively honest
politician. Whatever his colleagues may
have done, or attempted to do, he personally
has not filled the public offices "with his
friends and relations —so far as is known. He
has not flooded an electorate witli telegrams
urging the election of a supporter, and made
the taxpayer pay for the messages —so far as is
known. He has not begged his colleagues to
buy unnecessary goods from a political friend—
so far as is known. On the contrary, he has
remodelled the public service and taken patron-
age out of the hands of Ministers. He has
introduced direct taxation—exempting poverty
and making the rich landowner disgorge some of
his “ unearned increment” for the first time in
New South Wales history. He assisted to pass
the most comprehensive Act for the exclusion of
objectionable aliens which Australia has seen.
He assisted to pass an admirable Navigation
Amending Act which would have ended much
of the tyranny over seamen had not the Upper
House emasculated it. He was largely instru-
mental in bringing about the first referendum
in his province—and the first referendum any-
where is no small sign of democratic progress.
* * * *
Even did these improvements or attempted
improvements on the pre-existing order of
things stand alone they would make a very fair
three years record for the average Australian
politician. What a pity that Premier Reid
cannot cap his present climax by posing as the
one strong man in a truckling band, and return-
ing answer to Queen Victoria that the pro-
vincial interests which require his presence
in Australia are so important, and the
national interests at this special juncture are
possibly so stupendous, that he is quite
unable to 20 and be an insignificant
little fat man at the tail of her
record reign procession ! Premier Reid, in
fact, is asked to remove himself from Australia,
where he is a highly important figure, to Eng-
land, where he will be nobody. He is asked to
deprive Australia of his services—certainly
valuable, possibly essential—at what may prove
to be a most momentous epoch in her history,
in order to add one unnecessary name to the
list of persons present at a stupid and
ephemeral pageant. Very well, let Premier
Reid go—and take the consequences.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Political Stew.
The cynical corruption of Q. politics has
become commonplace. People have grown so
used to hearing that this politician declared on
his “honor” that an insolvent bank was
solvent, thereby enabling it to keep afloat
three years, receive millions of Govt, money
specially borrowed to tide it over its difficulties,
and pay scores of thousands in dividends on
non-existent capital; or that some politician,
whether or not in return for services rendered
or to be rendered, had been courteously released
by the bank from a heavy liability ; or that
some other politician bought a business for so
much, and obtained twice or thrice its price-
many thousands of pounds—as an overdraft on
its security ; —things like these are so familiar
that information that the whole of the Q.
Parliament had been arrested for a combination
of horse-stealing, arson, and embracery would
only excite mild surprise that some new-chum
police-officer had been found ass enough to
waste his time in serving the warrant. Con-
sequently the political appointments which are
greasing over Premier Nelson’s trip to Eng-
land, and sliding Truthful Tozer as his locum
tenens down somewhat reluctant gullets, are
judged of no particular importance in the
northern Lepers’ Home. Groom and Dickson
—an ex-Speaker and ex-Minister representing
the old Liberal tail in alleged Opposition—have
been limed just as Premier Nelson himself was
limed by MTlwraith. Groom gets the chair-
manship of a Lands Commission which should
put a very substantial honorarium in his
pocket. Its scope is rather wider than the Pacific
Ocean, and Groom and the undistinguished
Govt, hacks who are his colleagues start business
by tripping to Sydney to pile up fees and exes,
and “ collect information ” in the pleasant
autumn weather, while the famous local Public
Works Committee smiles in sympathy.
Dickson, a loose fish destitute of political
principle, who in the existing Parlia-
ment has supported Opposition or Minis-
try just as it suited his book, becomes
a salaried Minister. An alleged Mines Com-
mission has been created to fill the mouths of
other reliable party hacks—Smyth, Hamilton,
and O’Connell—whose faithful yelping for
their employers in season and out of season
has, it must be owned, deserved some reward ;
and that shifty politician, John Plumper
Hoolan, also gets a billet. The climax is
capped by the appointment to the Leg. Council of
Allan, a squatter-crony of Premier Nelson’s,
and Archibald, the candidate for Warwick
who consented after much pressure to retire in
favor of Attorney-General Byrnes when the
North Brisbane electors applied ice to that
gentleman’s swollen head. And thus does
Boodlew'raith justify its name and reputation.
Benevolence and Advt.
The S. A. Treasury will realise fully £lOO,OOO
in probate duty out of the estate of Thomas
Elder, the S.A. millionaire, who died a few
days ago. Another ,£154,000 goes in charity —
the same partly useful and mostly ornamental
kind of charity which all good millionaires
affect. Adelaide University gets <£25,000,
wherewith to manufacture more B. A.’s; .£20,000
goes to found a Chair of Music in the same
institution ; £20,000 to the University Medical
School ; £25,000 to buy pictures for Adelaide
Art Gallery ; £16,000 to help various churches
and missions ; and £BOOO goes to hospitals
which with the medical - school endow-
ment form the only really practical item in


the list. The charity of the defunct mil-
lionaire has two notable features ; it mostly
goes in works of dubious ornament, and it is
mostly confined to the cities which have more
than their fair share of it already, while the
backblocks towns, which are most in need of
ornament, get very little. If some eminently
practical millionaire who wanted to leave a
name behind him when he was dead, or who
wanted to get a big advertisement while living
with a view to a title or for some other pur-
pose, would present his country with the money
to build a new railroad in some place where it
is badly wanted, or would make a hundred
miles of good road where it is much needed, or
would dredge a harbor, or provide some in-
sanitary country town with a complete water
and sewerage system, or would found a sinking-
fund for the reduction of the public debt, he
would leave a memorable footprint on the sands
of time. Also, he would be such an entirely
new kind of benefactor that he would probably
get the biggest title going up to date, to say
nothing of the fact that he would get a sure
seat in Parliament for the district where his
railway was located. The man who gives his
money to found a Chair of Music, is lost in the
ruck ; every stereotyped plutocrat who goes in
for benevolence founds a chair of something
which provides a salary for an imported pro-
fessor, or builds an examination hall, or puts up
the money with which to buy pictures in
Europe and hang them up to moulder in a gal-
lery, or gives his loot to the church. On the
other hand, the man who made 100 miles of
good road or railway would be something
brilliantly original, and he would stand out
from the stereotyped crowd like a big gum-tree
on the Old Man Plain. What an advt. there is
waiting for the bloated plutocrat who leaves the
old, dull tracks of benevolence, and gives up
building white elephants with his coin, and
does something really useful !
A Sidelight on the Federal Railway
Problem.
Apropos of the question of Federal as against
provincial railway management, the following
extract from a recent Sydney Daily Telegraph
article is interesting reading ;
Those who oppose the transfer of the railways to
Federal control urge “ That if this be effected Sydney
will cease to be the shipping port for the territory of New
South Wales.” The reasonable inference from this state-
ment is “ That if the colonies do not federate, or if the
railways be not worked under a uniform system, Sydney
will be the shipping port for the produce of the colony.”"
Let us see whether this is a fact, and what is the pre-
sent state of things under the ideal isolation which some
desire to perpetuate.
Queensland in the north, by protective duties prevents
any inward trade, and by the Border Duties Act prohibits
outward trade by an export duty of £2 10s. per ton, and
thus forces wool which, geographically, should trend to
Sydney, on to her own railways.
Victoria has laid down various rebate lines, and within
the limits thus defined, offers great reductions on rates
for New South Wales wool, which eventually reaches her
railway system.
The first rebate area laid down within New South Wales
by the Victorian Railway Commissioners is bounded on
the north by the southern boundary of the colony of
Queensland at Goodooga ; it turns south easterly and
passes through Walgett, then southerly to Nugal, thence
westerly and southerly it follows (passing near Gongolgon
and Tarcoon) approximately the course of the River Dar-
ling, but south therefrom, till a point east from Wilcannia
is reached ; then turns south, passing west of Ivanhoe,
through Clare, and strikes the Murrumbidgee between
Oxley and Balranald ; thence it passes somewhat north
from the Murrumbidgee and the Murray to the South
Australian boundary. Over this vast area, embracing
approximately one-quarter of this colony, the Victorian
railway system offers to carry New South Wales wool at a
reduction of 46 to 61 per cent, of the charge made on
wool grown within Victoria’s own territory.
Adjoining this above-described area is another vast
territory, the boundary of which comes as far east as
Gongolgon (south from Brewarrina), and bearing south,
crosses the Lachlan between Cugong and Condobolin, and
about Wyalong, turning south-easterly, strikes the Mur-
rumbidgee at Wagga, and follows that river down to the
junction with the Lachlan. Over this area the Victorian
system of railways offers a reduction of 61 to 66 per cent.
Along the Murrumbidgee and Murray Rivers the rebate
is 38 to 43 per cent.
From Wagga Wagga towards Adelong, and embracing
Tumberumba, another rebate area is located, the southern
boundary of which, passing through Gerogery, turns
north-west near Coree, and again west to Balranald.
Within this area there is a general rebate of about 46 per
cent.
Thus on the north our trade practically ceases on the
south boundary of Queensland, and the Victorian system
of railways is offering special rebates of 50 per cent, in
freights to the inhabitants of more than half the territory
of New South Wales.
The result of these rebates, combined with the oppor-
tunities offered by the river system, is that little or no
wool reaches Sydney from an area bounded on the west
by the South Australian border, on the north by the
southern boundary of Queensland, to a point midway
between Wainpah and Hungerford, on the east by a line
crossing the Paroo below Wanaaring, and the Darling
below Tilpa, passing south-westerly west from Cohar,
south from Gilgun}'ah, and near Hillston and Gunbar, and
on the south embracing three-fourths of the Riverina
between the Murrumbidgee and the Murray. The whole
of the wool trade, and most of the other trade over this
area, is at present lost to New South Wales, and such
trade as does reach Sydney is done at “cut” rates, the
profit on the carriage being very doubtful.
The Prudent Federationist is, therefore, invited
to sit down and think the matter out on this
basis: Under provincial railway management
Victoria offers specially reduced rates—reduced
in some cases to one-third the regular figure—
to attract the trade of more than half New
South Wales to Melbourne. Victoria gets a
great deal of this trade in consequence. If the
railways are left under separate provincial
management instead of being handed over to
the Federal Government it will probably con-
tinue doing this, or it may do it on a still
larger scale, and N.S.W. can’t retaliate by
dragging Victorian trade to Sydney, the dis-
tances being too great. Under Federal manage-
ment, on the other hand, there would be a
uniform charge by distance, therefore Victoria
wouldn’t be allowed to drag the commerce of
half N.S.W. Melbourne-wards by differential
rates any longer. And, by a curiously topsy-
turvy species of logic, the N.S.W. people who
want to preserve the provincial railway system,
under which Melbourne grabs with consider-
able success for the trade of half their province,
do so on the plea that they want to prevent
their trade going to Melbourne. Their solitary
argument in favor of provincialism is just the
one argument above all others which knocks
provincialism endways in the first round.
—
Requires Shifting’..
The Queensland authorities have spent about
£1,000,000 in trying to make Brisbane a sea-
port, and the last flood silted the river up
again so badly that it became, in a few days,
almost as far from being a decent, reliable sea-
port as it was when the struggle started. It
will probably takeanothermillion tomake it any-
thing like a success, consifieringtherequirements
of modern shipping, and then the old man flood
which is bound to come again, sooner or later,
will probably undo the work once more to a
great extent. Even if the big flood didn’t
come again, which is an impossible supposition,
the current keeps depositing mud and sand at
all times, in larger or smaller quantities, and
the 20 miles or so of river must be dredged at
considerable expense for ever and ever, and the
expenditure must needs run into a great many
millions in course of time, seeing that it has all
the time there is in which to accumulate.
And the whole cash value of Brisbane—
land and buildings—is not quite £7,000,000.
The question is, therefore, whether it wouldn’t
be cheaper in the end to abandon the endless
struggle and build a new capital somewhere
near Cleveland, where there is deep, clean
water and plenty of sea-room, and no shifty,
narrow, crooked river to contend with, and no
lightering to do, and no long channel to keep
lighted and buoyed? In other words, it doesn’t
seem worth while to go in for a ceaseless ex-
penditure, which must pile up to many millions
in course of time, to keep the way open to a
town worth only £7,000,000 altogether, espe-
cially considering that all the dredging on earth
can t make it anything but a long, narrow,
crooked way at the best. Rockhampton, which
taken in all isn’t worth quite £1,500,000, and
which has 30 miles of shallow stream and ditch
and mud-fiat between itself and the sea, is an-
other bad bargain. It would take more than
Rockhampton is worth to make its river de-
cently navigable for ocean steamers, and it is
questionable whether the whole rental value of
the town would keep it so. If Brisbane and
Rockhampton would only throw themselves up
as a bad job before it gets too late, and start
afresh on a new site where commerce could get
at them, and let their hopeless rivers slide,
the ultimate saving would be something enor-
mous.

The Hon. A. J. Peacock’s Candidature.
A Few Simple Reasons Why
Mr. A. J. Peacock
Should be selected as ONE of the TEN Members fora Seat
in the Australian Federal Convention.
To The Electors op Victoria.
Creswick, February 8. 1897.
may it please you—
The Committee inaugurated to further the Candida-
ture of the Hon. A. J. Peacock, Chief-Secretary of Victoria,
in Creswick, his native place, respectfully solicits your
sympathetic and hearty co-operation in securing for him —-
as a representative Australian—a seat at the Federal Con-
vention.
The singularly pronounced success attending Mr.
Peacock’s career has been remarkable. [Likewise, his re-
markable career has been pronounced, also singular.]
He was appointed Minister of Education during his first
term of political service, and has since been continuously
elected unopposed for the Clunes and Allandale electorate.
[Gladstone and Bismarck can’t say as much.]
After nine years of public life as a Popular Pleader and
Champion of Democracy, he occupies the exalted and
responsible position of Chief-Secretary and Minister of
Education in the present Government.
This distinguished honor has been won by the tireless
energy of Mr. Peacock himself ; he has shown to the
youth of Australasia that men may rise from the dead
level of themselves by stepping-stones to the higher posi-
tions in the land. [Goethe and Tennyson reallv said in
their great play, “The Silver King”—
And men may rise by breaking stones
And help themselves to better things.
But there’s as much money in the Peacock version.] •
His indomitable perseverance, his practical grasp of
measures and men, has bracketed his name with the
ablest statesmen of Australia.
He has already given evidence of his genius and ability
by placing on the Statute Books of this colony some of
the most humane and merciful legislation ever effected by
a clear-minded, high-souled politician.
He is a man with nobility of purpose and an ambitious
desire to serve the best interests of his native land—Aus-
tralia—and he has the cause of Federation in its highest
ideals, ready to make use of when the time and hour
arrive.
Fame and success have not spoilt him ; he is a level-
headed thinker, and beloved by everyone who has the
honor of his acquaintance.
The committee [singular] takes the greatest pleasure in
thus briefly referring to Mr. Peacock, for they [plural]
have known him from his childhood.
His claimsupon the people of Victoria are unquestioned,
either as a representative of Truth and Justice [meaning,
of course, Allandale and Clunes], a successful and bril-
liant exponent of the power and usefulness of the Aus-
tralian Natives' Association [besides which Truth and Jus-
tice are a mere circumstance], or as an advance-courier in
the noble cause of Federation, a subject he has most
eloquently lectured upon throughout the whole of the
colonies.
He is one of the youngest Australians who has attained
such an eminent position amongst the governing intel-
lects in the Parliaments of his country.
The committee most cordially hope that you will use
your undoubted and invaluable influence with every
elector and well-wisher of Federation in your circle and
district and secure for Mr. Peacock a triumphant return
of one of the Ten Good Men and True, worthy Represen-
tative that should be selected from amongst the aspiring
candidates for their unquestionable integrity, ability, and
devoted adherence to the cause of Unity and Progress.
[Also, Allandale and Clunes.]
We anticipate your vote and interest in this National
Undertaking, and your assistance on behalf of Mr. Pea-
cock, for we earnestly desire to see him included amongst
those distinguished Representativ es and Statesmen upon
whom will devolve the imperishable honor of framing a
Constitution for Australia, that mighty diadem of Greater
Britain. [Also, Allandale and Clunes.]
Finally—One vote from every elector in Victoria for
Mr. A. J. Peacock, Chief Secretary of Victoria, and time
will prove that the high honor you thus eonh r upon him
will not be tarnished or sullied by one who has already
won distinction, both as an able administrator and a fear-
less champion of the rights and liberties of the Aus-
tralian people.
On behalf of the committee, believe us to be unitedly
in the cause, yours very sincerely, Wm. Caiull, J.P.',
Chairman ; W. B. Grose, M.L.A., L. D. Gibson, Vice-
Chairman; J. Gavan Reilly, Robt. Falla, J.P., P.
Mungovan, Hon. Secs.
Election Day—Thursday, March 4.
And yet Peacock was elected !
The Wesfcralian ten at the Federal Convention
will be an anomalous lot, representing nothing
in particular save themselves. Premier Forrest
worked the election out West in an artful
fashion. First, he agreed to come in with the
other provinces, it being understood that the
ten Westralian delegates should be appointed
by some form of popular election. Then he
decided that Westralia couldn’t come in—not
on the ground that he objected to popular elec-
tion, which would have roused the people to
aim bricks at his Government, but solely
because the impending dissolution of Parlia-
ment made it impossible for the local fossils to
get away. Next, he decided that Westralia
would come in after all, and that the above
excuse was all imagination; but this was
decided so late in the day that popular
election was absolutely impossible from
sheer want of time. Therefore, Parlia-
ment bad to choose the delegates itself,
and the beastly democratic element was shut
out of the contest, and the t’other-side vote
didn’t get a say. Yet it all came about quite
naturally; Forrest didn’t excite the public
wrath by telling the people in the first place
that they were unfit to be entrusted with a
matter of such moment; and having trusted the
people in the first place, he didn’t take the
right of election away from them voluntarily or
offensively. In fact, he was yearning all along
to entrust the settlement of the whole question
to the people, who would have probably taken
the chance to make things very disagreeable for
Forrest ; and then he artfully drove himself
into such an awkward corner that it was im-
possible to wait for the slow process of popular
election, despite his great desire to do so.
Consequently, Forrest reluctantly convened
his little Parliamentary gang which is
practically himself—and regretfully elected
himself and the rest of the gang; and
gets all the credit of wanting to leave the
matter to the electors, without any of the con-
sequences. Out of 54 politicians only 48 voted
—they formed the whole VVestralian Federal
constituency in themselves ; and they naturally
elected ten of themselves— Forrest, Lee
Steere, Leake, Piesse, Hackett, Loton,
James, Hassell, Sholl and Taylor. Sholl
represents 24 electors at the Gascoyne, and
Forrest and Lee Steere represent 489
electors between them, and the ten, taking
them all round, are beautifully representative
of the good old system of Government by the
Six Families.

After all the bitter wail that has been wailed
about how the other provinces want to
federate the railways so as to get hold of
N.S.W.’s profitable system, and to combine it
with their own unprofitable ones, the results of
the Federal election are a trifle surprising. Out
of the ten representatives from each province,
N.S.W. and S.A. supply three each who want
to federate the railways, while Victoria and
Tasmania—the provinces with the biggest rail-
way deficit, and consequently with the most
to gain—return only one man apiece who is
avowedly in favor of railway consolidation.
Therefore if the results mean anything it would
seem that these provinces are not hungering
after the N.S.W. railways as alleged by Heydon
and Nugent Robertson, and that they rather
suspect the cornstalk of wanting to amalgamate
with them for some sinister cornstalk purpose
of his own. And whereas the Mangy Dog
party hasalways pointed to Victoria—that being
the province with the biggest railway shortage
—as the one above all others which wanted to
unite its unpayable lines with those of the
mother province, it now appears that Victoria is
the only one of the four which has returned a
majority definitely pledged to have nothing to do
with railway federation—a majority which
wants to lay for the designing cornstalk with a
club if he is found trying to get at Victoria’s
railways under any circumstances whatever.
The public-debt problem shows an equally
upside-down result. If N.S.W. and Victoria
combine their debts, N.S.W. makes a large pro-
fit out of its neighbor while Victoria makes a
big loss. Yet N.S.W. returns only three
delegates who want to consolidate the debts
while Victoria returns five and two more who
are in favor of partial consolidation. Little
things like these upset quite a lot of calcula-
tions.
Rhodes’ acknowledgment that Chamberlain
knew nothing of the projected Transvaal coup
disposes of another of the myths of the fatuous
Stead, who tried to whitewash his African idol
at the expeiyse of an English enemy who has
characteristically snubbed him. Saturday
Review (which has consistently denounced the
Transvaal grab) amplifies Rhodes’ “ unctuous
righteousness ” plea in this fashion :
“ I took Pondoland,” Rhodes seems to say to the
British public, “and you thanked me; I seized Gazaland
and Manicaland for you, and thrust back the Portuguese,
and you cheered me ; at my own risk and with my own
money I won you Mashonaland and Matabililand, and
spread your Empire to the Shirri Highlands and made you
undisputed masters of all South Africa from Cape Town
to the Zambesi, and you hailed mo as a conqueror and
made me a Privy Councillor of our Queen. And now, for-
sooth, because I have failed to incorporate the Transvaal
with the rest, you wall plague me with questions, and try
me according to the rules of abstract right and justice.
What base hypocrisy ! One of my earliest recollections
in South Africa was the way in which Lord Kimberley
stole the Diamond Fields from the Orange Free State. If
you want to know the story, read it in Froude, and see
what he says about the morality of it. Have you
punished Lord Kimberley or given back the Diamond
Fields to their lawful owners ? You hypocrites! Who
was it but Sir Henry Loch, your High Commissioner,
who first put thoughts of revolt into the heads of the
citizens of Johannesburg; who was it but the same
gentleman who advised them to provide themselves with
arms? You have rewarded him with a peerage, and will
you punish me who have done nothing but carry out his
recommendations at my own expense ?”
Which condemns Britain without excusing
Rhodes.

“ X.” to The Bulletin re King David Syme :
The Ten Victorian candidates chosen by David Syme for
the Federal Convention have every one of them been
elected, and the Age, which has been almost frightened
at its own complete success, is jubilating cautiously over
the event. If the result is to be set down exclusively to
this cause, it is not a matter for universal cheering, for
David’s politics are by no means perfect. There is no
fun in a Referendum for us Victorian democrats, if he is
to run the show every time, for there are various little
matters, particularly a few relating to land, that we will
not find on the Age ticket for years to come. We are
almost satisfied, however, that the Age’s success is only
an adventitious one, or that, at most, it has stood in the
position of the man who makes the overweight. Without
its instructions, we could safely reckon that the first six
would have got in anyway— Turner, Quick and Deakin
upon obvious grounds ; Peacock and Trenwitii out of
pure love of democracy ; and Isaacs for his intermittent
brilliancy. Probably Graham Berry’s old fame would
have pulled him through also. Remain Fraser, Zeal
and Higgins. How far the Age helped the last man can
only be conjectured, but its share in the success
of the others is less doubtful. All the Orangemen
in Victoria would not have elected Fraser by themselves,
and Zeal’s name is little known to the mass of Victorian
voters. Even these, however, are comparatively low
down in the mining constituencies (where the Agf, would
he most likely to determine the chances of unknown men),
being practically on the same level as Murray Smith,

“ E.L.” to The Bulletin :
The financial cul de sac into which Premier Reid has
driven the N.S.W. batch of Convention members illus-
trates a personal weakness which he foolishly regards as
strength. “ 111 pay them, never fear,” he cried, when
the Leg. Council rejected that portion of the Enabling
BUI which provided for the remuneration of Convention
members. He was asked then and many times after to
state the manner of payment contemplated, but repelled
questioners with repetitions of enigmatical assurance,
ihe time to settle the question was when the difficulty
arose, and satisfactorily settled it would have been had
the Premier then taken it courageously in hand. Instead
he raced the session to a close, and Parliament broke up
with, 111 pay them, never fear,” in its ears. Now the
question insists on being answered, and the valiant
Premier discloses his strategic intention. He will pay the
members and move for an indemnity when Parliament
meets. In the first place, this proves the danger of
putting off a difficulty till a future day, for it adds insult
. .u rl,"” 01 to the other objections that body had
to the Bill in its original state. And, secondly, now that
the ten men are elected, other and delicate considerations
come into play. The ten, taken as a body, are politically
opposed to payment of members. Their own speeches
are citable against them. Six of them are members of
Assembly, and a seventh is a member of the Council. How
shall these associate themselves with an Indemnity Bill ?
The remaining three say they don’t want pay. The
country most certainly desires to pay those who work for
in this case the Premier’s procrastination and
111 pay them, never fear” have put both country and
members in a cul de sac.
The Ponting-Huddart Parker (Alert) case is
apparently just beginning a series of the fami-
liar_new-trial waltzes which to shipping cases
seem second nature. A new trial has been
granted against the company on the ground
that while the case was pending jurvmen (one
at any rate) were seen drinking and chatting
with the company’s counsel and solicitor in
Menzies bar. The court did not think that
there was any attempt to influence the jury,
but, acting on precedent, granted a new trial.
But before the order is finally made a law-point
has to be argued as to whether the Alert w'as a
“ British ship.” It will be a curious argument,
too, turning mainly on what appears to be a
piece of bad draughtsmanship in a Victorian
Act. So Ponting hasn’t got his new trial yet,
nor have either Porting or Huddart Parker
yet taken the inevitable steps to have the result
of the new trial set aside, nor to appeal to the
Privy Council against the setting aside or not
setting aside of the third or fourth new trial.
But no doubt it will all come in due time.

Ed. Bulletin, —This is going to be a special
plea in defence of the bloodthirsty Jaypee,
the respectable grocer who howls for the use of
the lash whenever he gets a chance to shriek in
that wise. Recently, at Castlemaine (Vic.), a
J-P- was giving a man 14 days for obscene lan-
guage, and (as usual in similar cases) “ex-
pressed his regret ” that the law did not allow
him to order a flogging as well. The language
was merely ordinary bullockese—language can
only grow worse up to a certain point ; there-
after it progresses in the direction of variety—
and the man was inordinately drunk ; but
Bumble would still have liked to give him the
cat. Now, this particular Bumble is a very
harmless man, and on ordinary occasions shows
no special predisposition to gore and red murder;
and the writer believes that it would be im-
possible for anyone to take him seriously in this
matter. Moreover, he believes that nine-tenths
of the J.P. breed are much the same, and that
when they talk lash they never mean it. It is
a good British bluff, that’s all. If they had the
power, they would be absolutely afraid to ex-
ercise it; and in a vote by ballot they would
refuse it almost unanimously. Posing is a
universal art, and it is the Britisher’s misfor-
tune that he would rather brag of brutality
than of anything else. I beg to move that the
Castlemaine J.P. be hereby acquitted. —Poseur.
“ Simply a Citizen ” writes to the Sydney
dailies, explaining why Premier Reid should go
’Ome :
His absence from the forthcoming celebrations would
be a matter for deep regret at any time, but more espe-
cially at this juncture, in view of what is now taking
place in South-eastern Europe. The Turkish Empire is
now rapidly breaking up; its ultimate fate cannot much
longer be delayed. What is now holding it together
is the question, What is to become-of the fragments
And in this question Australia is deeply interested'
seeing that our trade-routes pass along" its coasts;
the route of the future down the Euphrates Valley even
passes through the heart of the Empire. This vast tract
of country, rich in potential wealth, anil once densely
peopled, should be included within the wide extended
British Empire, if only for the benefit of its present
miserable inhabitants. It cannot be allowed to go to any
other nation. Mr. Chamberlain has asked for the pre-
sence home of the Australian Premiers to discuss un-
named subjects. May this be one of them? Perhaps so.
\es, Georgey Porgy must go ’Ome to settle up
the lurkish Empire and see that the Euphrates
Valley is grabbed by the only true Christian,
J. Bull, “for the sake of its ” —here drop a
silent tear—“ miserable inhabitants.” But
why, in the name of all that is sane, do the
Sydney dailies inflict on their miserable readers
such hypocritical muck ?


A sceptical doctor recently called at the
Edison Electric Parlor, 162 Pitt-street, Sydney,
to test the effectiveness of the X rays. He placed
his hand upon the fluorescent screen, and straight-
way savv several shot pellets which had been
lodged in his hands in boyhood. The latest
novelties for the Vitascope, sensational and
humorous, provide, more entertainment than all
the numerous imitators put together. Other
attractions are the Edison Kinetoseope and
Kinetophone.*
