Frank Thornton as “ The Bookmaker,” in
which character he transpired at Sydney Criterion
on Saturday, is rather a new departure. Barring
the vagaries of one leading red-headed character
in huge checks, the new comedy isn’t at all comic
apparently it isn’t meant to be. It is a pathetic
love-story in which the good daughter of the good
but embarrassed peer can’t marry the good young
man of her choice because neither of them has any
money. Also the good son of the good old peer
can t marry the girl of his choice, partly because
they have no money either, and partly because lie
has already married a red-headed adventuress of
the worst description. Further, the wicked
marquis, who is rich and stony and loathsome,
insists on the daughter marrying him, failing
which he will foreclose the mortgage and shoot
everybody into the street, and the paternal earl
will have to end his days shoving a baked potato
barrow for a livelihood. So they are all beau-
tifully and conventionally miserable together
an the ancestral home, and groan just like the
family in the London Journal, till the book-
maker—who is a distant relative and has just
come into a baronetcy and a huge pile of money—
inserts his flaming head and his awful suit of
clothes into the scene. The bookmaker strews his
mangled “ h ” all over the marble floors, and puts
his knife down his throat at dinner, and talks at
the top of his voice—mostly about ’oss —to make
himself heard above his clothes. But his feelin’
art is in the right place, and he slops money
about like water till he relieves the family of its
troubles. He even relieves the son from the in-
cubus of his barmaid wife by proving himself to be
that explosive female’s real, original husband;
and everybody is left in a state of dazzling happi-
ness except the marquis, who is ignominiously
sacked without a character. There is no visible
reason why the bookmaker should do all this ex-
cept his loud check suit and his feelin’ ’art; he
is one of these strange, conventional persons
who burst into the old-fashioned drama, and
rain cheques on all the good people who are in
trouble, and then go away as unaccountably as
they came. And the good people in the old-
fashioned drama, not being able to earn 12s. a
week and their beer, obviously require to have the
benevolent stranger rush in and pour cheques on
them ; else the last act would always see them
with their hair sticking up through their broken
hats, selling pigs’-trotters at a street corner ; and
then people would presei tly boot the drama into
oblivion.
*** « _
The “ Bookmaker ” is a nice, satisfactory
play in its own fashion, and the characters are
inuch brighter and more original than the
play. Thornton’s bookie is the British ’Arry in a
violent state of development, and is a quaint mix-
ture ofcomedy, embarrassment, pathos and horse.
He isn’t so comic as Thornton often has been, but
he is probably quite as comic as any one burdened
with so much pathos and horse could be. Miss
Bessie Rignold is a bright and fresh and clever
patch of loveliness against the background of the
impecunious peer and the mortgaged mansion ;
and Seabrooke is another pleasant circum-
stance. Scot Inglis is much too large a man for
his small part, and Herbert Terry now and then
wails at unsuitable moments in the voice of the
German music-master in “Miss Brown.’’ The
loathsome marquis and Miss Carew are reasonably
successful without achieving anything calculated
to make the universe sit up suddenly, and the
scenery consists of one room in the Earl’s castle.
Said castle is seemingly mortgaged for .£ 100,000 at
4 per cent., judging from the joy which arises
when an unknown benefactor sends along JISOOO
to pay the half-year’s interest. It seems to be
quite a habit with unknown benefactors to drop
large sums of iriyney into this family.
** * *
A of the “ Shamrock and the
Rose at Sydney Royal, by “arrangement” with
Flam Billolloway, is doing moderate business
in the presence of a small amount of enthusiasm
about two feet high. Reynolds’ old drama has
been revived a good many times already, and
almost everything, including the earth, happens by
arrangement with Billolloway. There is a general
impression abroad that the Fall, as described in
Genesis, happened by arrangement with that
general arranger of things, and that he gave the
serpent a leg up over the fence on commission.
fjo Shamrock and the other adjacent vege-
table is a respectable Irish drama of the usual
kind. It suggests that 90 per cent, of the Irish
nation lives by serving writs on and chucking out
the other 10 per cent, for non-payment of the rent,
and that the Irish landlord gets his rents in solely
b y ejecting his tenants and leaving the property
vacant. The only other visible occupation of the
community, when it isn’t serving writs on some-
body, seems to be leading the colleen astray with
a bogus marriage ; and the most notable feature of
the good Irishman is his tendency to get married
when his sole assets are a warm heart and a bright-
red waistcoat. Irish drama is the great shiftless
drama of the earth—the drama in which nobody
seems to have anything, and everybody seems to
live on nothing, and where the heroine is in a
constant state of carrying gratis potatoes to
several thousand ejected widows, and everybody
is understood to be more or less an old woman
dying on the highways, except the process-server
and the informer. Misses Harrie Ireland and
F itzmauriceGill brighten up the present production
a good deal, and Sweeney and Pliturner give it a
lot of valuable assistance, and maul the process-
server in between times; and the colleen is led
astray with much ability by Inman, and the rest
of the 00. waves a comprehensive brogue and shil-
lelagh in the background. Miss Gill, the lady
who has been led astray, weeps through 13 very
beautiful scenes by Brunton, Howden and Whaite,
and when she has passed by, leaving a trail of woe
behind her, the process-server generally humps his
ill-gotten gains across the same landscape, and the
informer is somewhere around.
** * *
“ Mafcsa ” fills the biggest theatre in
Sydney to the door, or thereabouts, eight times a
week, and its Egyptian glory shows no sign of
fading. If the Firm proposes to go one better
next year the only way it can be done, so far as
The Bulletin can see, is to put some powerful in-
tellect into the plot and dialogue; in the matter
of pure spectacle the Rising of the Nile and the
vision of Memphis as it used to be in the days of
Williamson’s great - great - very-great- grand father
won’t be excelled without a strain which will
crack something badly. A change of programme
at Her Majesty’s is expected at some date so re-
mote as not to be worth talking about at present.
** * *
Bracy’s good company and finely-mounted
opera only attract a thin scattering of people to
Sydney Lyceum. Possibly the trouble arises from
the want of more well-known names on the bill,
but Braey’s lady artists are, in the main, about as
clever and as nice-looking a crowd as could be got
together just now in Australia, and Bracy himself
preserves his eternal youth and sings as well as he
did many years ago. Miss Celia Mavis now takes
turns with Miss Darley in the leading character,
and though the latter is probably the more win-
some charmer of the two, there is much to be said
for her rival. There is also a great deal to be said,
in a pleasant tone of voice, for Miss Julia Spencer
as Stephania in a short frock ; and Albert Whelan
is a weirdly comic old servitor, and though the
substitution of Knight Aston for John Forde is
hardly a change for the better, inasmuch as the
new man sings a little better than the old one and
acts a good deal worse, still his youthfulness, like
Bracy’s, is an interesting study. Altogether,
Bracy’s show is many degrees better than some
that have prospered greatly in Sydney, and its
ill luck is unaccountable. Possibly the ghost of
the man whom Scot Inglis was supposed to be
when M‘Carthy dropped the property boulder on
his head still haunts the Lyceum and brings
disaster in its train. “ Nemesis ”is announced
for Saturday, though by present appearance the
Lyceum is already choke-full of nemesis—the
worst kind.
** * *
The early departure of Chirgwin from
Sydney Palace and of the Vaidis sisters from the
Tivoli, and the reappearance of Godfrey at the
latter house on Saturday, are the principal changes
in Rickards’ arrangements at present. Nobody
very new is expected just now at either theatre,
but Rickards proposes to rush them both with a
mass of fresh talent at an early date.
** * *
James MacMahon has taken another 12
months’ lease of his present premises, and reckons
on keeping the cinematographe a fixture till well
on in 1898. With a view to this, he has laid in
some new pictures, with even more legs than usual
in them, that astute showman having realised
that, though other things may come and go, the
demand for legs has no end, and the female
extremity, with the stocking on it, goeth on for
ever.
** * *
“ Two Little Vagabonds,” which put in a
first Australian appearance at Melbourne Prin-
cess’s on Saturday, is an audacious medley of
pathos, bathos, and pantomimic sensationalism.
It has affecting ” scenes, strong enough, of
their kind, to draw tears from a lawyer, intro-
duced into a story that compels critical derision.
It makes direct appeals to the public heart with-
out seriously attempting to impose upon the
common-sense, and yet, no doubt, it is capable of
attracting a second visit from the very people who
laugh at their own sympathy with such obvious
claptrap. “Two Little Vagabonds,’’ adapted
from the French by G. R. Sims and another,
merely claims to be a good business drama,
constructed On sound commercial princi-
ples. The first of five acts serves as a pro-
logue, and is very French in character. By
weight of ingenious circumstances, a reason-
ably - jealous husband is forced to suppose
that he wasn’t the father of his wife’s child. After
a fine, stormy interview with the lady (who has
been rushed into shielding a sister’s giddiness), he
hustles her from the drawing-room and turns out
the gas. Whereupon an absurd thief, called
Gaffer, comes along to burgle valuables in the
moonlight, and, being captured by the husband, is
allowed to go free on the condition that he shall
kidnap the child aforesaid. Then the mother is
fetched back upon the stage to learn that her
lawful infant lias been doomed to grow up among
the criminal classes, at the mercy of an unknown
person who dropped in casually. She falls down,
followed by a slow curtain.
* * * *
The rest of this wild narrative, dated
seven years later, is built around the ragged per-
S 0I jL- tles W 0 h°y s —the kidnapped youngster
and his consumptive mate. They are educated to
steal by Gaffer, but they cultivate virtue and
brotherly love when the boss is out of sight,
t 6 a e^m 0 the centre-pieces throughout a series
of effective situations created from miraculous
meetings and impossible arrangements in general.
Ihe bereaved mother, the peculiar father, and the
giddy sister are all brought together by accident
at a spot where Gaffer happens to be camping, and
the long-lost son is purchased on spec, from that
extraordinary felon for £3OO. But the right bov
runs away to London before the transaction is
completed, consequently Gaffer rings in the
wrong boy, who amounts to the same thing as the
genuine article, except that he is rapidly suc-
cumbing to a bad corf. Next act finds the
right boy walking casually into a gorgeous
mansion where the wrong boy is surrounded
by luxuries and medical attendance. The • two
mates embrace one another, just about the
time when the right boy’s mother has discovered,
by accident, that she bought the wrong son.
Shortly afterwards the right boy produces evi-
dence of his identity (to slow music) whioh so re-
joices the lady that she straightway retires to bed,
leaving her real offspring to roost in his rags along
with the spurious son who is dying of phthisis. For
seven long years she has sought her lost darling,
and now when he turns up unexpectedly at his
dear old home she doesn’t even ask him to take a
bath. Her eccentricity is essential to the plot.
The right boy slinks away to steal some “ papers ”
(which are expected to explain all) from Gaffer’s
den, and gets there in- time to rescue his father,
who had also come for. the same papers, and been
chloroformed a short time previously. Father
and son break through the roof and escape vid a
comic water-spout, and get back home in time to
seize Gaffer’s gang in the act of choking the con-
sumptive boy, who expires prematurely from the
shock. Here endeth the drama.
* * * •
Miss Marie Neilson plays the lively boy,
while Miss Florence Terriss undertakes the boy
with the bad corf. Neither actress has the quali-
fications of a Jennie Lee for Jo-like character-
study ; and the first-named never once gets away
from her sex. In the quiet passages, Miss Neilson
is a bright little woman trying ;o talk the language
of a vulgar boy ; when she ge o excited, she speaks
as a cultured lady, with a riof, sweet voice. Her
performance, of course, will glow more convincing
when she gets used to it. Miss Terriss is already
a passable imitation of a boy i.e. , a sick,
delicate youth and she also may be expected
to improve on , her first attempt before a
strange audience. • Both actresses are clever
artists, but they don’t strike one as being
very experienced impersonators of vagabond
boys. Miss Ada Ferrar and Julius Knight are
admirable as the wife and husband. Miss Elliott
Page is a successful erring sister, and Gaston
Mervale fails considerably as the impressible
Gaffer. Several small parts get well treated by
Miss Nellie Mortyne and others. Also a few
lines come very nicely and naturally from the
mouth of Mrs. Maesmore Morris, who looks sweet
in the garb of a hospital nurse. A matinee on
Saturday.
** * *
The Brough farewell season at Melb. Bijou
has disposed of “Niobe” and is now submitting
“ A Woman of No Importance ” to final notice.
O. Wilde’s epigrams and moral peculiarities have
been much mentioned lately in polite circles. The
present revival is by special desire of those people
who felt sorry for having missed the chance to
sample O. Wilde’s play when it first arrived from
Sydney, and whose yearning for epigrams which
soar far beyond their intellectual grasp is, at least,
an amiable weakness. The Bijou should be
pretty well crowded-these last threenights of Oscar’s
firework display. On Saturday (20th) “ A Village
Priest” will wrestle with his vows once again,
and continue for five nights thereafter to draw
pious distinctions between a criminal in the eyes
of the Law, and a criminal in the sight of Heaven,
when the local authorities call upon the holy man
for a plain, straightforward statement of worldly
truth. The Bulletin has always held on to the
theory that Father Tit'neradge, in his stirring
drama, is a first-class liar.
** * *
“ The Derby Winner ” flourishes, as usual,
at the Royal, and the tawny-haired adventuress
goes on disturbing domestic happiness every even-
ing till further notice. The end of Bland Holt’s
prodigious entertainment is not yet, but it will
probably happen some time or other before Easter.
** * *
Also the Opera House bill is much the
same as before, with the exception of a new and
warlike ballet which arrived on Saturday. The
wonderful horizontal barmen will remain at their
posts another week, but Charles Godfrey and the
Allisons are going away for an uncertain period
after this (Thursday) evening.

Wilfred Shine, who came to these parts
with the “ Morocco Bound ” company, and has
enjoyed more than his fair share of adversity in
the meantime, takes a matinee benefit at Mel-
bourne Bijou to-day (Thursday). Shine’s imme-
diate object is to reach dear old Hingland in time
to wave his handkerchief at our revered Sovereign
and the patronage of Lord Brassey is promised
towards the attainment of that end.

That voluminous composition, Brigade Bandmasf
Hutohinson’s “Federation,” has been put in rehearsal*
and a large number of vocalists and instrumentalists hav

S.S. : “Is there any relationship between Ferris
Brough’s latest young man, and clever Miss Faber whn
was recently with the co. ? The resemblance is remark
able. Ferris has the identical mannerisms so conspicuous
in Beryl Faber, and so often commented on; he has a
weird trick of opening his mouth sometimes ahead of his
dialogue, and leaving it open, a veritable trap for winded
insects. He has, too, the Faber trick of chewing his
tongue, and jerking up his gothic eyebrows. Watching
him, one feels like David Copperfield’s aunt in the pre
sence of Uriah Heep, and wants to yell out: 1 For God’s
sake, man, don’t be galvanic ! ’ ”
No persuasion could bring basso De Alba to sing for his
brother Spaniard co.’s Melb. benefit last week. De Alba
as a very young man, left the stage in the prime of his
magnificent powers, on marrying his wealthy wife, with a
promise never to appear again.
Miss Marston Leigh died in America a few weeks ago
Well-known in Melb. and Sydney. Daughter of Heraud
London Athenaeum critic, and sister of Edith Heraud'
aotress. Married first a son of Mrs. Warner, tragedienne'
He died. Then she came to Australia, and married James
Carden. She stage-named herself after Henry Marston
and Leigh Murray, her two favorite actors. YVhen very
young her Ophelia evoked great applause in London. 3

“ Louis Bradfield, now moving up, gets a fair amount
of ‘ fat ’ in the ‘ Geisha,’ ” writes The Bulletin’s London
correspondent, “ and if Roberts would but demise so as
to be a mere memory to play-goers, his faithfully-imitative
under-study would double his present salary. How the
statement ever birthed itself in Australia that the artifi-
cially-thatched comedian had only seen Roberts play once
may be explained when Gillies grows a moustache. Here
he acknowledges that he is a direct copy, and thus ex-
cused himself for going very flat before a swell audience
at the Grafton Gallery. ‘ It’s no use ; they’ve seen all my
business before. They’ve seen Roberts.’ Such candour
for a comedian is refreshing.”
Tasmania is still a very small place. For instance (says
a Bulletin correspondent) the proprietors of a Hobart
daily are the lessees of the only theatre in the town and
sometimes import a show as a business spec, and to scare
the spiders out of the building. On these occasions repre-
sentatives of the “other” weekly paper are regarded as
interlopers and don’t figure on the deadhead-list.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
According to cabled information the English
creditors are still holding meetings and calling the
Queensland National Bank a disgraceful swindle and the
Queensland Government a discreditable accomplice in the
bank’s doings. So far, the reconstruction scheme seems
to have made little real headway ; the whole time of the
English depositors is apparently taken up by calling the
bank various more or less appropriate names. One thing
which doesn’t help the reconstruction along very much is
the fact that the Q. Government has reluctantly, and
under great pressure, decided to start its committee of
enquiry on further investigations into the bank’s condi-
tion. This demand for further investigation clearly inti-
mates that the local people believe there are more
horrors yet to be revealed, and more scandalous mis-
management, and that even the enormous shortage
already brought to light isn’t necessarily the whole short-
age—perhaps nothing even distantly resembling it. And
depositors naturally want to know what there really is
left to reconstruct before they start reconstructing it—
whatever it may be.
Supposing the Queensland National does recon-
struct, the State’s prior claim is an interesting item. The
State has at present a first lien on the old assets for its
old deposits. It is proposed that in the case of liquida-
tion all new business acquired since the ’93 reconstruction
will have precedence—this concession is necessary to get
any new business at all. But presumably the Govern-
ment’s new claims will have precedence of those of
private depositors, therefore, if the bank’s proposals are
accepted and the Q.N.B. goes on once more, things may
pan out something like this : New deposits will come in
to a greater or less extent from the public and the
Government. The old deposits of the Government are to
be paid off before the old private depositor gets a show.
The new deposits of the public may be used to pay off
the old deposits of the Government. Supposing the
Q.N.8., after it has finished paying off the Government’s
old deposits, comes to a dead stop, then it seems that the
first claim on its assets will be the new Government
deposits, the second claim will be the new private
deposits, the third will be the old private deposits. If the
bank breaks down before it has paid off the Govt.’s old
deposit things will be quite as bad; the preference
which it is proposed to give to the new creditors is only
a preference over the old private creditors—not the old
Government creditor. Therefore if the public supplies
new deposits, being incited thereto by the apparent
security afforded by the preference to be given to new
depositors in the event of a winding-up, it is just possible
that there are not enough genuine assets to pay even the
Government’s claim, and that the new deposits will be
seized upon ultimately by a liquidator to pay the Govern-
ment with. The preference to be given to new depositors
doesn’t amount to much if it is only a preference over the
old small depositors—not over the biggest one also. In
other words, supposing the Q.N.B. owes the Government
£4,000,000, and the assets will only sell for £3,800,000
and until the investigation is quite finished no one is
really certain what they will sell for —then if the Q.N.B.
reconstructs and takes £200,000 of preferential deposits
from the public, these may be swallowed up any day to
make good the above shortage. So long as the Govern-
ment always comes in first, the proposed preferential
claim doesn’t amount to so very much after all. If the
Q.N.B. winds up now the Government will probably take
everything. If it goes on for a while the Government
may take everything a few years hence instead of taking
it now, and there may be mote to grab a few years hence
than there is at present.
* * * *
The only arrangement which promises to give
the Q.N.B. a decent chance is that the Government
should promise to deposit no more money in it, and
should take its blighting patronage somewhere else. The
Government has precedence of everybody, and so long as
it keeps putting in money it will have precedence. The
Govt, account has caused most of the loss to private
depositors. The bank had about four million of private
and nearly as many of State deposits ; if it has fooled
away half of the money that means that the private de-
positors lose everything ; if there were no State deposits
and the bank still fooled away half the money the private
depositors would only lose 10s. in the £. The State gets
the same interest as private depositors, and under its
prior lien the private depositors have to insure it against
loss. They get nothing whatever for insuring it. The
State can put any amount of money it likes in the hank,
therefore the extent of the risk undertaken by the private
depositor is simply unlimited. If the latter fully under-
stands the situation, even_the proposal to give priority—
which isn’t really any priority worth mentioning—to new
deposits won’t induce them to deposit a copper with the
reconstructed Q.N.B.
* • *
In England they describe the late Westralian
loan as a failure and an utter collapse. The loan was not
directly a Government one—the VV.A. Treasury gave
£1,100,000 in 3 per cent, stock as the purchase-money for
the Great Southern Railway, and the sellers put the stock
on the market so disastrously that only £233,000 was
tendered for at 98, and the balance had to be sold pri-
vately at a big discount. The Forrest Government now
tries to believe that if the stock had been offered by
■ Government direct the result would have been different,
which—seeing that the stock and the security and the
interest and the state of the market would have been the
same—is difficult of belief. The more probable explana-
tion is that the boom is off, temporarily or permanently,
and that the flotation of 3 per cent, loans at par isn’t
going to happen again for a long time to corne. There
was never anything in the circumstances of the province
to account for that sensational transaction which sent
Westralia off its head last year, and J. Bull is probably
almost as sorry for it now as for many other W.A. specu-
lations.
• * * *
Old James Munro’s Real Estate Bank still
liquidates. It has now been five years at it. At the end
of the fourth year of liquidation it still owed £593,000,
and during the last twelve months all the money received
was £9423. At this pace the Real Estate Bank should
finally wind up in a geological epoch or two. In the
fourth year of liquidation the amount realised by sales of
property was £ll2this year it seems to be about £5OO.
One property which the Munro institution paid £l7O per
acre for, in the davs when it gibbered about millions and
wore straws in its hair, was put up at auction lately, but
the best offer was £6 an acre. The bank has made
£500,000 worth of Calls, but only managed to collect
£153,000, and by this time the shareholders have appa-
rently been sucked dry, for payments from this source
have' almost ceased. A British institution to which the
uncalled capital was pawned is harrying the ruins of the
old bank with a claim for £112,000, and there are other
woes besides.
* * * •
One bank is reported to have an artful device
for paying the Queensland dividend-tax at the expense of
its customers. When the tax was imposed, it is alleged
that it surreptitiously added 2s. 6d. per cent, to the
interest on all advances under £5OOO, and Is. 3d. per cent,
on larger advances. That is to say, if a customer gets an
advance of £4OOO, and agrees to pay £7 per cent, interest,
he is really charged £7 2s. 6d. It is so difficult to check
the interest charged on a constantly fluctuating balance,
that not one customer in 500 ever drops to the device.
Whether any other banks follow the same artful little
plan isn’t known, but if every overdrafter proceeds to
carefully check the interest charged against him, he may
possibly discover something interesting.
* * * •
The balance-sheet of the New York Life Insur-
ance Co. shows new premiums for 1896, c 1,237,913 ; re-
newal premiums, £5,169,098; interest and rents
£1,646,395, making the total income for the year,
£8,053,406. Payments to policy-holders (death losses,
endowments, annuities, cash bonuses and surrenders)
absorb £3,803,214. Expenses amount to £1,628,669,
which is equal to over 20 per cent, on total income, or
over 25 per cent, on premium income—a remarkably steep
rate for an institution of the New York Life’s age and
dimensions. The accumulated funds were increased
during the year by the very large sum of £2,621,523, and
now amount to over 38) millions, while the surplus of
assets over liabilities, as certified by the New York State
auditor, is £5,490,000, half-a-million more than it was last
year—a very solid surplus which should be large enough
to cover any possible demands upon it, no matter how
big a financial crisis mav come along.

The pestilential prevalence of alleged Friendly
Society lotteries in Vic. led The Bulletin last week to
mix up two shows that don’t play in one another’s back-
yards. The Druids are advertising a £lOOO Golden Druid,
accompanied by statistics which prove that it is worth
£9lO in the melting-pot. The difference is due to the
encouragement of imagination in the minds of the young.
The Druid lottery has so far dodged the police, and the
“ drawing ” of the “Art ” union is announced to come off
later bn. It is noticed that the statement originally
made that the Royal Mint would certify to the Weight
and quality of the “ Golden Druid” has now been silently
withdrawn from the daily press, but is still visible brt the
posters at suburban agencies.
It was the A.N.A. gold vase, value £lOOO, which
the young man from Whorouly won. It is whispered
that the finances of the A.N.A. art-union are very dis-
appointing. People seem to be getting tired of paying
shillings for a microscopic, over-priced chance of a lump
of gold. The A.N.A. people are taking the extraordinary
course of advertising for the people who paid cash at the
gates.
* • • •
About the best share-list up to date is a new
one issued by Edwin Elliott & Co., sharebrokers, of
377 Collins-street, Melbourne. It is full of informa-
tion, and in the matter of detail it has no rival. The
issue for March contains an account of the state of the
market for all manner of securities, the prospects of
various institutions, loans floated and in course of flota
tion, &c. The share-list proper includes all Victorian
municipal debentures, Government stocks, deposits of
all kinds, all the usual mass of miscellaneous undertak-
ings, and 23 closely-printed pages of mining companies in
Victoria, N.S.W., S.A.. Tasmania and W.A., with a state-
ment against each of nominal capital, paid-up ditto, date
and amount of last dividend, date and amount of last
call, and latest share quotations. There are over 800
mines quoted altogether, and when one looks down the
long regular list of calls, and notes the huge gaps in the
dividend columns, the pathos of mining becomes very
real. And while the last call is generally very recent the
last dividend is often a long way back, for the gaps—and
there are pages of them—only refer to mines that have
never paid any dividend at all. The champion grief of
the' collection seems to be the G. G. Consolidated ; last
call, December, ’96 ; last dividend, August, 1872. Twenty-
four years of unrewarded grind stand between these two
figures.
* * * *
The effect of the increased reduction of sulphide
ore at the B.H. Proprietary works is shown in the
gradually increasing aggregate cost per ton of ore, with a
corresponding fall in the profit per ton. When the
“ economies ” were introduced after the big strike in ’92
the cost per ton, amounting to £3 14s. Id. in the half-year
immediately preceding the contest, fell gradually to £2
os. lid. in the first half of ’94. At present the figure is
just over £2 10s. In the same lapse of time profit per ton
has come back from £2 17s. lOd. to 14s. Bd., and aggregate
divs. from £576,000 to £144,000.. In the absence of any
considerable plant for dealing with sulphides there has
not, however, been any increase in the quantity of ore
treated; on the contrary, there has been a consistent
falling-off from 325,949 tons in the latter half of ’94 to
208,303 tons in the corresponding six months of ’96.
Barring the half-year in which the big strike of
’92 took place, the aggregate of B.H. Proprietary divs.,
£144,000, for the last six months was.smaller than for any
similar period since ’B7. The figures in closest neighbor-
hood are those for the first half of 1888 (£152,000). And
it is interesting to compare some details for the two
terms, and to reflect whether, after all, N.S.W. (which
never did own many shares) is not better off under
present conditions. Here are a few of the figures side by
side-
-1888, J-yr. 1896, J-yr.
Dividends £152,000 £144,000
Mine wages, salaries and contracts 59,732 136,915
Smelters’ wages, &c. ... ... 23,032 54,033
Coke 41,773 75,203
Freight and Charges ... ... 34,390 49,956
Mine Timber 22,375 22,260
When it is added that in the half of ’BB the quantity of
ore treated was 39,789 tons, and in the half of ’96 208,303
tons, of a much more refractory character, shareholders
and others will be able to make for themselves some
interesting comparisons.
Of all the processes which have been tried at
the Broken Hill Proprietary, the only one that has more
than held its own, and continues to do so, is leaching.
The quantity of ore smelted has dropped from 534,254
tons in 1894 to 329,929 tons in 1896, the decline being
persistent in each half-year. Concentration has hitherto
been a costly experiment and generally a ghastly failure ;
and the amalgamation plant, erected at a cost of over
£28,000, treated altogether less than 76,000 tons of ore,
and then was snuffed out for ever. The leaching-plant,
however, set to work on ore in the beginning of ’94, has
in six half-years mounted by steady and regular stages
from 10,514 tons the first half to 39,460 tons during the
co.’s last half-year.
The B.H. Prop.’s present margin of profit (14s.
Bd. per ton of ore) is much the lowest in its record.
Assuming that no further reductions can be made in pro-
duction-charges (though since the opening of the year
very substantial reductions in freight have, as a matter
of fact, been made), how much would the market values
of silver and lead require to fall before all profit would
cease? For everything depends upon these markets.
The average price received for the silver last half-year was
2s. 9d. per oz. ; for the lead, £lO 17s. lOd. per ton. The
gross product of silver was 4,028,2920 z., and of lead
10,064 tons. The lowest price ever realised for lead was
£1 10s. per ton less than that quoted. At that rate, the
profit would have been £15,096 less. The lowest
silver ever realised on a half-year’s average was 2s. 7jd,
At that rate, a further £29,372 less would have been
netted. Together, £44,468 less. In other words, some-
what under a Is. div. The total expenses, not including
depreciation, last half-year were £490,308. If the aver-
age realised for the silver fell to 2s. per oz., and for
the lead to £9 per ton, on last half-year’s figures
a profit of £3OOO odd would still remain. And as
Broken Hill silver is about 996 fine as against 920
standard, the market quotation for standard silver would
have to fall to about Is. IOJd. per oz. before Broken Hill
silver would command no more than 2s. That, then,
may be taken as Broken Hill’s present pay limit, with the
operation of ores of the same class as are now accepted—
lead at £9 and silver at Is. 10id.
B.H. South, which has declared its second
div., will not (on the balance-sheet put forward to the
shareholders the other day) be able to keep up these
monthly payments. Entering the July-Dee. half-year
with a credit balance of £8394, it wound up with a balance
of £18,885. This gives a profit of under £lO,OOO for the
six months; and each sixpenny div. absorbs i5OOO. No
doubt the exploratory work has been, somewhat heavy,
and £2254 has been spent upon additional plant; but
then, development work must be continuous —and so it
seems must additions and alterations of concentrating
plants. Making the most ample allowances, it seems
pretty certain that, even with the added crushing power
and buddies, no more than three sixpenny divs. can be
earned in a half-year.
The most satisfactory feature about the B.H.
South report is the increase in the quantity of ore put
through the mill and the improved extraction. “ The re-
covery of lead is 83T per cent (or 4 per cent, in excess of
last year), and of silver 49'5 per cent (or 2‘9 per cent, in
excess of last year).” But those figures alone mean
nothing, or very little. It is easy to recover 99 per cent,
of the lead and 90 per cent, of the silver —if you are con-
tent to leave 95 per cent, of the zinc in. Still, there has
been an improvement, thanks to the additional plant;
but how long it will last is uncertain, in view of the fact
that many of the present faces show exceedingly hard ore.
B.H. North does seem to mean real mining busi-
ness. It is doing more prospecting than at any previous
time—so far with indifferent reward ; for where in new
localities ore is got it is exceedingly hard. European ad-
vices to Broken Hill people say that silver-market pro-
spects are no brighter. The cable quotes 2s. 5%d. ; and
now Japan (which hasn’t been a big silver-buyer lately,
though) is further Europeanising herself by formally
adopting a gold-currency. As against that, however,
Proprietary shares steadily drain from Australia, mostly
towards Scotland.
» * * •
A little more activity is showing at the Thacka-
ringa portion of the Barrier district. The Lily has a
much-advertised strike of rich ore. But Thackaringa is
essentially a lead-ore country, and all finds of rich ore
during the past 12 years have been very small patches.
Apparently a float or two are in view.
The British is showing the drain on the car-
bonates very plainly.
The North Central : “A change for the better
is coming into the shaft.” It grows wearisome. But
when they really do get a patch of vein matter, those
shares will bound. And such a patch is quite likely
whether the lode lies that way or not. Isolated streaks
and pockets of ore have occasionally been found right
away from the lode, both at B.H. and off the Comstock.
A small ore-dressing plant for the Silver Hill
Junction is about ready for erection, but divs. are in the
far distance yet. And the increased plant at the North
will be jigging regularly before the end of the month.
• • * •
Mine workmen, crowded out in the West, are
flocking to Broken Hill. But the market there is terribly
crowded, too ; and, in consequence, contracts are being
taken at wretchedly low rates.
• * • *
A peculiar feature of the Broken Hill lode is
the frequent occurrence of large cavities (technically
vughs), lined in most cases with very rich ores or ren-
dered picturesque by stalactites of lime, lead, iron, or
zinc. One such has just been broken into in the Junc-
tion North : a fair-sized house could be built in it.
* * * *
Junction North lode still takes very careful
handling and expensive staying. It is friable, heavy, and
treacherous.

White Cliffs miners.

At Muttama, N.S.W., writes a correspondent,
Beasley and party are raising some very rich stone from
their new find. Ten tons taken from the surface some
time back, yielded 4oz. 13dwts. per ton, but recently they
have struck a reef 2ft. wide, which, so far, looks more
like lOoz.

* * * »
Wonderfully rich gold in arsenical pyrites and
ealcite has been struck in the crosscut from the D’Arcy
Wentworth shaft, establishing beyond doubt that the
Lucknow contact-joint, from which tons of gold have
been taken, has gone into that property much above
1090 ft. Verily, a nice state of things for the Earl Kilmorey
to reflect over. He is chairman of the Wentworth mines,
and has come out to see them. This find puts a feather
in the judgment cap of W. A. Irwin, now managing the
Associated mines at Kalgoorlie. But, at the same time, it
is a bit of a staggerer for the parent co. which has over
1000 acres mostly where it doesn’t want them. Another
strip of 10 acres of the Wentworth estate would have
carried the contact down another 1000 ft., and there
would have been no D’Arcy mine. What a sensational
play might be written of these mines, especially if, in the
c aste, could be included Carrie-Swain-Gardiner 1
* * * *
Gundagai, N.S.W. —“ A mining-expert says it
is probable that a large amount of English capital will
soon be attracted to Gundagai.” From what the writer
knows, Bull will probably put more money into Gundagai
if two things happen—if the Gibraltar mine at Adelong
can keep up its present monthly yield, which is problem-
atical, and the Prince of Wales mine, nearer Gundagai,
gets going successfully with its 30-head mill on a 20ft.
reef of allegedly' nearlv-ounce rock.

The public are getting tired of reading of the
large quantities of machinery arriving on the Hill End
mine, W.A., and that it is looking as well as when it
crushed upwards of 20oz. to the ton with a little three-
head battery. Looking over the big dump of stone at
grass on this mine recently, one manager asked another
how much it would go, and he answered “Well, it is
supposed to go seven ounces, but there is very little
danger of being struck by lightning in this country.”

Westralia — a London financial and mining
paper, published with a very obvious object—regards The
Bulletin’s endorsement of the great and permanent rich-
ness of the Lake View, Great Boulder, and Ivanhoe, of
special importance after its criticisms of these three mines
in the early stage of their existence. The statement that
the writer, “ having failed to make his pile there, did his
best to convince the publio that the mines were absolutely
worthless,” is worthy of the paper. The inference that it
is the writer, and not the opinion with reference to these
mines, that, has been changed, is false. True, that when
the writer first saw these three mines he thought next to
nothing of them. So did the public, or Boulders would
not have been down to two shillings, and unsaleable at
that—one big line of G.B. paids was sold at “ a bob.”
Thousands of Lake Views and Ivanhoes were forfeited for
non-payment of calls. This Bi lletin' fully appreciates
the effort made in all directions to conceal the fact that
these three mines at one time—and for i, long time—were
to all appearances worthless. Callahan, now manager of
the biggest of them, thought so. Reports in the prospec-
tuses did not say so -they never do. The working capital
put up by Bull developed the Boulder into a great mine,
and the others also became great by development of ore
bodies then unknown to anybody. If there is any disgrace
in the writer not seeing below the surface he is able and
willing to bear it. Nor does he want any credit in regard
to scores of other Westralian mines he has seen and
derided, and which have not been transformed into Great
Boulders—mines that have been described by lying
experts and newspapers of the Westraha order as
amazingly rich—and by the villainous unanimity of lying
and liars have been sold in London at enormous sums,
and have had millions of money wasted on them.

* * * *
Coolgardie:—“Cristy and party, of the St.
George mine, have struck a lode 20ft. wide. Over 50oz.
have been dollied out during the last two days.”
Alas! how many such have been found in W.A.?
Big reef of white quartz, in which, after much nap-
ping, a patch is found and dollied out in a day or
two, then no more for a day or two, perhaps for
ever. The Londonderry, Wealth of Nations, were only-
big overcoat pockets—the others are, by comparison,
small ticket-pockets. At one time they were all good
enough to sell a mine on, but now they must be worked
for all they are worth, and it is more than the average
W.A. pock“t that lasts a week.
* * • *
The Investors’ Guide, issued by Clarke and Co.
(of Adelaide), for March, is well worth looking at in con-
nection with the same firm’s special Westralian number,
dated February, which discloses an appalling decline in
mining market prices between July, 1896, and January,
1897. Twenty-two stocks having the word “Hannan’s”
for a front title are now quoted at about 25 per cent., on
an average, of their July figures.
* * * *
Clarke and Co.’s latest circular gives a report on
the Norseman field by little “Uitlander” Sampson, late
of the Argus, who is running a mining paper in that far-
away region. Sampson is artistically discreet; “ Since
the examination of the mines I am convinced of the
wealth of the field, but, at the same time, it is not, in my
opinion, one of sudden popularity. It is a field that will
slowly and surely reach a good position.”
* * * •
More sensation by wire from the Great Boulder,
which is never allowed to stay a week without paper
yabber. “At the 300 ft. level’ the western leg of the
lode is 6ft. wide, and the average value is soz. ; within
120 ft. of the main shaft the western branch is extremely
rich, being worth from Boz. to 9oz. to the ton. The pre-
sent fac£ of the north drive is worth about 9oz. to the ton.
This is the third lode proved in the mine, and its dis-
covery has doubled the value of the mine. This line of
reef alone, proved for 1150 ft. long, to a depth of 300 ft., is
worth, at a very low estimate, three anda-quarter millions
sterling. The mine has improved at least 100 per cent,
during the last few days.” There is a lot more printed in
the Eastern papers. If it is all true, future crushings
from this mine should be at least 6oz. to the ton instead
of 3oz. But the market has not responded proportion-
ately.

Mount Morgan. North Co. avers it has struck the con-

true, but the very name Mt. Morgan tacked on to an out-
side show makes one immediately suspicious.

Queensland is issuing monthly returns of the
gold-yield, and Vic. threatens to follow suit. Two totals
this year —January, 44,6030 z. ; February, 54,5000 z.
Premier Nelson will probably point to the increase of
nearly 10,000 oz. in a month as one of the advantages of
keeping clear of Federation.

About 100 men are waiting in Cooktown to go
over to New Guinea, which number, added to previous
departures, will make a sum total of 400 sailing to Papua
within three months. It is said that the gold on the
Mambare is of two kinds —one heavy, the other light and
scaly. Obtained on beaches and river-banks.
* • • •
By a very small majority two Adelaide men
have displaced the two Melbourne candidates for the
vacancies on the South B.H. Co.’s board ; and we shall
see what we shall see. The polling was very heavy : 82
per cent, of the shares were represented. Mr. Laughton,
managing-director of Block 14, who lately was given a
seat on the South board and worked with the Adelaide
faction, showed shareholders that Block 14’s record was
far the better; but the Melbourne men’s retort that
Block 14 made its money out of purchased ore is largely
solid truth, though what the exact proportion of profits
made one way and the other is Fourteen’s balance-sheets
do not show. Simultaneously with the announcement of
the result of the poll came the report that what seems to
be the Western lode has been picked up in the property
at 700 ft.—a development likely to prove of great import-
ance ; the stuff is good where struck.

COOLALTA
When the fleet was at Hobart, people
missed “ those pretty little launches ” which used
to scud about the Derwent. The reason was that
the sailors had been “ done bad ” by the French
some time before, both with the oars and at “tug-
o’-war.” Admiral Bridge didn’t like it, and made
the jolly tars pull backwards and forwards even in
the stiffest of seabreezes and southerly-busters.
* * * *
An Adelaide tramp lately walked into
Govt. House and demanded a meal. Then the
visitor climbed upstairs and went to sleep in one
of Gov. Buxton’s bedrooms, whence he was police-
ejected. The want of hospitality shown by H.E.
on this occasion seems to accentuate society com-
plaints of the exclusiveness of the vice-regal set.

* * * *
The Chow mania is on the women of Vic-
toria. Single teaching has been all the rage for
some time. Almost every suburb has its classes.
Chow picnics, got up by white maidens, are not
uncommon, and now we have the Chow surprise-
party. Each maid gets her Bible Chow to call at
a certain house on a certain night. An hour is
devoted to praying, singing and Bible-reading.
Ihen the sweet creatures have great fun teaching
John funny little English games (hunt the slipper,
postman’s knock, kiss in the ring, &c.), and then
John teaches his teacher some Chinese games.
Mr. and Mrs. ROBERT BROUGH.
Photo, by Falk.
The girls declare it much nicer than English sur-
pnse-parties-and declare that “ you don’t know
how nice they are till you know them A
The other day, Meston deported some 50
aboriginals from Maryborough to Fraser Island
in the hope of reforming and preserving the poor
battered remnants. It remains to be seen whether
opium and rum will conquer, or Meston. To any
one who remembers the powerful tribes of Wide
Bay and Fraser Island some 30 years ago, the
sight of these poor decrepit outcasts trotting off
their island cemetery must have been painful in
the extreme. ' 1

*. _ *
Sydney newspaper reports of funerals are
notable for their free mention of the inevitable
ghoul who (time being valuable) never attends in
person but heaps wreaths on dead men’s graves
with the sole apparent object of having it para,
graphed that he sent a “ handsome floral tribute,”
obtained from some fashionable florist. To such
excess has this sort of thing gone of late that it is
questionable whether some dear friends of the
departed are not really in league with the wreath-
merchants.
* * * *
Melb. Age now has ‘ ‘ Births, ” ‘ ‘ Marriages, ”
“Deaths,” “In Memoriam ” and “Bereavement
Notices.” The “ Bereavement Notice ”is that in
which the relatives return thanks for sympathy
expended over the late-lamented. Thus there is
only one way of announcing the joyful marriage
episode, but there are three different fashions of
lingering tearfully over the tomb and intruding
the bones of your household grief on the public
notice. The “In Memoriam ” is the saddest
affair of the lot. When the tenth anniversary of
your grief comes round it may possibly be meri-
torious to still remember the departed, but to
advertise that you still remember him and to
deposit the remains of your private pathos on
everybody’s breakfast table in the morning paper,
is overdoing it. An idea that would meet the
case much better would be to have in each ceme-
tery a building set apart, where people could go
and sit in ashes and sackcloth on melancholy
anniversaries, and other people who liked grief
and misery could go in on payment of a small
fee and see them doing it, and people who
didn’t want to be bathed in woe which wasn’t
their own could stay away, and everybody would
be satisfied. The only trouble would be that
many individuals who were fond of grief would
go there to lament over imaginary relatives who
never died at all, and thus get in the road of
persons who had real lamentation to do. But
perhaps this wouldn’t very much matter.
* * * *
Within the last few days two divorce cases
have arisen in which it was reported that the
parties were married, and that as soon as they
came out of church the bridegroom sprinted round
the nearest corner and never came back. In
neither instance was any reason assigned; to all
appearance the bridegroom got married casually,
and then went away casually, and casually stayed
away. One vanished husband met his wife a year
or two after, and asked her casually how she was
getting on, and then made a remark about the
weather and said “S’long,” and went round
another corner. The other didn’t even make that
amount of re-appearance; he remained permanently
round his original corner. The Court worried and
tore its wig a good deal over these cases with th*
idea that there was collusion somewhere but
could find no traces of it; apparently it was

Some little
time ago, six eman -
pated Maori land
ladies resolved to
spend a holiday
camping out “on
their own,” with
no men in the
party. They
reached their des-
tination—a seclu-
ded gully, eight
miles from any-
where—all right.
They also pitched
their tent with
reasonable success
alongside a beauti-
ful lagoon, and
then they got into
six nicely - frilled
bathing costumes
and went in for a
swim. And, while
they were swim-
ming, a smoulder-
ing tree, which
they had taken no
account of, fell
square on top of
the tent and
squashed it in,
and the whole
show and all the
clothes blazed up
there and then,
and were con-
sumed, and the
party was left out
alone in its bath-
ing costume and
nothing else. A
woman may have
the suffrage, but,
until she learns not to pitch her tent alongside a
burning tree, she isn’t going to be the equal of
man, all the same.
* * * *
Actress Ada Ward—just turned Hallelujah-
lass—attributes the topsy-turvy to an appearance
to her of “our Lord ’’—whom she of course iden-
tified by the conventional portraits of the Saviour.
It’s always so ! People who have “visions” of
Heaven or t’other place never, by any chance,
describe anything but what they’ve been taught
about angels, and harps, and crowns—or demons,
and flames, and bowlings. They can’t add any-
thing from their own imagination, because they
haven’t any—or they would never have swallowed
such iirotesqueries about a future existence.
Visions are from within, not from without, and
what can never be proved not purely subjective
is triumphantly paraded as proof of the objective
existence of Paradise and Tophet, whereas it
merely proves the existence of itself.

Editor Bulletin, —Considering the popu-
larity of the name of Victoria amongst those
people whose lot it falls to to bestow names, I pro-
pose to add to your grovel programme for the
record reign by changing the name of everything
into “ Victoria.” Also let everybody be called
\ ictoria, male and female alike. It would create
the greatest confusion ever known —that of the
Tower of Babel wouldn’t be a circumstance to it :
it would be the record confusion of the world, and
the greatest lickspittle performance in creation.
At the same time it would simplify things a great
i? i a com P an i° n scheme I suggest that all
the locks in Australia be made to open with one
key—which would also simplify things.—F.

Crime is the only thing that is booming in
the Vic. legal world. The February list was the
longest on record, and for the first time for over a
decade the criminal court has combined its sit-
tings from the middle of one month to the middle
of the next. The attractive parcels of “under 16”
stock are at present specially appreciated by the
gallery experts.

A frowsy back-lane foul with garbage, right in
the heart of the din,
One in clerical guise, with the wolf in his eyes
Prowling the corner along with two half-naked
implets of sin.
A swirl at an odorsome doorway, a volley more
foul than the rest—
Mouldy bread, putrid bones, scattered over the
stones,
A rush, and a morsel of toast disappears in the
clerical vest.
A grey-stubbled visage, all shame-flushed, glides
into the roar of the street,
And the implets wade in as they chuckle and
grin :
“ Say, Mick, now that ’ere wus a w’ite cove ! ’E
knowed we wus ’ard up for meat! ”
H.P.B.


Crowned heads of Europe have always
given their royal patronage to Ed. Pinaud, of
Paris, but never so much as lately ; his perfumes
are now recognised as incomparably the best in
Europe. The biggest hits of Ed. Pinaud have
been “ Violette Heine ” (quite different from
“ Rhine Violet ”), and “Preciosa,” the sweetest
and most delicate perfumes ever made; in fact,
they are known everywhere as the quintessences
of the richest flowers grown in France, and are
sure to be rushed by everybody.*

Melbourne worshippers snort on finding
the local Anglican cathedral doors closed against
them when oratorios are the form of divine wor-
ship on tap. The alternative to going away and
making a night of it is to stump up half-a-crown,
and join in the melodious devotions. This is how
“the authorities” explain: “In moderating
what might be a somewhat unseemly rush we are
conducing to, rather than retarding, a spirit of
reverence.” And nothing less than half-a-crown
will assure “a spirit of reverence.” It is good,
worldly horse-sense —people respect the thing they
pay for, and cheapness breeds contempt. Ask
any theatrical manager. There is a tale of one
who actually went into the street and asked
people to enter his theatre free. “ What’ll yer
give us ? ” was the natural query. The analogy is
fair ; people won’t go to church now unless they
are paid to go or have to pay to go.

The assets over liabilities of the Austral-
asian branch of the Salvation Army were, to the
end of September last, .£111,690 11s. 6d. The
Army will soon have a beam in its eye.
* * * *
Melb. : “ Seriously, where does the Salva-
tion Army get all the money ? It is paying
off .£19,500 on the Y.M.C.A. Hall which it bought,
and it dubs down £3OOO for the Model Lodging
House ; another lump to follow in six months.
But, drenched and almost demoralised with
money, they starve the bulk of their officers, and
the .£25,000, or what not, is saved out of the lady
chaplain’s—l mean captain’s—ragged clothing.”

A pair of horses attached to a carriage
bolted down Pitt-st., Sydney, for half-a-mile the
other day, smashing all before them till they came
to a dead stop right against S.M. Herald office.
Their momentum was wasted on the inertia of
that solid old Conservative journal. The Tory
prejudices of the Herald are so ancient and so
firmly-rooted that they would bring up a good-
sized earthquake “ all-standing.”
* * * *
The juvenile Rechabites of Victoria have
not yet been called upon to take a pledge against
smoking as well as drinking. The question of the
dual oath was raised at the recent Rechabite con-
ference, but several delegates protested that it
would be inconsistent for the old ’uns to damn
tobacco whilst they themselves were “indulging
in the habit,” and the proposition was accordingly
lost. A decent regard for consistency also pre-
vents the matured Rechs. from imposing an anti-
financial frauds pledge upon their deah young
friends.

recently from a neigh-
boring province, had
engaged rooms, and
he retired at a rather
late hour with his law-
ful wedded wife one
night last week. The
eminently respectable
pair had hardly sunk
into a virtuous doze
when they were a-
wakened by a series
of shocks followed by
various dull thuds,
suggesting that the
roof was falling in on
them a rafter at a
time. As a matter of
fact, somebody was
“laying into” the
discreet gentleman
and his amiable lady
with a club. Shrieks,
shrill alto profanity,
and hoarse cries, in-
terspersed with the
steady thump of a
lethal weapon,
brought assistance
with lights, and a
muscular, elderly
lady, armed with a
fire-shovel, was dis-
covered battering the
bedded couple with
the energy of despair
and the fiery indigna-
tion of a wronged
matron. She was
pulled off and held
down, uttering spas-
modic gasps about
villains and hussies.
It was all an error, of
course. The athletic
female had hired a
room at the palace for
the express purpose of
“laying for ” her way
ward husband and
some “ depraved per-
son.” She thought she
had cornered the sin-
ners, but gracefully
admitted that it was
her mistake.

hymn-book?”

(Fact was Willie had got a sniff of the benzine the lady had been “ cleaning ”
her gloves with.)

Divorce-Court Justice Simpson stated from
the Bench last week that he could not, under the
law, hear a divorce case in camera. Minister
Gould told Parliament, in June last, that a judge
could hear a case in camera where public decency
required it, and, as a matter of fact, several N.S.W .
politicians and others have obtained divorces in
camera. There is a little discrepancy here.
FILIAL AFFECTION.
JIM : “It was very fortunate for me, Billy, that my old man was sent here.”
BILLY : “ And it was very unfortunate for me, Jim, that my old man CAME here! ”
The Popular Preachers in Melbourne will
soon be in full cry on the text of Federation. Rev.
Alexander Marshall has made a start. “ Through
Federation,” said he, sermonising at Scots’ Church
on a recent Sunday, “the charm of a common
name and destiny will be upon us all.” Which is
distinctly pretty. But destiny in which world .
’Twas the Black Recording Angel of Damnation,
And he said “ For me this mighty cheerful looks,
And a devilish good thing is Federation—
It will very greatly simplify my books.”
Section XV. of N.S.W. Dog Act reads :
“ If any constable neglect to seize and kill all
dogs found at large, he shall for every such neglect
pay a fine of ten shillings.” Here is a chance for
citizens who think laws, because they are laws,
should be enforced. The fact that no constable
has yet been charged with such neglect proves
that the police are really without enemies.
Another infliction has been added to the
burden of the Melb. householder, whose life al-
ready consists of a series of interviews with
rabbit-men, fruit-hawkers, and obituary-poets.
The last pest is a greasy person with a large port-
folio, which he proceeds to unfold on his knee after
he has carefully inserted his toe into the door-
jamb. Then, opening with a slab of grovel cribbed
from the speech of some fat Legislative Councillor,
he perorates at length on the glorious reign of Her
Gracious Majesty Queen Victoria. He raises his
belltopper respectfully as he pronounces the name,
and submits a number of designs for illuminations
—glaring scarlet profiles of fat women surrounded
by wreaths of vegetables, and with the words
“ God Save the Queen ”in big blue letters. The
illumination-canvasser is lOdeg. more virulent than
the book-canvasser, and when he doesn’t get an
order it is only because he is vigorously stoushed
by the eldest son, or because the house-hound has
chewed several pounds of meat out of him.
Meanwhile, the cunning Melb. business man is
preparing his gas-jets for the big celebration, so
as to combine perfervid loyalty with judicious
advt. Who does not remember the Melb streets
at the time of the ’B7 jubilee ? The whole city
scintillated with such devices as “God save our
Queen; good tweed suits, 305.,” or “ Fifty years
of a blameless life ; our best blood-sausages, 3d.”

The newspapers in Sydney Public Library
are arranged in a row of old packing-cases. The
flooring is worn out, and the staircase absolutely
dangerous. And what did the Marble Bar cost?
A Chinese cook at a Yalgoo (W.A.) hotel
was about to dish up soup for dinner, when the
Jap. laundryman employed at the same place
dished up the China-Jap. war. Then the Chow
dashed the pot of soup over the Jap., saying
“ Wah foil you always talkee talkee Jap. killum
Chinaman, you yellow bellee ! ” The Jap. had to
go to hospital.

THE LADY CRICKETER.
It was a fair “ Forget-me-Not,”
A damsel known to fame
As one among the little lot
Who play our noble game.
She blushed a bright Vermillion
When quitting the pavilion
(The captain was to blame).
The maiden fluttered forth to scout —
The “ Sea-Foams ” being in ;
Her figure not at all too stout,
Nor yet a bit too thin,
Was clad in raiment simple ;
The sweetest little dimple
Adorned her dainty chin.
Her eyes an instant flashed in mine,
And tempted me to speak
As follows : “ Amazon div'ne,
Excuse my vulgar ‘ cheek ;
Pray, whither do you wander ?
Which favored spot out yonder
Doth Margarita seek ? ”
The color tinged her angry brow
Still deeper than before.
She muttered, “ Do not ask me now.”
I pressed her all the more
To name her destination,
Whilst a look of agitation
The modest vision wore.
“ Where will you stand to run them out,
Or catch them for an * egg ? ’
What is your title when you scout ?
O answer me, I beg.”
She whispered (libel shocking
On the limb within her stocking !)
“ They call me Short-Square-Leg.”
Melb.
E.F.
