A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, March 16,1897.
Mr Dear Mtee—
Florence Schmidt, a Rockhampton girl and a
twelvemonths’ pupil of Signor Steffani, of Sydney,
has suddenly sprung into notice since her vocal
recital given last week in the Y.M.C.A. Hall,
before an invited company of professional and
amateur musicians. Her singing was quite sen-
sationally successful for a first performance, a,nd
worked up her audience—an afternoon one, which
is proverbially cold—into enthusiasm. Miss
Schmidt’s father was a German, but his daughter
displayed in her vocalization the impulsive tem-
perament of the artist, and made her success
chiefly in the florid arias of the Italian school;
her mother is Irish, and in the race struggle
the Celtic has overpowered the Teutonic type.
Steffani is immensely proud of his pupil and con-
siders her destined to be a great vocalist. “There
are plenty good voices in the world,” he says,
“ but very few singers. Miss Schmidt is one of
the few.’’ Her ambition has always been musical.
In Rockhampton she has appeared on concert
platforms as a pianist, and has preserved an en-
thusiasm for everything connected with music,
remarkable in a place where her only means of
keeping enthusiasm alive was by reading about her
favorite subject. Her voice is a clear soprano,
very pure and true, and, besides, she has the
gift of style and imparts a distinction to what
she is singing. One of her selections at her
recital was the well-known test piece, the
“ Shadow song,” through which she came triumph-
ant. The inevitable comparison with Melba is,
of course, a mistake at this stage —but this, at
least, may be said—should Miss Schmidt ever
rouse Oovent Garden to five recalls after the
Garden scene in “Faust,” it won’t be in spite of
her coldness as is the case with Melba ; her singing
is, even now at the beginning, thrillingly intelli-
gent and sympathetic.
In view of the anticipated boom m titles next
June, I should have thought that the market for
Windsor uniforms and gold-lace would have been
active, but the demand is still flat. At the
auction-sale of a C.M.G.’s furniture and effects
last week, “lot 62” was a “Court sword, riohly
gilt handle and scabbard ” —purchased by a dealer
for Bs. 6d., and carried off by him along with com-
monplace stair-rods and pots and kettles.
A sassiety man-milliner is at present hustling
to get all his orders completed for tailor-made coat
and skirt costumes. Not for years has there been
such a demand for tailor-built serges and cloths.
Some women are innovating to the extent of hav-
ing the jacket cut “Eton” fashion, but the
majority will be clad in the orthodox three-quar-
ter length coat, and all Sydney will appear arrayed
in one uniform.
Gatherings at the Victoria Club, in Phillip-
street, are becoming more dressy and less deserted
by men. Last week’s tea-party was a toney
assemblage, and graced by two of the Cri. co.—
Bessie Rignold, who sang, and Noney Seabrook.
The proprietress is ambitioning to have a “ salon
orchestra ” started, not necessarily of women
only—a female string band is always rather flabby
about the double bass, and the woman isn’t yet
born who can blow herself assertively into a
cornet; but, with men to do the heavy business, a
club-band ought to have a career.
Lesna, the French champion wheelman, knows
no English. Little Gulliver and he were intro-
duced to each other in a cycling store one day last
week, but could make no headway. “He jabbered
away in French,” said Jack of the Frenchmen,
“ but, lor, he might just as well have been talking
Chinee.” On Saturday afternoon at the League
Sports the natty Poignon, manager of Lesna,
screamed Gallec encouragement to him as he
whizzed past with Elliott gaining on him, “ Allez,
Allezl’’ “What’s he saying?” asked a maiden
of her attendant swain. “’Urry, ’urry,’’ he ex-
plained, “that’s the only English he knows.” The
male with explanatory comments is frequent
everywhere when he takes out his girl, but most
frequent at bicycle sports. He expounds loftily
to her things of which he is quite ignorant, and
she admiringly exclaims “oh, fancy ! ” To judge
by the queries of the young woman out with her
young man, the entire universe seems to be to her
an unintelligible parable of confused and meaning-
less sights and sounds until interpreted by the
explanatory male.
S. Churchill Otton issued gushtul programmes
fora “Federation concert” at the Town Hall on
Saturday night, as a tribute to the elected ten
whose names, in large caps, took up most of one
page of the programme. “ Federation concert ”
is a cheap - sounding name, like “Hampden
grocery,” or “ Brand laundry,” but served as a
draw to a fair audience which insisted on encoring
everybody. Items which bore out the title of the
concertwereajerkypolka tooted on two cornets,and
an original outpouring of patriotic sentiment by
Leon Caron and Bert Royle, sung by J. Simonsen,
which went with a sprint and had to be repeated.
Mrs. Tierney, in pink and green frills, like a grate
ornament, sang effectively, and Madame Caron,
after an Italian aria with many trills and shakes,
was handed up an inharmonious vegetable offer-
ing in yellow and pink. Miss Eva Mylott, in
white, hurled a powerful contralto at the audi-
ence which contained an uncommon number of
pretty girls—probably because it was Saturday
night, and the couples took their stroll Town
Hall-wards to have a shillingsworth.
A curious group collected at St. John’s Church,
Darlinghurst, on Thursday last, to see ex-Sheriff
Cowper face the matrimonial music at past-super-
annuation age. His bride, a tall, well-looking
young Maoriland lady, was married under her
original name as Miss Amy Bramley, and her
wedding-gown was a subdued and becoming grey
tint. Sir G. Dibbs acted father in the ceremony,
and subsequently refreshments were distributed
at a select Potts Point caravanserai to the guests,
whose names were withheld “by request” from
an inquisitive public thirsting for printed infor-
mation on the interesting subject.
The wedding of Capt. Ferguson and the Hon.
Margaret Brand has been fixed for a month
ahead (20th April), at the Cathedral, where, unless
extra cast-iron vice-regal precautions are taken to
prevent crowding, there will most probably be a
repetition of the villainous scenes at the two
famous “ crush-weddings ” Essie Jenyns’ and
Ida Hamilton’s, which were also, incidentally, of
course, J. A. Woods’ and Scot Inglis’ bridal
scrambles. But nobody ever thinks of tacking
the bridegroom’s name on to his own nuptial cele-
bration.
Why doesn’t some kindly trustee exhibit a
“ key ” along with the new N.S.W. Gallery pic-
ture, “The Judgment of Paris”? Visitors
admire cautiously, digging deep into their
memories for any buried fossil fragments of
mythology that would help them to find out what
the painting is about. The general impression is
that the shepherd of Mt. Ida is choosing a wife,
and that Venus, whose golden hair is a-hanging
down her back, is the favored object. Another
explanation finding many supporters is that Paris
is deciding between virtue, vice and respectability
—or that the male figure represents the first Federal
Premier choosing between Melb., Sydney, and
Bathurst as a site for the capital.
Florrie Young and husband Ri vington are stay-
ing at Manly until the sailing of the Ophir. The
Melb. soprano still cherishes the idea of making a
name in London stageland, though her husband’s
family are strictly opposed to any connection
appearing before footlights—especially in “ boy ”
parts.
“ The Bookmaker” has every appearance of con-
tinuing to lay the odds at the Criterion for some
time to come, though his second night suffered,
like all the other theatres, from the electrically-lit
bicycle carnival. Thornton shows a gaudy red
tie, many diamond rings, and the wrong sort of
brown boots, but isn’t otherwise aggressively vul-
gar in attire ; the usual vociferous checks of the
stage horsey-man are absent, and the actor depends
on his acting and not on his clothes to characterise
his part. Noney Seabrooke, who has patched up
her tiff with Thornton and is still leading lady,
looks an expert horsewoman as Lady Jessie, and
very much like herself taking her daily ride in
Centennial Park—Miss S. is an enthusiast for
riding who has not yet deserted her steed for a
bike. Her trim little figure displays the latest
mode in Rotten Row riding-rig—a rather full-
basqued bodice and neat checked waistcoat accu-
rately cut, over a just-long-enough skirt with a
glimpse of jaunty leggings, hat of “bowler”
shape, which is English for “boxer.” Bessie
Rignold is an intense-looking Sybil. She is one
of those girls who “ make up ” effectively. In the
street she looks like a skit on herself.
It is quite uncommon now to find an injured
wife going for judicial separation instead of seek-
ing to cut the painter altogether in the Divorce-
court and making a fresh start. A recent plaintiff
in a Sydney j.s. case admitted that her husband
would rather she went for divorce, but she wasn’t
going to do it. She wanted separate support, and
had nothing else in view. His surprised Honor
asked what she meant by that, and she
explained that a woman wanting divorce
has generally her eye on someone wait-
ing to wed her as soon as she has her
decree absolute. In America, divorce doesn’t
imply severing of acquaintance, and a woman
may have four or five ex-husbands all friendly
together. At a recent wedding of a much-divorced
lady in the U.S., she had her four previous ven-
tures acting as groomsmen at the ceremony of
uniting with hubby No. 5. It is a fact that the
first husband of R. L. Stevenson’s wife gave her
away—not in every sense--to the author, and
made an eloquent speech at the wedding-break-
fast, in which he very Americanly said he had so
much confidence in R.L.S. that he handed over to
him the entire family.
An exaggerated dress-bill alleged to be the ordi-
nary toggery-account of a wealthy Englishwoman
has been claiming its bloated total of £12,000 as a
usual amount for a British millionairess to spend
in wearing apparel. Such birds of Paradise don’t
come out to the kawlinies, at any rate. Most of
the touring duchesses and countesses whom we
have had the opportunity of inspecting on this
side of the globe have been chiefly remarkable
for a fondness for economical dust-cloaks and flat-
heeled, long-enduring boots, while their bonnets
might have been built in a back-blocks general-
store. On the mail-steamer, the first-class passen-
gers dress at each other, and have rival diamond-
shows every dinner-time, but it’s the Australian
toney who thus gleam in emulation —the English
globe-trotters are eminently dowds. There was a
frantic attempt to dress up to vice-royalty on the
P. and O. from Hobart, with the returning vice-
regal family on board, but His Ex. appeared at
his first dinner clad in tweeds, and a disappointed
shudder ran around the saloon.
Needs’ annual pupils’ ball was held on Friday
evening last at the rooms at Nithsdale. It was a
well - attended festivity with a business-like
air of dancing for dancing’s sake; every man
approached his partner with an eye to getting as
much waltz out of her as time allowed, and girls
consulted their programmes as carefully as bet-
ting-books and jotted down engagements ahead in
a thoroughly practical spirit. There was an easy-
running floor and a sufficient supper which was
taken by the dancers with a view to recuperation
for further sprints on the ball-room track.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ Signora Coy’s benefit concert fell upon a mar-
row-searching night. Nevertheless, the audience
rolled up in force. Romantic patriarchs, eager to
witness the re-illumination of the lights of other
days,braved the biting South Polebreezesasstoutly
as the carping juniors who dropped in to quiz the
raptures of their progenitors and remained to
applaud. The beneficiare, her husband and
daughter, got bouquets enough and to spare, and,
like most of their fellow performers, let loose
floods of florid Italian vocalization upon their im-
pressed hearers. Upturned moustachios were
sufficiently prevalent in the Town Hall that night
to impart quite a flavor of fierceness to the alto-
gether. Otherwise the audience would not have
impressed the uninitiated onlooker as particularly
Uitlandish. Brunettes were hardly more plentiful
than usual. Australians, as a race, are darkening
faster even than the people at ’Ome, and such
blondes as there are run mostly to the tawny or
‘ strawberry ’ type. Before long, flaxen hair, from
its very rarity, will, by itself, qualify the owner for
professional belledom. Passing in review the
sharers in the Coy programme : Ida Osborne, for
example, looked far more like the typical Italian
of one’s fancy than either soprano Rebottaro or
the ingenue of the evening, winsome young
Cesca Vollugi. The latter, by the way, covered
herself and her teacher (Signora Coy) with glory
by winning an unusually spontaneous encore and
quantities of flowers, in addition to the pure white
posy brought along especially for the debutante
by the good-hearted wife of ‘Big’ Clarke the
Second. Poor old Signor Cecci would have re-
joiced in the initial success of his dead partner s
daughter. He had lived under the Vollugi roof
for 21 years, and his end was hastened by excite-
ment over a law-suit instituted to recover the
price of Mdme. V.’s piano from an indebted
compatriot.
“At the Coy concert it was remarked that
Madame Boema’s hair, which was only turn-
ing grey the last time she sang in public, is
now perfectly white, through worry and grief over
her only son’s illness. The young fellow is stricken
with consumption.
“ The members of W.C.T.U. are all agog at
present over the despatch of two dear creatures to
Europe and America on Conference business. The
lucky delegates, wichout spending a penny of
their own, will have a grand trip and unlimited
openings for yabber. Can’t you imagine you hear
them full tilt at this sort of thing? ‘Dear
sisters ! your hearts would bleed if you could only
picture one-half the awful intemperance of Aus-
tralia, the great Godless land represented by Me
at this Blessed Conference ! ’
“Really ! platform holiness pays. One gushful
W.C.T.U. delegate belonging to another pro-
vince parades over the country on behalf of the
Union, while her meek husband minds his
suburban drapery. Fortunately for himself, he
basks happily in reflected glory. ‘ There were
just the three sisters,’ he observes reverentially.
‘ One married Major Dash (Salvation Army), the
second wedded Mr. Blank, the third accepted
Me ! ’ The saint in question is no orator, but
she firmly believes in herself, and has been
known to start off with a complacent squeak to
this effect: ‘ Dear sisters ! as I speak, no doubt
some of you are thinking “ but we have not your
power of language, we could never address meet-
ings as you do.” Dear sisters ! have you not your
own opportunities ? Can you not collect money
for the Cause ? Can you not petition your pastor
to disallow intoxicants at Sacrament ? ’
“There is trouble among Church of England
folks in Melbourne because the people who run the
Cathedral show are charging 2s. 6d. for admission
to hear Bach’s ‘ Passion ’ music. As it will cost
about .£l5O to pay expenses on this occasion, and
as a collection only brings £3O, the charge is made
so that there may be no loss—cathedrals, like
theatres, abhorring a bad ‘treasury.’ Besides,
enough buttons have been taken in the collection-
bags to supply the trousers of all the bishops,
deans, canons, and choir-boys from now to the
Day of Judgment. The pious protests in the
papers are probably from people who have hitherto
enjoyed the music * on the nod.’
“ The charge of 2s. 6d. admission to the per-
formance of ‘ The Passion ’ at St. Paul’s Cathe-
dral seems somewhat excessive when the seating
accommodation is taken into account, for only
about half the audience will be able to see the
choir, and little more than that number will be
able to hear perfectly. The charge presses very
heavily on loyal supporters of the church, who
would prefer to pay a shilling for the pit.
“Melbourne’s Worth seems determined to
strengthen the growing taste for red, for his window
in Collins-street was a blaze of the Turkiest last
week, and the city is more than ever dotted o’er
with goblin touches. The red-flyer, with glimpses
of a red tie and hat-band above, scorches through
the streets, and Reggy Beau-Bright—l had almost
said Brummel —after a year’s visit to England,
returns with a red exotic in his button-hole. Even
noses are red since cyclones have been infesting
the coastal districts.
“ The wife of a gentleman renowned for his
smart waistcoats was carefully photographed in
the neglige ‘boudoir’ dress in which she languished
after the christening of the well-announced heir.
The portrait, now on view in a Collins-street
show-case, represents the lady as a sweet-eyed,
seraphic, fragile creature, on whom the hallowing
influences of a little event have shed subtle beauty.
An ignoramus who demurred to the exhibition of
this work-of-art was certainly never intended for
a happy father, and most people will think that
the photographer should be canonised for leaving
to posterity a picture so calculated to dispel the
gloomy suspicion that marriage is a failure.
“The poetry of matrimony—worse luck !—is a
luxury sacred to the gilded classes, and far
beyond the reach of ladies who turn a mangle, or
do a day’s cleaning for 3s. 6d. Even the bank-
clerk in a tidy collar finds ‘ baby ’ a terrible
awakening from love’s young dream as soon as
his Angelina begins to get about again ; and by
the time that their household treasure has com-
menced to ‘ take notice ’ the unloveliness of things
pertaining to its early career has entered into
Edwin s soul. The wife may be a good manager,
but when required to rock the cradle on a modest
salary, she is bound to hang up her aesthetic
yearnings along with the linen in the back-yard.
Which reminds one that if the world didn’t wear
clothes, more than half of the refining influence
of hard cash would become null and void in uppah
circles.
“ Melb. sassietyhas received yet another fashion-
able infant into its midst. For weeks be-
fore the auspicious event friends of the family
were invited to inspect the layette and bassinette
(by cards) at a leading draper’s establish-
ment in Swanston-street ! Astounding ! Just
supposing that—but no matter !In due course
one may expect to hear of the Town Hall being
engaged by a mother-expectant for a grand dis-
play of baby’s first trousseau, and cynics will need
to probe themselves with a safety-pin to make
sure they are awake.
Brassey did very well at the opening of
Melb. Cycle Show. In fact, she did very much
better than her stout spouse would have done
bhe was really graceful, in manner and speech'
instead of simply ponderous and stodgy. Gov’
Brassey would have quoted statistics as to the use
of the bike in the navy, and related how his
defunct father very nearly invented a bicycle-
also he would have said hum-ha, and waddled a’
great deal. But Lady Brassey is really ‘very
nice.’ In fact, having no objectionableness to
cover up with a title, it’s quite a pity she is a Lady
anything. She is good enough to be simply Mrs.
“A very smart audience filled the Princess's on
Saturday night at the premiere of ‘Two Vaga-
bonds.’ The play began at a quarter to eight,
and the d.c. box occupants began to arrive half-
an-hour later, so that the first two acts were
apparently gone through on the stage in dumb
show. Miss Elliott Page, by far the most distin-
guished-looking member of the co., endures much
gilded misery and displays wide expanse of bare
neck and shoulder-blade during this most most
affecting drammer. If Miss E.P. is an exponent
of the very latest mode of evening gowning we
must believe that the bodices are being snipped
away by fashion-designers in every direction, so
that to keep them on one’s bones at all appears to
be a perfect conjuring trick. An evening gown
of white satin, filmed over with cloudy grey
chiffon, has a bodice of insignificant proportions
supported by bands of steel sequins. Miss Fer-
rar’s most noticeable gown is of apricot silk cut in
a graceful Princess robe and has the long, up-to-
date tight sleeves. The pathetic acting of Miss
Florence Terriss silenced an unusually restless and
noisy house, and a louder audience nor a larger
display of white-handkerchief has never been seen
at this theatre than there was at the close of the
last act. Mrs. Maesmore-Morris surprised many
by changing on that evening from a merely pretty-
woman to a sympathetic actress. Her scene with
the dying boy was much applauded.
“A French comedy is in course of rehearsal
among the Nicest people in Melbourne —Lord
Shaftesbury and Miss Daisy Madden to the fore.
“One result of the rout of the Tory rump in
Victoria is that dapper little draper Sargood is
leaving the country for a few months. He is
going to England and America with his family. A
big party of employes and admirers was invited
to his place, Rippon Lea, for the Saturday after
the election. It was, of course, arranged to be a
sort of triumph meeting, with the victorious Sar-
good as the heroic figure in the middle. But the
electors spoiled the fun, and there were no con-
gratulations. The function was more like a wake
for one politically corpsed.
“Nothing more idiotic can be imagined than
the Military Tournament as carried out in
Melbourne. How a number of men could be
persuaded to make such fools of themselves is a
mystery. A crowd of lunatics meant to look like
Dervishes rush about anyhow, and another crowd
of Victorian Mounted Rifles and nigger minstrels
supposed to be Egyptians also rush about firing,
shouting, and * screaming, until Lieut.-Colonel
Hood flourishes his sword, shouts ‘Hurrah for the
flag,’ waves a bit of rag, and looks as much like a
conqueror as he knows how. Policemen could
never be persuaded to make such a stupid exhibi-
tion of themselves, but Victorian soldiers seem to
think they are showing the public what war is
like. They are not; they are only showing what
fools they are.
“ At the opening of the Military Tournament,
His Ex. and Lady B. drove up, followed by five
vehicles of various descriptions, each packed quite
full of the numerous ‘ house-party ’ at present
being entertained at Govt. House. The labor of
finding amusement for an assorted horde of guests
in this season in melancholy Melbourne must be
particularly heavy, and it was a frankly-bored-
looking party which filed on to the Exhibition
platform behind H.E. Lady B. wore seasonable
cream alpaca with touches of pale blue, and pro-
vided against climatic eccentricities with a pile of
wraps in the carriage. Mrs. Freeman Thomas
wore the bodice of her blouse low cut, with a lace
tucker, and left her pretty hands and arms glove-
less ; the Brand girls were in black and white.
These ladies don’t do much to convince a doubtful
public that Sydney modistes are as smart as Melb.
ditto.
Yours affectionately,
CLEO.
Rev. E. G. Moberly does not go to Suva,
Fiji (writes a correspondent re a recent Bulletin
paragraph). He accepted the post, through a
telegram from Sydney, but on arrival found the
position filled, and that the offer to him was un-
authorised. Mr. Moberly is not a brother, but a
nephew of Bishop Moberly. He is brother-in-law
to the present Archbishop of Canterbury.
Hotplate Jaffray has taken back what he
said about the inmates of Melb. Benevolent
Asylum recently, and these “ pampered paupers ”
are to be supplied with warm crockery in future.
But, as a set-off against this concession, the com-
mittee has ruled that they shall, when the superin-
tendent chooses, be obliged to work, even if late at
night. Outside the Asylum, Chinamen are fined
for doing the same thing.
Barring Lyne, of N.S.W., who is non-
committal, the Opposition leader of every pro-
vince is more or less enthusiastic about the neces-
sity of his particular Premier going to England
next June, and wants to whoop the head of the
Government on the downhill track to destruction.
Lyne, who doesn’t whoop, is wise in his genera-
tion. If Reid goes with his approval, then Lyne
can’t well jeer at him for his inconsistency in
doing what he said Dibbs shouldn’t have done.
On the other hand, if he starts disapproving now
Reid may not go at all, and there will be nothing
to jeer about. Therefore, the best thing is neither
to approve nor disapprove till the eyeglassed one
has committed himself badly, and then uncork
the vial of contempt forthwith. Meanwhile, the
spectacle of Reid struggling desperately with his
mind, and trying in vain to make it up, and
asking people to “press” him, must interest
Lyne.
“The N.S W. Premier said he had endeavored to per-

agreed to visit England, he felt it had become a matter of
serious consideration whether it would not be a wrong
thing for the mother colony not to be represented. If he
decided to go he would call Parliament together, so as to
give members and the country an opportunity of prevent
ing him going if so desired.”

Any teetotaller in the Federal Convention 1
Lord Jersey is 52 years old to-day (Satur-
day, March 20).
The wife of Premier Forrest (W.A.) is
compiling a treatise on Westralian aborigines.
“Nosey Bob,” the N.S.W. official man-
butcher, has personally attended to 33 stranglings.
Many square miles near Hamilton (Vic.)
are almost solely peopled by M‘Leans, who
among themselves speak hardly anything but
Gaelic.
It is proposed to provide a bursary in
Sydney University with the old Jack Robertson
Memorial Fund. Why not found a Chair of
Language ?
At latest, Tom Bracken, the M.L. poet,
bad rallied somewhat from his severe illness, and
a fund had been started to send him to his people
in Ireland.
N.S.W. ex-Sheriff Cowper, just retired on
a pension, has got married again. The Bulletin
respectfully abstains from several first-class gal-
lows-jokes.
There is, as yet, no sort of stone to mark
the “Vagabond’s” burial place in Melbourne
cemetery. A “ Vag.” memorial fund started by
either of the local dailies would soon supply the
want.

The Princess Royal was the shortest
woman, and Wales the shortest man of Queen
Victoria’s full-grown family. Each of the succeed-
ing sons was taller and slighter than his predeces-
sor, Leopold, the youngest, being a comparatively
lofty invalid. , ,
S.M. Herald proudly parades Sir Richard
Temple’s intolerably dull articles on Queen V ic. s
reign-regardless of the fact , that its own Henry
Lucy dubs him easily champion of all the bores in
the House of Commons !
Perky little Shenton, President of Wes-
tralian Leg. Council, has gone ’Ome ‘‘on his own,
to the record-reign celebration. He is the record
capital-city Australian mayor, having eleven times
filled the civic chair of Perth.
There is living now in Melbourne an aged
“ gentleman of means ” who is alleged to be a son
of George IV. George’s descendants are as com-
mon as banana-skins in Sydney, and can be picked
out a mile off by their startling resemblance to
Queen Victoria.
The Queensland Ministerial delegation to
the foot of the Throne will consist of old-man
Hughey himself, one wife, one son, three daughters
and Tommy Byrnes. Specially-prepared apart-
ments on board the magnificent Barbarossa will
accommodate the party. The British money-
lender will perceive that Queensland has indeed
turned the corner. ,

dailies, hasn’t got a motto. The above should
suit Mr. Syme.

revisited Springsure (Q.) the other day after a
lapse of 30 years. It was while “schoolmastering

than the provinoe he now vice-regally rules.
Sydney Burdekin, in whose drawing-room
the Reid Ministry took form and shape, will have
his claim to the longed-for “ handle ” spiritedly

A Melbourne clubman having undertaken
to eat his hat if six M.L.C.’s were not elected to
the Fed. Convention, fulfilled his undertaking.
On the following Saturday night he ate a hat, a
brown boxer apparently, before several wondering
Fatmen. The hat was made of paste by a local
pastryoook.
The biography of
the late Alfred Stephen
is seriously hanging fire.
For the last twelve or
fifteen years of his life
the ex-Chief Justice gave
a portion of every week,
and often of several
consecutive days, to the
work. His method was
to turn his papers and
notebooks over, and dic-
tate the narratives they
suggested, one of his
daughters always acting
as amanuensis. A couple
of years before his death
he was able to forecast
the finish of what, from
the-bulk of manuscript,
should make a substan-
tial volume.
AN.S.W. barrister,
some years ago, appeared
before the Full Court
to argue two points on
behalf of a convicted
thief. “ Your Honor,”
said he, “I do not in-
tend to waste the time
of the Court arguing the
first point, as in my
opinion it is untenable
and should not have
been raised ; ” and then
he went on for nearly
half a day to argue the
second point. The
Court, per Windeyer,
promptly gave judg-
ment. Said His Honor:
“The Court decides
that there is absolutely
nothing in the second
point which has l>een
lengthily argued by the
— er ieamed counsel
for the prisoner, but as
the Court is unanimously
of opinion that the first
point, which the hum
—learned counsel con-
siders untenable, is ab-
solutely fatal, the result
is that the. conviction is
quashed and the prisoner
is ordered to be dis-
charged.” Whereupon the—hum —learned coun-
sel ’s face turned a beautiful pea-green.
Gov. Brassey got one big shock in Maori-
land. At Invercargill, he bumped right into a
constable the image of himself. So far, in N.S. W.,
there are two individuals who have been mistaken
for Gov. Hampden: one is a suburban bank-
manager, and the other a railway inspector.
Do you know why the people at large per-
sist in pronouncing the name of Griffith, Queens-
land’s Chief Justice, with a final “s”? It is because
of the uncontrollable desire to hiss, and that hiss—
like the smile of the Cheshire cat—will linger after
the name of the arch-renegade has ceased to be a
memory.
Of the Tas. Federal delegates two (Brad-
don and Fysh) are knights. Seven others are
“ Honorable” —some throughout the British
dominions, others “within the province only.
M J Clarke has nought but the plain Mister,
but, with four of the others, is a gentleman by
Act of Parliament.

ing for Legislature honors at the forthcoming
general elections. Editor Vosper, of Coolgardie

G. H. Reid, The Bulletin is told, did not
absolutely decline the knighthood offered to him.
He followed a precedent which is now practically
the etiquette of such incidents that is, he gave
an answer which leaves it open to him to accept
at a later date. Premier Laurier, of Canada, has
just used the same formula.
Thomas Elder, who has just died worth a
million, and Henry Ayers, who has been very ill
lately, were the only Australians with the right to
put 'G.C.M.G. after their names. Parkes and
Stephen also attained to that rank. For years
before his death Elder was a helpless imbecile, and
his will was made nearly seven years ago. He was
a bachelor, and his sister is the wife of Barr Smith,
his partner, now about the wealthiest man in S.A.
AT THE CALEDONIAN GATHERING.
David Syme, now loyally defraying the
expense of a Vic. rifle-team to the Diamond
Jubilee ” shivoo, is the proprietor of the same Age
which “threw off” so sarcastically about the
reception of the Duke of Edinburgh in Melbourne.
On that great occasion the Argus, describing the
illuminations of the Public Library, said:
“Cressets of flame adorned the portals of the
edifice,” while Syme’s organ brutally observed:
“ On the gate-posts were stuck two burning pots
of fat.”
Laureate Austin has omitted “Jameson’s
Ride ” from his new book of poems, but he gets
there just the same with some deplorable twaddle
about Henry Battenberg having been given a seat
at “the holiest of all English hearths.” Doubt-
less an eminently respectable hearth, Victoria’s—
but only to the born lickspittle is it any “ holier ”
than the hearth of the humblest of her subjects.
It’s a blunder which would never have been made
by Alfred the First, whose flunkey-verse was at
all events the degradation of his genius ; whereas
Alfred the Second never had any genius to de-
grade.

It is chronicled of Robert Mills, an old
Brisbane printer who died the other day, that he
once worked on the late Parkes’ Empire. 1 his is
about the thousandth comp., to the present
writer’s knowledge, about whom that claim has
been set up. Either Parkes changed his staff
every day, or veteran comps, regard the old
Empire as a sort of Waterloo.
The British Sec. of State for the Coloniee
now adopts the mode of signature used bv mem
bers of the peerage, and signs his despatches and
letters simply “Chamberlain.” After all, you
can’t well blame him. Few men like to sigr
themselves “Joseph.”

A big Tasmanian landowner goes near the
championship for “thrift.” On the local railway*
there is no intermediate rate for parcels between
3d. and 9d. A pair of boots weigh too much to
go for 3d., and so are charged 9d. To save the
3d., the city shoemaker is instructed to forward
squatter M'Misery’s boots one at a time.

It is said that David Gaunson, who gained
almost the same number of votes in the Federal
poll as his old enemy, Purves, threatens libel
action against both Melb. morning papers fot
scoring his name off the advisatory lists. Gaun-
son holds that every candidate whose name was
published with a line through it was illegally held
up to public ridicule and contempt, and, there
fore, can recover damages. The Bulletin has nc
hesitation in saying that if this is the ground of
Gaunson’s contention he is very grievously ‘ out
in his law.
Editor Cooper really does deserve the
honor of that Sydney Union Club membership
Edinburgh people have been crying out for a
Radical paper these many years, and within the
last 12 months have formed the nucleus of a com
pany to produce an intelligent democratic journal.
The Scotsman began as a rank Radical. In nis
early life Cooper was Radical in word and
thought. But years passed, the Scot man became
wealthy, and Mr. Cooper discovered all the vir-
tues of Respectability. Scotch Tories are now
the chief patrons of the paper and of the little
errey man also. Hence the naturalness of that
Club incident.

GEORGE’S MASTERPIECE.
“The statement that Premier Reid might, while in England, advance the Aliens Immigration Bill, and so present the case as to secure the royal approval to that measure, has evidently
impressed Labor members. One of the party, referring to the position recently, said if Mr. Reid could obtain approval to this measure, as the result of his visit, it would certainly be time and money
well spent. He added that the party would hear what Mr. Reid had to say about the trip, and if, as seemed possible, the Premier brought forward good reasons for N.S.W. being r epresented by
him at the historical gathering, then'the party would support him in making the trip, Upon this view of the question being made known to the Premier, Mr. Reid replied: ‘Yes, this Aliens
Immigration Bill is a much more important matter than is generally imagined.’”
N.S.W. Labor-Member (after inspecting the picture): “ Decidedly , George , you ought to go ; even at the risk of being blighted (And then they both winked the other eye.)

POLITICAL
POINTS
The greatest of all scandals in connection
with Melbourne Post-office mismanagement is
P.M.G. Duffy’s pretence that there have been no
scandals worth mentioning.
Tasmania’s ten Federal Convention dele-
gates are mostly the old familiar crowd : Fysh,
Braddon, Dobson, Henry, Lewis, Nick Brown,
Grant, Adye Douglas, Moore, Clarke.
Are the Mercadool inquiries and prosecu-
tions for dummying and perjury dead? And
when are the other “flagrant cases,” of which
there was so much talk, coming on for hearing?
It is either a case of embarras de richesses, or the
N.S.W. Govt, has already bitten off a lot more
than it can chew. Meanwhile, dummying in home-
stead-leases proceeds merrily.
The strong Conservative element in the
B.A. Federation representatives is due to the
neglect of the Labor leaders to organise and keep
their party in working order. Several Labor men
missed election by only a few hundred votes, and
city organisation would easily have put in
M‘Pherson. OLoghlin, Charleston, and M‘Gregor.
As it is, the Liberals have suffered a pronounced
defeat, the first for several years.
POT AND KETTLE.
SHE: “I wish you'd give up that stupid habit of saying 'By Jove!'
What do you know about Jove? The idea! ”
HE : “ I will, my dear, when you give over saying 'The idea!' What
do you know about ideas? By Jove!”
Up to the late election the M.L. Tory
party flatly denied that tfcere was any industrial
revival in Maoriland, and averred that the country
was persistently on the downward slither into
bankruptcy. Since the election it admits that
there is a great revival, but states that it is owing
to a restoration of confidence caused by the
strengthening of the Opposition at the polls. In
other words, the people voted so consistently for
Seddon that he starts the new Parliament with a
majority of 14. And the same people are so much
relieved to find that the majority is no more than
14 that everything is looking up through sheer
gladness. What a truly beautiful thing is Tory
logic! ___
The S.A. Single-taxers put up three
Federal Convention candidates, and the three
brought up almost the extreme tail of the proces-
sion ; they couldn’t get absolutely at the tail, for
that place was occupied by the deceased George
Ash. The best Single-taxer couldn’t muster 1700
votes against Kingston’s 24,000. Judging by this
and the results in the other provinces, the Single-
tax cult is dead and buried and forgotten and
busted and gone to ruin generally.
The Argus always turns dog—and a pretty
ordinary yellow dog at that —on its fallen idols.
It supported M‘lntyre and Gillies for the Federal
Convention all it knew, till they were shamefully
licked. Then, when it was no use holding them
up as strong men any longer, it stated that they
“were recognised as the weakest of the candi-
dates,” by way of explaining that it was their per-
sonal weakness and not their own and the Argus’s
principles that the people objected to.
According to the Govt. Statistician, N.S.
Wales, for the year ending February, 1896, spent
a trifle over .£200,000 on relief works ;it gave a
miner’s-right and rail way-pass and free-ration
apiece to 7093 men —the rations alone being valued
at £880; it also supplied rations to 6500 people
other than miners; and the total Government
relief expenditure during the year appears to have
been £330,000. This is proportionately more than
is spent on relief in that very poor and low-down
country, Ireland.
S.A. Register strongly opposed the intro-
duction of party politics into the Federation con-
test. Two days before polling-day, when the
candidates’ mouths were closed, it suddenly went
for Premier Kingston thus :
When he wants to work a point in his own favor—and
when does he not? —he goes blustering about the country
like a dog suffering from an incipient attack of rabies,
yelping at large and snapping viciously at everybody
from whom he does not hope to get a bone of personal-
political advantage.
And a column more, in the same strain.
That lazy and useless
politician, John Duffy,
Vic. Postmaster-General,
is going to Washington
Postal Conference. Du-
ring his absence, his De-
partment, which has got
into a hideously smellful
mess, is to be scrubbed
and disinfected by other
Ministers. Duffy will re-
turn to find everything all
right, so that he may goto
sleep and draw his salary
as usual. Duffy has been
in office as much as any
living Vic. politician ex-
cept Gillies and Berry ;
yet he never did anything
except make alleged
humorous speeches and
grow larger beneath the
waistcoat.
S.A. is the only
one of the seven pro-
vinces where the Post-
master-General is a per-
manent official who sticks
to business and doesn’t
fool with politics. The
plan seems to pan out
well—at least, S.A. is the
only province, barM.L.,
which makes any profit
worth mentioning on its
Postal department. The
post-office is a plain busi-
ness matter, like the rail-
ways and the Govt,
printing-office, and it in-
volves no question of
party politics except the
question of finding billets
in the department for the
P.M.G.’s political sup-
porters. There is abso-
lutely no more reason why
a fresh and perfectly
ignorant man should be
shoved into the billet with
each change of Ministry,
and made boss over a
large amount of intricate
electrical machinery
which he doesn’t even
know by name, than
there is why the Govt.
Astronomer should be a
member of the Cabinet,
and Kidd and Joe Cook
and Dan. O’Connor should
take turns at watching
the transit of Venus
through the big telescope,
according to which party
happens to be on top.
J. T. Annear, the bull-voiced Chairman of
Committees in MTlwraith’s province, is not feel-
ing very well. He was a great “ Lib.” in the old
days, and ratted just as smartly as Barlow, Dick-
son, or Foxton. Yet he only gets a miserable
billet while they become Ministers. Moreover,
Maryborough is getting restive. The democratic
lions roar just as Jacky himself used to in his
prime, and there is no tree in sight. To-morrow
there will be another skeleton on the plain.
The session of Tas. Parliament opened on
the 9th inst. lasted exactly 2h. 20min. Leave of
absence was granted to Attorney-General Clark,
and a new Speaker was elected. N. J. Brown, a
ponderous ex-squatter and ex-Speaker, took the
Speakership from Stafford Bird by 17 votes to 16.
The Ministry thus experienced its first defeat of
the session—also its last; but solaces itself with
the fact that the Opposition loses the debating
strength of the massive Brown and the Ministry
gains the ditto ditto of the dethroned Bird. The
latter was regarded as the author of the Great
Western gag incident of last session—hence the
defeat.
The succession - duties received by S. A.
Govt, from millionaire Elder’s estate should bring
the Treasurer out with a substantial surplus in
June, especially as the Customs returns on March
6 were .£41,111 in excess of the receipts for the
same period of last financial year.
At Sydney Federal Convention of 91,
Gillies, Nicholas Fitzgerald, and W rixon voted
hard against the proposal that a Federal Senate
should be elected by the people and not by Parlia-
ment. This time the people took care that they
shouldn’t get a chance to vote on the subject.

The batch of Q. Govt, bailackers who
have been rewarded by being shoved on a Lands
Commission are staying at the Hotel Australia
—Sydney's most palatial hotel. Their “ labours ”
start with a pleasant picnic to enquire how things
are done in N.S.W”. By the way, there are
ructions on the Commission already, and J. T.
Bell, a little pig who has stayed at home, says
the Sydney picnic “ borders on the scandalous.”
Tasmania put up one howling anti-Fede-
ratiomst, Norman Cameron, and one parson, Wool-
nough, among its Federal candidates, and they
both had a great downfall. Cameron, who fore-
told the rum of everybody and the bursting-up of
everything through Federation, was 19th on the
list ; and parson Woolnough was only two re-
moves from the extreme end. March 4 vyas a
cold day for parsontcal ambition in two provinces,
Only 7500 electors voted at Tas. Federal
elections, and there are 31,000 on the rolls. As a
result, the delegates are for the most part promi-
nent stout persons whose chief recommendation is
that they have been fastened to Tasmania longer
than the rest of the barnacles. The one exception
is M J Clarke, a Launceston lawyer, who
managed to so far overcome the insular prejudice
against new-chums as to get m at the tail of the

Writes “Fluffy” :
Despite my candid expression of the facts, Australian
Premiers are to be hustled to accept Adelaide for the
Federal Convention. The Federal Council tacitly admitted
that its insignificance was somewhat in consequence of
the political insignificance of Hobart, and Hobart is only
one shade worse than Adelaide, and Adelaide will do to
the Convention what. Hobart did to the Council. lam
surprised that Premier Reid takes so little notice of my
arguments. Forrest is different. He barracks for
Adelaide so as to spoil Federation. He sees the incon-
sequence of meeting in a small town 1000 miles away from
a great newspaper. Melbourne or Sydney should get the
Convention. Why, Adelaide can hardly provide the mem-
bers with decent lodgings near their w6rk 1
N.S.W. Public Works Committee now in-
cludes flabby little “Captain” Frank Famell,
Daniel Demosthenes O’Connor, and ex-Laboi
member J. Lionel Fegan—three costly puff-balls
who assist in dealing annually with schemes in-
volving millions.
S.A. Federation election served to settle
in a way the rival claims of Solomon and Copley
for the National Ass leadership. “Turncoat
Bill ” Copley was somewhere about 18th, whereas
Solomon was elected. Perhaps Copley will now
want to be a Liberal again.
Melb. Table Talk in-
dignantly repudiates the
charge of laziness brought
against Duncan Gillies and
says : “ Nearly forty years
of public service is his re-
cord, having filled every de-
partment of the public ser-
vice except the legal.” In
other words, DuncaD has
slept in every State Depart-
ment except the legal, and
would have slept in the
legal also if he had known
how. The present writer
was once shown a big tor-
toise with a reputed record
of 150 years of public ser-
vice—just the same kind
as Duncan’s.
The Victorian Purifi-
cation of Rolls Act, de-
vised by Premier Munro in
the days when that futile
old Scotchman was still a
little tin god, is getting to
be, a nuisance. It was
nominally intended to pro-
vide for the removal of
dead voters, absentees and
criminals, but it is so con-
structed that under its pro-
visions inspectors wander
round the country remov-
ing people off the roll*
wholesale with a shovel.
Even the man who moves
from one street to another
or who shifts into the next
house isdisfranchised under
this Act, if the inspector
gets his eye on him : ditto
in many cases the man who
doesn't remove. The gene-
ral idea of the Act, as the
Age pnts it, is that voting
is a crime to be suppressed
as much as possible. If
the Turner Government
doesn’t wake up and sup-
press the Roll Purification
bus’ness it will discover
some day that its majority
has been disfranchised
bodily.
S. A. Tories have been put into great heart
at the result of the Convention elections and by
the victory of ex-Mayor Willcox in the N.E. dis-
trict of the Leg. Council. They will, therefore,
make a big bid for success at the Council
elections in May. Retiring members :—Conser-
vatives : Richard Baker /President), Haslara,
Darling, Magarey, and Warren. Liberals : Addi-
son, Kirkpatrick, and Guthrie, the two last-
named being Labor members.
Trenwith, of Victoria, is the only Labor
candidate elected to the Federal Convention In
N.S. W., M'Gowen, the top man of the party, was
fifteenth, and the next one was twentieth. In
Tasmania, the highest Labor man stood nine-
teenth on the list. In S.A., the highest Labor
man, Charleston, was eleventh, 962 votes behind
Solomon ; while M'Pherson and Batchelor were
thirteenth and fifteenth. Judging by the fact
that S.A. Chief Secretary O'Loughlin was de-
feated—he only secured twelfth place on the list —
and that such hardshell Tories as Downer and
Baker were elected, it seems just possible that the
Kingston Ministry isn’t quite as solid as it looks.
It is remarkably solid for a S.A. Ministry, cer-
tainly, but that doesn’t mean much ; a S.A.
Ministry is just a shade more solid than a Mexi-
can president used to be, in the good old days
when there were 260 revolutions in half a century.

SUFFERED FOR NINETEEN YEARS.
A SUPPOSED INCURABLE CURED LN
TWO MONTHS.
DR. WILLIAMS’ PINK PELLS AGAIN
\ ICTORIOUS AFTER HOSPITALS
PRONOUNCED A LADY INCURABLE
—THE AWFUL EFFECTS OF RHEU-
MATIC FEVER—A HUNDRED AND
FIFTY PEOPLE WATCHING HER—
A BRIGHT AND CHERRY PERSON-
ALITY—WHAT SHE SAYS ABOUT
THE PINK PELLS.
From the Australian Star.
The truest of axioms remarks that there is no
smoke without fire, and when the public talk in
warm and appreciative terms of the benefit they
have derived from the use of some specific, there
is always certain to be clear and honest evidence
behind to prove these words.
Hearing in especial of a local cure which had,
amongst numberless others, been effected by the
use of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People, a
reporter was despatched to the residence of the
patient in question to personally investigate the
case.
Mrs. Evans is the lady’s name, and her house is
situated at No. 105 Mary-street, Newtown, near
St. Peters.
When we entered we were met by a bright and
cheerful woman, past middle age, but still wearing
on her face the signs of recent illness and departed
pain.
We explained our mission.
“ Oh, ’ she said, “ sit down by the window there.
I'll tell you all you want to* know —and more
besides ” —she added, cheerily, “ about the mar-
vellous change that has taken place in mv con-
dition entirely since I began taking Dr. William*’
Pink Pills.”
“ You had previously been an invalid, had you
not ? ” we asked.
“ Yes,” she replied. “lam a widow, and the
mother of twelve children, and for nineteen years I
have suffered intensely bom the after effects of
rheumatic fever.”
“ And now?”
“ Ah,” she said, shaking her head and smiling,
“ I am much better now.”
“ How did you first hear of the Pills, then ? ”
“Well, a man left a pamphlet at my door
issued by the Dr. Williams Pink Pills Companv,
and I read it, and was struck by a case in it
similar to my own in which a cure had been
effected. ”
“ Did you get a box ? ”
“ Yes, I got a box, and just took the Pills about
the end of November or the beginning of Decem-
ber. ”
‘' And the improvement ? ”
“It dated gradually from that date, and as I took
each box I could feel myself perceptibly growing
better, until to-day I'm a changed woman to what
I was two months ago. ”
“ Were you very bad, then ? ”
“Yes. Before I began taking Dr. William*’
Pink Pills I couldn’t move my shoulder, neck, arms,
or hands. I couldn't think of using the sewing
machine. Now I turn the wheel quite easily with
my hand ; but before that I couldn’t lift it from my
side. Look what I’ve done with the machine,
thanks to the Pills.’’ And she she showed us a
number of ladies’ articles profusely ornamented
with fancy stitching and frills of deft embroidery.
Each stitch was white and regular, and each curve
cut with the nicest attention to detaiL
“You know I earn my living by this,” she said,
looking up, “and I’d be thankful for more cus-
tomers. But before I took Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills I could no more think of using the machine
than I could of flying.”
“ Did you try much medicine before you bought
the Pills ? ”
“ Yes, continually for 19 years; and I could
swim,” she added reminiscently, “in all the lini-
ments and stuff I’ve taken. Bottle after bottle-
one thing followed another —and there seemed to
be no end to it,”
“ Didn’t you try the hospitals ? ”
“ Yes; I tried them all. But after I’d been at
each place a while they all declared me incurable.
Why, I walked into the Sydney Infirmary in
Macquarie-street but it did me no good, and I
had to be carried out.”
“ And you have had private doctors, I suppose?”
“ Yes, I have. Plenty of them —and too many,”
she remarked, with a laugh. “ But it was no
good. After they’d treated me a bit they all told
me the same : ‘lt’s no use,’ they said. ‘You’ll
never get better. We can’t do any more for you.’
So I wasted mv money year after year. One doc-
tor recommended me to go into the hospital and
have my sinews cut. But I wouldn’t agree to
that.”
“Then you ascribe the improvement in your
Condition absolutely to the use of Dr. William*’
Pink Pills ? ”
“ Yes, absolutely,” she repiled. “ I have taken
Beven boxes by now, and I’m using my eighth.
And I intend to keep on using them. They’re the
only thing I’ve ever had that have done me any
good, and I can’t be too thankful to them. I
believe absolutely in them.
“Why, look,” she added, quicklv, as she raised
her arms, and pinned her collar with the greatest
ease. “ I could not raise my hands from my waist
before I took the Pills, and was thoroughly useless.
Now I can earn my own living. And the awful
pains from my neck and chest and shoulders have
all gone away. I never feel them a bit now.
Though in the old days,” and she gave a little
shiver at the recollection, “ if I made the slightest
move sharp pains would dart all through my neck
and shoulders.”
“ Your case is exceedingly interesting.”
“ Do you know that there are about 150 people
who have heard of me and are watching my case ?
I’m a great object of interest to the neighborhood,
and as I get well others who’re also suffering are
taking the pills, and I recommend them strongly
to all my friends. I bless the day,” she added
conclusively, “ when I first bought a box of Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills. Many people know of me,”
she added, “through my late husband, who was
for several yearn a leading officer in the Sons and
Daughters of Temperance.”
Then the reporter read the interview over to her,
and she signified its correctness and signed her
name, “Honor Evans,” below. “Honor by
name,” she said “And Honor by nature,” we
added, looking into her earnest face as we bade her
good-bye.
As we left we felt that here,-under our personal
observation, was a case, similar to many, in which
the efficacy and marvellous effects of Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills had been honestly and exhaustively
demonstrated.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Newhaven collapsed like a pricked balloon in
the St. Leger, and suffered a defeat estimable in weight
at 141 b. from The Officer. His sides heaved distressfully
after the race, and he sweated very freely. Five days
later, in the Champion Stakes, when no exception could
be taken to Holmes’s handling of The Officer, Newhaven
beat The Officer about as easily as The Officer had beaten
him, and pulled up with scarce a stain upon his bright
coat. Surely’ this was a sufficiently extraordinary reversal
of form to have an explanation asked for.” —(Melbourne
Sportsman.) But an enquiry would have been calculated
to- “ shake public confidence,” and the great aim of all
ruling powers in Smellbourne is to—haw I—prevent1—prevent that
sort of thing, doncherknow.
•*• * .
The Newmarket Handicap winner, Maluma, is
thrown into the City and Suburban Handicap with 7st.
61b. The race should be a gift for Mrs. Langtry’s mare
unless the change of hemisphere has injured his constitu-
tion.
• * • •
Some of the superfine noblemen who write horse
paragraphs for Melbourne papers are protesting against
the “horrible,” “ nasty ” and “ vulgar” name of Nudity
for a two-year-old colt. Wherefore The Bulletin, fearing
that the owner of the animal may have laid himself open
to prosecution for obscenity, or insulting behavior, ad-
vises him to change its name to Fig Leaf. What a
triumph for the blushful purists of the sporting press—
Fig Leaf (late Nudity’)!
• * •
Victoria, according to the Racing Chronicle,
is fearfully and wonderfully prolific of up-country meet-
ings. N.S.W. is responsible for six meetings per week
throughout the year, the average being mainly built up,
of course, by holiday fixtures in rough and rural districts.
But Victoria runs 509 horse gatherings per annum, irre-
spective of perpetual pony racing at Ascot, where the
added money is occasionally a handsome sum.
One solitary Chow horse-owner has been dis-
covered by the Chronicle. The name of this heathen
pioneer in “Australia’s national sport” is sufficiently
appropriate—Ching Doo.
• * * *
Aurum has been backed, in bogus wagers pro-
bably, to win V.R.C. Derbv and Cup, “ at prices ranging
from 1000 to 30 to 1000 to 50.” A real wager of 20 to 1
about Aurum for a “double” that falls due next Nov.
would amount to strong evidence of lunacy in the fool
who accepted it. Granted that the own brother to
Auraria may be a “good thing” when the time draws
near, the St. Alban’s stable, always clever at dissembling
the truth, will manage to get some money on at a fairly
reasonable price.
» * * »
Writes a correspondent :—“ Brief-snatchers ”
have been decidedly obtrusive on Flemington Hill during
the present V.R.C. meeting, and their connection with
certain bookmakers has been so evident that a revision of
the V.R.C. registry is contemplated.
• • • •
“ Clean ” sweeps—Adams’.
* * * »
“Forfeit being paid on Ebor, leaves Norton to
uphold the hopes of the South,” writes The Bulletin’s
London correspondent. “He may do it worthily, too.
He won the Tixhill Handicap, 3 miles, with 12st. up, as
he liked. From an authentic source I hear Daimio is all
to pieces—won’t eat, and won’t jump.”
* « * *
“On the declaration of weights for the Liver-
pool Grand National,” writes The Bulletin’s London
correspondent, “ it was decided by the sports and oracles
here that Ebor at 12st. 31b. held Daimio safe at 12st. 61b.,
though Norton at lOst. 71b. was likely to beat both. The
Australians are not considered seriously to have a
possible, though the English jumpers are at present a
very rocky lot. Ebor and Norton are in especially fine
form just now, but the general opinion of Daimio, based
on his first and only outing—when he performed much
better than the scribes said —damns the gelding as a duck-
footed, stuttering sky scraper, who may finish among the
first three in the 1900 Grand National. Cloister will cer-
tainly start, unless he has been turned into glue and
zoological beef by the eventful day.”
* * *
*
Zanonia, the angular and leggy cross-country
horse, will be sent for the Onkaparinga steeplechase, all
going well. Also, it is dead certain to win, in the racing
sense of the statement.
• * * *
It needs more close finishes to popularise trotting
about Sydney, but exciting finishes are not to be hoped
for whilst two-mile races are run. Should Mr. Deeble, of
Brighton, change his trots from two to one-mile dashes,
the writer feels assured he would be working in the inter-
ests of self and the trotting horse. Entries would
undoubtedly be larger, and the handicapper would have
a better show of bringing the field together, for a horse
would not have the time to spread-eagle his field and rob
the race of all interest, as is usually the case in a two-mile
event.
* * * *
Gold Medalist (by Medallion) is the flower of
the M.L. two-year-olds, and is doing what Carbine did as
a youngster here. As yet unbeaten, he is winning every-
where he starts. In him and Multiform “Yaldhurst”
Stead has a flying pair.
* * * *
A blight seems to have struck Dunedin (M.L.)
Cup. Only three starters for 500 sovs. this year. Cham-
pion Euroclydon (10st.), allegedly as good as Newhaven,
though his owner doesn’t think so, went lame a few days
before the race.
* * * *
A former well-known M.L. sweep-promoter
intends attempting the game at Hobart—but “Tattersall ”
needn’t tremble. Were consultations legalised in Maori-
land, an enormous amount would remain in the country
annually, and the incidental tax, together with the tote-
tax, would furnish a handsome contribution to the Govt,
revenue.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, —Be Tatt.’s sweeps and the
necessity of bribing owners to run and try. What is the
matter with drawing a sweep after the race or on the
very day of the race ? I am not a sweepist myself, and
may be only showing stupendous ignorance, but I’ll
chance it.—J.M., Maoriland.
• * * *
The five-“bob” banquet tendered to Premier
Reid, by his Melbourne freetrade admirers, was a small
thing indeed compared with last week’s send-off to book-
makers Arnfield and Sam Allen, who are about to go ’Ome
and glitter at the Longest Reign rejoicings. On this occa-
sion, more than a hundred red-faced capitalists staked 31s.
6d. each for the privilege of doing homage to the Free-
Raid apostles. The chair was filled to overflowing by Alf.
Joseph, and The Bulletin trusts that, for the sake of
uniformity, the banquet was “ served in Skinner’s best
style.”
* * * *
“A. W. Steel ” Eccles, who —er—departed,
some time ago, from Melbourne, and appears to be identi-
cal with the man alleged by cable to have broken the bank
at Monte Carlo, is a tall, bulky man, bearing a strong
general outside resemblance to Mrs. Madden's brother,
Frank Stephen. In his early days as a tipster, when he
shoved his way’ into the Melb. Hunt Club, and managed
to strike up a horsey acquaintance with Gov. Hopetoun,
Steel gave out that he came of a “ fine old Northumber-
land family,” but, after certain things transpired, one
heard less talk of blue blood.
* * * *
W. R. Wilson’s giant two-year-old, Majestic,
seems to be shaping better in public than was expected.
On Saturday the big ’un, starting at 4 to 1, won a £2OO
stake at Caulfield in workmanlike style. What are the
odds against Majestic being better than his stable com-
panion Aurum (they are both by Trenton) twelve months
hence ?
* * * *
Sunbury, who carried a lot of “ clever” people’s
money for the Newmarket Handicap, went for a recovery
in the St. Kilda Cup, at Caulfield, last Saturday, and
finally proved himself to be only’ a fair second-rater.
Just when Sunbury appeared to have the race won, Miss
Gladstone shot out and beat him cleverly.
* * • *
The recent improvement in the luck of the
Redleap stable has had a marked effect on Mr. Sep.
Miller’s florid countenance, which was rather down at the
mouth a while ago.
* * * *
“Elong ” to The Bulletin from near Mossgiel
(N.S.W.) : —“ Not long ago in The Bulletin was a sketch,
‘ The Stockman of the Future,’ on a bike. Two brothers
named Clancy, the well-known drovers of Booligal, passed
here on hikes, at 9.30 p.m., driving 15 head of horses.
Between here and Booligal.it is mostly salt and cotton
bush country’, and a few miles of lignum. The last seven
or eight miles of the journey it was quite dark, and 15
horses take driving at any time unless you have them in
a lane.” Clancy on a bike ! Two of him on bikes !
* » * »
George Giffen has finally arranged with Lyons,
Darling, C. Hill, Jones (S.A.), Trott and Trumble (Vic.),
Kelly, Gregory, M’Kibbin (N.S.W.), for the Westralian
trip at Easter. The team play’s in Perth, Coolgardie, and
Kalgoorlie, and will be away five weeks.
* * * *
Crioketer Blackham travelled all the \vav to
Nirranda (W. Vic.), the other day, to see that slashing
team, the Forget-me-Nots, play’ a match against the Sea
Foam sloggers. Both teams are, as their names suggest,
composed of ladies. The Sea Foam covey won by four
runs. After the match w’as over Blackham threw Miss
Lazzard, the wicket-keeper, into a flutter of delight by
remarking that she was his rival wicket-keeper. But the
teams are not likely to play a t their full strength next
season unless new recruits join them in the meantime.
The members are fast getting married.
• * • *
Hannigan, the Red fern (Syd.) bat, had rare luck
the other Saturday. Playing for Redfern against Pad-
dington, he put up the top score of 181 (three s’s and 21
4’s), and, whilst batting, news was brought to him that
he’d won £9O0 —a half-share in Wait-a-bit in Tatt.’s sweep
on the Newmarket.
* * * *
On this season’s form Victoria has only two
cricketers—Harry Trott and Trumble —who would be
picked in an Australian team.
* * • *
A West Victorian team of lady cricketers lately
played a married men’s eleven, and beat them badly’.
Most of the men were stumped—the usual penalty of
games with the allegedly softer sex.
• * • •
A full house greeted the N.S.W. Wheelmen
League’s meet last Saturday afternoon, and the night
meeting on Monday’ was another big score; a bumper
audience came despite the sharp southerly, and the league
was heartily’ cursed by theatre managers.
Walne, who has filled out and muscled up re-
markably, did an exhibition go in his heat of the First
Class Handicap. Starting from scratch with Porta he
quickly caught Payne (20yds.), steered his way through
the field, rushed to the front with the ringing of the bell,
snatched inside position a la Bilmartin, and cantered in
looking over his shoulder for a challenger. In the final he
pursued the same tactics, but a quarter-lap from home
Bilmartin suddenly outgeneralled Walne, Chapman, and
Megson. It was Martin who was inside, and nobody knew
how he got there; and in the run home the elusive
William put in a few tremendous plugs and won by half a
wheel. A blanket would have covered Martin, Chapman,
and Megson, but Martin took £l5, Chapman £3, and
Megson £2.
Porta shows his teeth now, when he bends over
and scorches. To watch Porta laboring convulsively’, and to
hear him panting, and to take note of the grinning rows
above the handle-bar, gives you the idea that the easy-
running cycle is as pleasurable as the treadmill. How-
ever, according to Dr. Muskett, the cycle more or less
cures baldness and wooden-legs.
Bilmartin bullocked like a son of Samson in his
heat of the First-class Handicap, until he caught his field,
whereupon he sat up and took his usual deep, full breath.
Edwards (England) and Ken Lewis also took a full breath
at the time. But they were off the track, and stretched
out on the grass. Except Walne, Megson and Martin, no
scratch-man shewed well in the handicap.
O. S. Prowse is an attractive pedaller, and won
his heats in the First-class Handicap and Sydney Wheel
Race with brilliant finishes. The crowd loves the man
who shoots out and comes home like an arrow.
Secretary Hunter went down on his knees to
Monsieur Lucien Lesna over the challenge race with W.
J. C. Elliott. Elliott was one of the four that Lesna had
beaten the previous Thursday, and Monsieur L.L. there-
fore reasoned that he could give Elliott a lap in five miles
and win smiling. However, during the week it became
evident that Elliott had made the match so well that he
had it won. Lesna, consequently, refused to start unless
he had all the best of the pacing and Elliott all the worst.
Elliott offered Lesna first pick of pacing, but Lesna
wanted Ist, 2nd, 3rd and 4th pick ; not Ist, 3rd, sth, 7th
pick. Hunter alternately’ threatened and entreated the
Frenchman, and at last got him to mount.
Rumors were rife that an arrangement had been
come to; that certain of Elliott’s pacers had been in-
fluenced into pacing their man so steadily that Lesna
must catch him. To meet this, Elliott warned the racing
committee that if his pacers did not obey his instructions
he would hold up his hand in protest and wheel off the
track.
The crowd’s excitement over this race was
tremendous ; and it soon reached boiling point. The
beginning was unpromising; with pistol crack Lesna’s
triplet dashed away with its man, but Elliott’s pacers had
no steam in them. Elliott thereupon spurted after Lesna,
caught him, and hung on to his opponent’s wheel.
Second lap Elliott’s pacers, stimulated by the
clamor of the crowd, picked their man up but so
execrably that he lost 20yds. to Lesna. Every time fresh
pacers picked Elliott up they lost ground for him. The
outcry of the crowd was deafening. “ Give Elliott pace ;
get going ; give Elliott a show,” &c., &c. Meanwhile,
Lesna’s pacers picked their man up and drew him along
with the precision of perfect machinery. The strangest
feature of the contest was the last. The full five miles
were run, with Elliott a quarter lap to the good, but the
bell was not rung until the race was really over, and
Lesna was therefore given an extra lap in which to catch
Elliott. And Elliott might have been caught had
he not, looking back over his shoulder, and seeing the
Frenchman’s pacers rapidly overhauling him, picked up
his machine, and, leaving his own pacers standing, come
away alone with a brilliant spurt. He won by 60yds.
It was somewhat remarkable, the easy precision
with which some of Elliott’s pacers afterwards picked up
Green, Porta and Walne. That several teams should
make the self-same blunder at, say, 3 p.m., and that they
should all improve equally by 4 p.m. wants a lot of ex-
planation. But Martin bet and lost £lO over the affair,
and Ken Lewis lost £2O. And Martin and Ken Lewis do
sometimes make mistakes.
Beautiful side touch to the Elliott-Lesna match.
The S.M. Herald, in its columns, claims that the match
was made at the Herald’s suggestion. Fancy the proper
old Herald being instrumental in bringing about a cycle-
match. Cycle-matches are as smellful as prize-fights.
Doubtless the next departure will be one of the reverends
of the Herald staff getting out upon the track in bloomers
to push Bilmartin off against Lesna ; or Mister James
himself seconding Plugger Bill when the plugger has a
knife-duel with Porta. The present scribe wishes that
the Herald would write to the League of Vic. Wheelmen
persuading that body to give its sanction to the Martin-
Lesna match. The Bulletin’s office-boy is just dying to
see Plugger Bill go for Lesna’s scalp.
The lap scratch races were mostly swift and
clever struggles. Megson came out hero ; very consoling
to him to down both Green and Edwards. For the En-
glishmen both “ did ” him in England. The walk-over in
several heats was a blunder of management. The lone
riders should have been pitted one against the other by
running two heats into one.
The paced race has caught on with the Sydney-
ites. Lesna v. Walne 10 miles evoked much enthusiasm.
If the flash tandem at the start hadn’t taken so much out
of Walne ere he had time to get his blood warm, Monsieur
might have squeaked a few times instead of winning with
an easy lead of 50yds.
Lesna behind pace both in movement and style
is the boss of the track. He clings to that back wheel
like an instinct. The only man who shapes with similar
effect is Green. And as Green’s distance is 10 miles, the
Englishman has his chance of giving the Frenchman his
Waterloo.
The athlete’s superstition ! Edwards did not
ride in his proper colors in his heat of the lap scratoh
races; he won his heat easily from sprinter Payne.
Ordered to put on his proper colors for the final, he did
so, and exclaimed : “Changing my colors! I’ll get
beaten ! ” And, sure enough, he was beaten hollow ; not
only by Megson, but also by Walne, who came out for the
lap final after his gruelling ten miles with Lesna. The
Bananalander was loudly cheered. He seems to be a
coming Bilmartin. His training has made him a big
fellow to what he was ; he is now a stone heavier than on
the day when he won his first big prize, £5O, at a League
carnival.
Those stockings of Bilmartin’s ! Everybody re-
marks them. Everybody would understand, however,
if they saw the little diamond brooch that Bill wears on
one of them. Those stockings make Bill feel well. And
Bill would rlo anything to make himself feel well.
* • * *
Charley Saiftuels, the phenomenal black runner,
came round to something like his old form whilst in
Callan Park Asylum, and has now gone to his home in
Queensland. Charley told an official in the Park that he
was offered £7OO to “go down” in his match with
Hutchens, and he has mourned ever since being such an
idiot as to refuse the offer, for, after all was over, his
“ party ” never gave him a stiver.
* * * *
A. W. Nicol, an enterprising young fellow from
Invercargill (M.L.) is in Melbourne just now with a new
“ starting-clock ” invention. By a simple arrangement of
pegs round the dial the clock-hand can be set to strike
any number of seconds or half-seconds (at appointed
intervals) within a given period. For bicycle or pedes-
trian handicaps constructed on a time scale, instead of a
scale of distance allowances, it seems bound to become
the starting-machine of the future. Meanwhile the in-
ventor, Nicol, wants to impress the advantages of time-
handicapping upon the unintelligent persons who manage
bike sport in Melbourne, and who, if they had any money
among them, would promptly form a syndicate for buying
the local fights of the clock aforesaid. In a time-started
race each competitor goes the full distance, the limit man
quitting “ scratch ” at the final strike of the clock, and so
on, until the last strike gives the signal for Bilmartin to
go in pursuit of the field. The new 33-stem would suit
spectators better than the present one ; it would also give
the handicapper a better grip of “ form” and the trouble
incidental to marking the track, and keeping a watch
upon starters all round the oval would be avoided. At
Invercargill, the clock was tried with complete success,
and the local club has decided to handicap by time in
preference to distance, henceforth and for ever. ’

There is just now on view in Regent-street
* the smallest bicycle in the world,’ made for • Master Rudd
of Melbourne, Australia.’ The youngster is recorded as
being two years and nine months old, and the gear 44
The machine has the Simpson lever chain, that ingenious'
much-advertised, overrated power-intensifier, and all up-
to-date additions and supposed improvements. It is
noticed here,” writes The Bulletin’s London correspon-
dent, “ that the toney people are withdrawing from the
bike-clubs, and there are other signs that society is tired
of the craze.”
* * * «
John Roberts’ fine attempt to give 12,000 points
to Peall, in a game of 24,000 up, may lead to another
match between the “ old man” and Frank Ives, provided
that the American wonder will consent to play cannon-
game, not only on an English billiard-table, but with balls
of the regulation size. When the men met three or four
years ago, Ives stipulated for conditions which gave him
a chance to get the balls jammed between the elbows of
a pocket, and by his greater skill in working a “ jam ” he
beat Roberts easily. Seeing that neither man was playing
the game at which he is champion in his own country
the chances are that Ives is the more versatile artist.

The Customs shed on the Moama side of the
Murray bridge is pretty well filled bv the burly form of
ex-champion Tnckett. What isn’t altogether taken un is
filled with War Crys and other Salvation Army literature
for which the shed is a disseminating centre. Trickett’s
“ testifyings ” are always interesting, and his spare
moments are taken up rehearsing, casting a suspicious
eye on passers-by, and calculating the approach of Feder-
ation and the establishment of interprovincial Freetrade.
Megson, the first day he rode a Humber won
the mile-race in Melb.*

Martin and his Red Bird won Sydney mile
March 4 ; Melb. half-mile and five miles, March 6.* ’
The immortal E. Y. Lowry, socialist-
alderman of Townsville (N.Q.) again (at Towns-
ville Council, the Town-Clerk being in the chair):
“ I beg to move, as an amendment, that Aid.
Lowry be Mayor. I am quite satisfied, having
known Aid. Lowry for so long, that, as far as
ability is concerned, he is quite as well qualified
for the position as any of his colleagues. If
elected, he will pocket the whole salary of the
office for the use of his wife and family. If any
vice-royal visitors come, the citizens will have to
put their hands into their own pockets if they wish
to entertain them ; he will not spend a penny for
that purpose. I believe that Aid. Lowry would
carry on the business of Mayor with credit to
himself and to the town.” After which testi-
monial to Lowry by Lowry, his colleagues beat
Lowry by 8 votes to 4.
The beautiful thing about the Federal
elections was that everybody was satisfied, and
everybody triumphed. Bruce Smith was the only
candidate who confessed publicly to “ personal
disappointment.” His rejection is no doubt due
to the fact that he has not been in Parliament for
several years. The Kyardinal considered that
“my candidature attained its full purpose ” when
he was jolly well licked. He never intended to
win, it seems—went through all the fuss and feud
with the idea of coming in fourteenth or there-
abouts. And Heydon, head of the Mangy Dog
party, said that the hairless canines had achieved
a great moral victory, or a virtual success, or words
to that effect; and they really never wanted to
get into the Convention at all. And everybody
else kept his upper lip stiff and talked in a similar
way—except the Labor-party. The L.P., some-
how, doesn’t feel quite well in its inside.
The Year, Book of Australia for 1897 is,
as usual, a splendid collection of information on
almost every subject, carefully compiled, up-to-
date, and arranged so that every reader can get at
the item he wants without breaking his neck over
other things that he isn’t in search of. There is
hardly any Australian subject concerning which
the Year Book doesn’t give information now ; the
present issue seems to be even more complete than
any of its predecessors. The only complaint is
that it is too exclusively Australian ; if the pro-
prietor could see his way to take in Tasmania and
Maoriland it would be perfect. Even if Maori -
land is too far off his beat Tasmania at least might
be included—its figures already appear in some
tables, but it wants to be included all round. So
far as the five continental provinces are concerned,
however, the Year Book is the best authority
going on almost every topic.

Parson Alexander, of North Melbourne,
who announced publicly that the son of a Jewish
rabbi had been converted and was going to be
baptised as a Presbyterian, has got a bad set back.
His converted Jew writes to the papers stating
that he isn’t going to be baptised as a Presby-
terian, and thereby creates a painful impression
that somebody connected with the story must
have been drinking. Considering that this Jew,
if he had come along, would have been almost the
only reliable Hebrew convert on record, the fact
that he isn’t coming is doubly painful. Converted
Jews are about as rare as are icebergs at Cooktown.
A thousand packages of dynamite, in the
manifest of a steamer just in at Melb., from
London, gave an agreeable surprise to the pas-
sengers.

Melb.’s largest and smallest lawyers are
respectively the Irish giant M‘Kean, whose 20
stone is distributed over 6ft. Bin. of height, and
A. E. Jones, the sft. 2in. Hebrew who has stepped
into Sam Lyons’s police-court practice. The pair
appeared one day in a case at the Melb. City
Court, and, on Jones taking exception to some of
M‘Kean’s law, the big man turned and, looking
down, let out characteristically : “ Why, ye little
whipper-snapper, I cud putt ye in me pocket.”
“If you did,” responded the diminutive Jones,”
“ you’d have more brains in your pocket than you
have in your head.”
