STOCK AND STATION AGENTS,
WOOL & PRODUCE SALESMEN.
Sydney, Isth February, 1897.
CIRCULAR.
Dear Sir,
We now beg to notify to you that on the Ist prox. we add to our well-
known Stock and Station Agency business that of Wool and Produce Salesmen.
This is quite a legitimate branch of our business that we now take up with
confidence.
We have secured the services of Mr. Edward N. Row, lately with Messrs.
Campbell, Nimmo & Co., formerly with Messrs. Goldsbrough & Co., and Dalgety
& Co., of Melbourne, who will conduct this branch of our business under the
supervision of our Managing Directors, and whose acknowledged business
capacity and energy, combined with the well-known position and standing of
this Company, are sufficient guarantee that any Wool or Produce entrusted to
our care will be disposed of to the best advantage.
Our Produce Stores are situated on the Circular Quay and Macquarie Street,
are large, well-lighted, and in every way convenient. Wool Stores we will secure
before the opening of next Wool Season, our present accommodation being ample
for any Wool offering during the Winter months.
As in our Stock business, you may rely that we will conduct this branch of
our business strictly as Selling Brokers only ; neither directly nor indirectly will
we entertain any interest in Produce committed to our care beyond that of realis-
ing the highest price. Buyers, too, may know that they will’compete only with
legitimate competitors. J
Our Produce Sales commence after the Ist March ; but our Stores are now open
for the reception of consignments, which, small or large, shall have equal attention •
a bag of cow hair, a few horns, sheepskins, or shank bones, will be cared for equally

Dunn, one of the employers’ board for
fixing wages in the Vic. boot-trade, was, previously
t» his employing: days, an ultra-radical democrat,
of even an anarchistic cast. With Robert Jones
and one or two others, he was running a journal,
some years ago, embodying their “ acratic,” vege-
tarian and other views. A strike or so in his
boot-factory has presumably altered many of his
old opinions.
Dear Bulletin,— Be the Bermagui mystery;
it is generally believed that Lamont Young and
party were shot; but there really is not one atom
of evidence to warrant the thought. I was on the
field at the time and remember the circumstances.
On the Saturday morning Young came to
M‘Gregor’s claim and watched a dish of dirt being
washed; the yield was Boz., and Young with
others grew really excited about the matter. He
offered to buy a share, but Mac. refused to accept.
He then offered .£6O cash for a twentieth share.
Mac. again declined. “Mind you, it is cash—
come to camp and I’ll pay you now.’’ “No,”
Mac. said, “ I wouldn’t take one hundred and
sixty.” Young then went away. That night the
party disappeared. Next morning at daylight a
boat pulled off from near Young’s tent and went
out to sea ; she was then pulled N. W. and ran
ashore where that same afternoon at 3 o’clock she
was found with a hole chopped in her bottom and
several large stones in her —no doubt to sink her.
There were no bullet holes in her nor any blood
stains. There were 3000 men on the field, and, in
my opinion, some of the worst class of unhanged
ruffians. My opinion is, as it has always been,
that Young and the five others were poisoned for
the cash they had in their tents. As there were
hundreds of shafts varying from 10 to 20 feet deep
in the pure sand, what was there to stop the
bodies from being covered up, and all marks re-
moved by simply filling in holes—sun and wind
would remove all traces of where the shaft had been!
Lamont Young was either buried in the sand or
taken out and thrown overboard. There were
thousands of sharks about. The murderers, after
chopping and trying to sink the boat, could have
quietly walked back and joined the crowd and
then left at their leisure—which, if the mystery is
ever solved, you will find they did. — Cyril G.
A Bulletin correspondent’s dog-story :
“ One evening I saw a boy lead a retriever down
to the bank of the Tutaekuri River (Napier, M.L.),
tie a stone round the dog’s neck, and push him
into the water. The stone slipped, and the dog
scrambled out. _ The young butcher caught him,
knelt on him, tied another stone round his neck,
and pushed him in again. Once more the stone
slipped, and, almost suffocated, the poor brute
managed to struggle out. Just as the young fiend
seized him a third time a stalwart Maori rushed
up and slung the boy right into the current of
swirling eddies. And the dog? He plunged in
and pulled the boy on to the bank.”

A few words as to the progress and growth in
public favour of the Registered Alfalme Herbal
Remedies would not be out of place here, and
would be additional evidence as to their real
genuineness and great virtues as claimed for them
by Herr Rassmussen, their discoverer and sole
proprietor.
The great virtues of the Alfaline Herbal Reme-
dies are so well known that it is quite unnecessary
to say much in regard to them in this notice ; the
very fact of their having been before the public
for so many years, and continually growing in
popularity, is quite sufficient evidence that they
are all they are claimed to be. Taking into con-
sideration the fact that they have been awarded
the Parisian Gold Medal, and that so many thou-
sands of original and unsolicited testimonials have
been received, and may be seen at the Head Office,
some of which appear in his pamphlets, there
should be no difficulty in predicting a great future
for these health-giving Remedies unprecedented
in the History of Herbal Remedies.
About twelve years ago Herr Rassmussen intro-
duced these Remedies to the Australian public by
opening a Depot for their sale at 547 George
Street, Sydney, N.S.W., his present Head Office,
and although at that time he did not advertise in
any journal, the wonderful virtues of these Reme-
dies were known far and wide within a few
months. Orders for them came from every
quarter, not only from all the Australian colonies,
but from all over the world. In 1891 the clamour
for the Alfaline Herbal Remedies had become so
great that it became impossible to supply everyone
from the Sydney Depot. Letters arrived by thou-
sands, many orders came by telegraph, and some
people even travelled over a thousand miles to
make sure of obtaining some of these God-inspired
Remedies. What was to be done ? It was Herr
Rassmussen’s duty to see that every sufferer from
the Blood, Skin, Nerves, Liver, and other affec-
tions, whose very life and happiness depended on
these Remedies, should be able to obtain same
without undue delay. Herr Rassmussen had thus
no other alternative than to open Branch Depots
for the sale of the Alfaline Remedies in every
colony, and, if possible, in every country in the
world, as he desired to keep the sale of his Herbal
Remedies in his own hands entirely, and thus
avoid the agency of chemists, who generally have
a substitute which they prefer to sell instead of
the real article. A start was made at once in that
direction, and a very large and palatial Depot
was opened at 150 Bourke Street, Melbourne, in
1891. Immediately after, a fine Branch was
established at 154 Rundle Street, Adelaide, fol-
lowed by the opening of an extensive Branch at
91 Lambton Quay, Wellington, New Zealand. A
large Branch was then established at the corner of
Hay and William Streets, Perth, W.A., and
shortly after another Branch, next to Coolgardie
Hotel, Bailey Street, Coolgardie, and N 0.2 Lin-
dell’s Chambers, Hannan street, Kalgoorlie, W.A.,
not to forget the very extensive Branch opened
some seven years ago at 160 Queen Street, Bris-
bane, which is now the Head Depot for Queens-
land, and finally a Branch just opened at Charters
Towers, North Queensland.
It is not unreasonable to ask, “ Is it possible
such success should follow unless these well-known
Herbal Remedies are all they are claimed to be,
and is it likely that their popularity should keep
growing to such an extent, unless they contained
exceptional virtues out of the common ? ” The
evidence that Herr Rassmussen’s Herbal Remedies
contain wonderful virtues is simply overwhelming,
as he possesses thousands of Testimonials to that
effect.
The whole of these Branches are each doing a
large and flourishing business. There is not a
country on the face of the globe where these Re-
medies have not been heard of, and orders arrive
daily from the uttermost parts of the earth.

you that r having to pass their camp next day, I found

Legal Notices-
Patents Office,
Sydney, 12th March, 1897-
APPLICATION FOR AMENDMENT OF
LETTERS PATENT UNDER THE
PATENTS LAW AMENDMENT ACT,
1895.
NOTICE is hereby given that any person or
Grsons intending to oppose this application must
ive at the Patents Office, within one month
from the 12th instant, notice in the prescribed
form of his or their objections thereto.
Letters Patent No. 453, 21st January, 1888,
granted to The Cassel Gold Extracting Com-
pany (Limited), for “Improvements in obtain-
ing gold and silver from ores and other
compounds.”
The Australian Gold Recovery Company
(Limited), as assignees of the Cassel Gold Extract-
ing Company (Limited) [through their Patent
Agent, Fred. Walsh, manager for Edward Waters],
have applied for leave to amend the Specification
of the Letters Patent above referred to.
The proposed amendments are as follow :
1. To insert after the paragraph terminating
with the words “or closed vessels” the follow-
ing
Whereas a claim simply and broadly for the
use of cyanogen or a cyanide or cyanogen-
yielding substance might be held to in-
clude solutions of any strength, and
whereas solutions containing cyanogen in
a proportion not exceeding eight parts of
cyanogen to one thousand parts of water
are most advantageous, having a selec-
tive action such as to dissolve gold and
silver in preference to the baser metals,
whilst stronger solutions, besides being
more, costly, are of less practical value, we
hereby disclaim the use of such stronger
solutions.”
2. In the claim to strike out the words
"cyanogen or cyanide or other substance or com-
pound containing or yielding cyanogen substan-
tially as specified,” and to substitute therefor the
following words :
“ A solution containing cyanogen in a propor-
tion not exceeding eight parts of cyanogen
to one thousand parts of water.”
A. PARRY LONG,
[8269] Examiner of Patents.

An indignant Anglican clergyman writes
to a recent Guardian, English Church paper,
because circulars are sent out, from trading rev.
gentlemen, to all the clergy, offering MSS. ser-
mons, any quantity, for lOd. each ! Suitable for
ordinary times or special, as weddings, funerals,
harvest rejoicing, times of drought, times of abun-
dant rain, and so forth—says the prospectus. The
point seems to be overlooked that the demand
makes the supply. Yes, the clergyman choked
with tears over the oak-coffined and gilt-clasped
corpse of his beloved friend, the Squiah, fetches
out the tenpenny sermon, in genuine MSS., mind
you, no pantographing, to show he did it all out
of his own head. “ Brethren, I feel I can hardly
proceed further.” Oh, boo-hoo! And then the
tenpenny MSS. (bearing the marks of the pint-
pot of the Rev. Julius Boozer, M.A., who wrote
the sermon for the sweater and got for it the price
of a couple of pints) returns thanks to Almighty
God for this glorious rain. God save Our Gracious
—Establishment. j
A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 6Goft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic : 1, New Books ; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery ; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods ; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures ; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition ;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
mam walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long. *
The tariff of a boarding-house, located on
the outskirts of a W.A. town among the big
mines, was—board, 275. 6d. a week, or single
meals 2s. Another started in opposition—board,
255., single meals Is. 6d. In close proximity there
was a small store with a wine-and-beer license.
The business of the store fell, as everybody about
the place went boarding, so the storekeeper started
a “ tin-dog boarding-house”: half-a-tin of dog,
glass of local beer and a piece of bread, one
shilling. Then the other two boarding-houses
shut up.
At Bloomsbury (England), a dressmaker
has been suing a parson of the Anglican Church
for the recovery of <£l, which she put in the plate
while in a state of temporary insanity. Asked for
evidence as to the insanity, she said she knew she
was insane because she put the pound in the plate;
she couldn’t have done so otherwise. She was
quite composed when she went into the church,
but presently a strange feeling came over her, and
she dropped her sovereign in the collection, and
didn’t fully recover her senses for three days.
Case dismissed. The law invariably shirks awk-
ward questions, and the question how much money
a sane dressmaker would naturally put in the plate
was too much for it.
C : Q. Postal department vagaries. If,
when travelling about Queensland, you leave a
township that you have intimated to your possible
correspondents as your latest postal address, and
if you take the trouble to go to the local post-office
there and inform the postmaster to forward your
letters on to another town, when you get those
letters you are charged extra stampage. If, on
the other hand, you don’t take any trouble to in-
form the local post-office, but tell your Mr. Public-
house or your Mrs. Hashfakir that you are going
on to “ Anywhere,”- when you get your letters
afterwards, you will see the old address marked
out and the words “Try Anywhere” blue-pen-
cilled, whereupon you are not charged any extra
fees for stamps. If you travel out of Q. and a
letter follows you into N.S.W., or S.A., you get it
all right in every case ivithout extra fees. Suppose
a case : X is travelling and Y is his correspondent
in Brisbane. Xis fool enough to inform each
postmaster at each stopping-place to forward his
letters to his next destination ; Y writes to X at
St. George, placing a twopenny stamp on the
envelope enclosing the letter. X has gone on to
Cunnamulla ; letter follows with “2d. to pay ” on
it (perhaps “4d.”). X has now reached Eulo;
letter follows with “4d. to pay.” By this, Xis
at Thargomindah ; letter on trail, with “ 6d. to
pay.” But X has gone on to Oontoo; letter dogs
him, “Bd. to pay.” Here, the P.O. finds that X
has crossed over the border to Innamincka (S. A.);
letter collars him there, with surcharges all erased
— nothin;) to pay ! The P.O. nearly all over the
world carries letters for shifting addresses “on its
own.” The land of Nelsonboodlethynne is the
grasping exception.

CHITIS AND ASTHMA RAPIDLY
CURED BY Hearne's Bronchitis cure.
Those who have taken this medicine are amazed
at its wonderful influence. Sufferers from any
form of bronchitis, distressing cough, difficulty of
breathing, hoarseness, pain or soreness in the
chest, experience delightful and immediate relief,
and to those who are subject to colds on the chest
it is invaluable, as it effects a complete cure. It
is most comforting in allaying tickling and irrita-
tion in the throat, and giving strength to the voice*,
and it never allows a cough or asthma to become
chronic. No house should be without it, as, taken
at the beginning, a dose is generally sufficient,
and a complete cure is certain.
“Mr. W. G. HEARNE.—Dear Sir, —Your medicine has
cured me of bronchitis and asthma, from which I suffered
for upwards of seven years, during which period I was
scarcely ever free from coughs, and frequently the diffi-
culty of breathing was so distressing that for nights in
succession I had to sit up. I write you this acknowledg-
ment from a sense of duty, as in my case every other
treatment had failed. For a year previous I had been
getting very much worse, and at the time I obtained your
medicine I was confined to bed suffering from a most
violent cough, expectorating blood and matter, and
apparently beyond hope of recovery. The first dose of
the medicine gave me welcome relief, and I steadily
improved as I continued the treatment, until I became,
as I am now, quite well.—Yours sincerely, H. WALKER,
Balmain, Sydney."
“ My son, six years old, had bronchitis for 12 months.

The Mystery of Murboola .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Melbourne Exhibition of 1880, locally de-
fined as a “national boon,” was so chiefly to Mel-
Dourne publicans, who spun webs to catch the un-
sophisticated from afar. These, identifiable by
the grass seeds, shreds of wool, and raging thirsts
they carried about their persons, were attracted
by thousands to the garish apotheosis of damaged
whisky. They brought, too, the flat-chested
females of their race, guiltless of curves, if not of
freckles and barbaric adornment. Those Austra-
lians who, surviving Melbourne publicans, police,
cabmen and calico jemmies, returned home, cursed
the City of Stinks categorically and copiously.
All did not return. Numbers went out —Heaven
and the local banditti only knew how. Some,
swollen, eel-bitten and carious, floated in due time
to the surface-slime of loathsome Yarra. Some
vanished for evermore. Others were variously
found after many days. Queer epoch ! Epoch of
money-grabbing brutality masquerading as “ pro-
gress ’’—epoch of sealskin jackets and burst boots,
of diamonds and dirty linen. Its secret history
remains to be told. Here, one insignificant page
only.
* * * *
Daniel Joad and his wife, Seba, from
Murboola, “ way-back ” nor’west of Victoria, were
among the visitors. Life was very real at Mur-
boola. The Joads had been married and lived
there four years. Joad was not “ nice.” He was
a mean man. He was cruel —unhappy when not
hurting something. Dogs, passing him, glanced
obliquely and soliloquised about his character ;
children shunned him ; white men yearned exceed-
ingly, sometimes irrepressibly, to kick him.
Revenge, lust, and chiefly avarice prompted his
marriage. Seba took him to demonstrate her
utter indifference to Tom Black, who, after mak-
ing hot, sweet love to her, had married “ that
odious, red-headed creature, Sarah Belsey.” And
then, to demonstrate her consistency, Seba had
selected Joad because there was strong physical
likeness between the two men. Mentally and
morally each was the other’s antithesis. Between
Sarah and Joad there had been passages. None
knew better than Joad that Black was indifferent
to Sarah and loved Seba, and that Black made a
loveless match to save the too-ardent Sally from
shame not of his causing—though the woman led
Black to believe otherwise. Yet the amiable
Joad proposed, when he gained possession
of Seba, to wreak on her the venomed hate
he bore for Black. The Blacks went away
to Gippsland. The Joads, after marriage,
journeyed nor’west to land bought for and
settled upon Seba by her father, whose only
child she was (and he a widower). The old
man farmed near Stawell, and worshipped
“that theer gell o’mine.” She, handsome, tall,
lithe, full-busted and Indian-eyed, had some edu-
cation, more imagination, read comprehendingly
of poetical and prose passion, and played, not
inharmoniously, on the metallic made-in-Germany
“ pianner ” which the old fellow carted home one
day with his seed-potatoes. She dreamt of fuller
existence, in which calves and butter had small
place, and yearned for the infinite—which meant
a good husband. That bill, her father’s neighbors
agreed, Joad did not fill. They wondered such a
girl, “ with a tidy bit of money, too,” should
marry “ that sneaking cur,” and trust herself in
a lonely land with one whose antecedents were
vague. Even new bucolic populations demand
pedigrees.
* » * *
Six months after marriage, Seba carried a
bruised face and battered body. Later, the best
revolver purchasable. Later still, Joad, presum-
ably by way of atonement, introduced a young
lady with rolling eyes and much cerise hair wan-
dering about a wanton neck and bust —“ t» help
with the house-work,” he explained. Ere many
days Seba drove a dishevelled female to the
railway, twenty miles away, returning with a
sturdy lock, which she fixed on the door of a bed-
room—in future hers alone.
“You lay hand on me or come into that room
and I’ll shoot you. If I miss, I’ll poison you with
as littl* shame as I would a dingo.”
Joad believed her. A new look had come into
her deep and once dreamful eyes—such a look as
Joad’s might have worn. He wished to kill her.
Perhaps he might have done so scatheless. The
nearest neighbors—save the men Joad employed,
who lived six hundred yards away and seldom
stayed more than a few weeks—were five miles
distant; the nearest pub., six, and Joad never
entered it, soaking slyly at home. Twenty miles
separated Murboola from the township. Thus
tragedy at Murboola had small risk of discovery.
Seba’s safety lay in the fact that she had willed
Murboola away from Joad, and he knew it. But
most of the money in the bank was in Joad’s
name. Seba didn’t know that.
* • « *
These tw« visited Melbourne in 1880. He
bitterly opposed her coming, but gave in with the
air of one who whispers to himself —“Wait!”
Arrived, they took cab for a third-rate hotel.
Blocked by street traffic, both —strange coincidence
—caught glimpse of Tom Black and Sarah, evi-
dently also just arrived. Seba crimsoned ; Joad
uncovered his teeth hyena-wise. It was Seba who
arranged for rooms at the shabby hotel. Joad,
never alighting, and saying he was going to the
bank, drove away. Seba, knocked-up, went
straight to bed, though it was afternoon. All
night she lay in waking stupor, varied only by
dreary nightmare. Four years of Joad had affected
even her healthy nerves. Towards noon she
dressed, and encountering a squalidly - smart
chambermaid in the untidy passage, begged for
some tea.
“Your man must be having a high old time,”
remarked this vestal; “Mrs. Corr, the lady as
keeps the hotel, says he ain’t been in all night.”
Seba loathed Joad. Yet the news dismayed
her. Save in dreams, she had seen no city. What
little she had seen of the bestial rush and selfish-
ness of this appalled her. Alone and penniless —
Joad on some pretence had borrowed her purse —
the city people seemed to her more arid and in-
hospitable than the cruel scrub in the blazing nor’-
west. She was too ill to remember that a tele-
gram would bring her money.
Presently Mrs. Corr herself appeared—Milesian,
robust, resplendent in satin ; bearing great gold
plates on neck and breast, as warrior’s armor;
her pudgy fingers bloated with rings—veritable
knuckledusters.
“ An’ what’s the matter wid you. Are ye ill ? ”
“ Yes,” said Seba, “ and I’m troubled about my
husband ; he’s not back, I hear, and—and he’s
left me without a penny.”
The grass-widow of Mr. Corr—he sweated for
gold at Temora just then—was rich in life’s ex-
perience, unscholastic wisdom won in miners’
camps and shady grog-shops. Irish by birth, her
education had been racy of earlier Australia.
With moral code more than elastic, chastity less
than doubtful, and a vocabulary speckled by pro-
fanity, she, a past-mistress in the art of lambing-
down, had yet for real modesty and distress the
soft heart not uncommon in bold, bad men. She
could respect a “square” woman. Seba was
assuredly that, was ill, and troubled with a hus-
band who evidently painted towns red. Men
were beasts —even Mrs. Corr admitted.
“ Get to bed, child. I’ll send up some proper
tucker. An’ ye needn’t bother ’bout money. Sure
ye can have what ye want. I’ll send one of the
chaps to look for your man. Give us his photo. ;
we’ve none of us seen him.”
She reappeared with breakfast, made Seba take
a little brandy —“ out o’ me own bottle ;no hurt in
it ” —opened the windows, lowered the blinds, left
the day’s paper, and went. Ten minutes after-
wards, this strong-armed amazon nearly laid-out a
cabman with a lemonade-bottle for attempting to
abduct an opulent and inebriated boarder.
The last thing Seba remembered was reading
the names of Mr. and Mrs. Tom Black in the pas-
senger-list of the Chimborazo, bound for England.
Towards night she felt worse, and a doctor
was sent for. No sign of Joad. One of the led-
captains so often in evidence in hotels gen-
tlemen who toil and spin not, but are ever
gloriously arrayed in liquor —discovered that Joad
had driven straight to the head office of the
bank he dealt with, and had there changed
many notes into gold. Five days in all passed
without further news of him. Seba was slowly
recovering. Then one of the Hibernian retainers,
suffering unwonted spasms of sobriety and lucidity,
suggested an information for wife-desertion. This
laid, a photo, was given the police, and they were
informed that the absentee had disappeared carry-
ing much gold. That evening the usual “ ener-
getic officer” made the usual “clever capture.”
He spotted his man, who was seriously, though
unaggressively drunk, mooning aimlessly about
Collins-street. Searched, the man had neither
papers nor money, save three one-pound notes
slipped into his vest lining. He was utterly
befogged ; the police-surgeon said he had been
dreadfully hocussed, probably with some prepara-
tion of opium—and more than once. The inspec-
tor managed to piece this story from the prisoner’s
incoherency—had gone with wife to unknown
hotel; wife had gone out; he, later ; forgot locality
and never knew name of hotel; had enquired at
another hotel, where had one glass of something to
drink, and—and it was “time to get the milkers
in.” Laughed idiotically when told he hadn’t
seen his wife for five nights. Opined it was
“jolly goo’ job.” Mrs. Corr bailed out the
“ bloomin’ deserter ” and drove him, still doltish
and at times unconscious, to the hotel. Seba
wouldn’t see him. She cried a good deal when she
heard he was back.
* * * *
On the second day of his return. “ Come
up and see your wife, you bloomin’ deserter,” said
Mrs. Corr. He didn’t seem either anxious or
lucid, but went. Mrs. Corr pushed him into
Seba’s room, discreetly retiring. As he entered
Seba rose with a darkening face and lurid eyes ;
but, looking up, went deathly white to the lips
and put her hand to her heart—“ Y ou ! you 1 My
God ! ”
* * * »
But the interview must have ended not
unpleasantly. When the man came out it was
with shining eyes and brightened intellect. He
laughed good laughter ; his voice was resonant
with new happiness. Mrs. Corr found Seba a
woman all transfigured—lovelight in her eyes, a
bride’s blush on her cheek.
“ Well, ye two bangs me ! I thought ye hated
him, and clivil a much he seemed to care for ye.
An’ now he’s shoutin’ the house down for drinks
for all hands and—and ye look like a philanderin
miss wid her first boy calf. My word ! but you’re
pretty, child.”
• » * *
Mrs. Joad and the “Deserter,” as the
landlady persisted in calling him, departed next
day ; but not before he had made addition to the
stupendous bric-a-brac which adorned her ample
person. She was loud in his praises—“ the foinest
feller iver came into the house; free wid his
money, for all he’d lost such a lot; must be well
in ; a proper man, that Joad,” and so on.
“ Wot d’ye call him? ” asked an old bush hand
who was knocking down a cheque of prepossessing
dimensions.
“Joad?”
“ Joad, as one time lived in Stawell ?
Ves J *
“ Don’t tell me,” said the old chap. “ I knowed
Joad, rot him ! Knowed him five, years ago, a
measly hound ! That wasn’t Joad.”
“You old fool, you’ll be seein’ tiger-snakes to-
night,” and Mrs. Corr fined him the drinks.
* * * *
Bht, after all, Mrs. Joad was fated to make
the journey without male escort. She stopped, en
route, at Stawell, getting to her father’s house in
time to close his eyes. He, long ailing, bad
steadily refused to bother “that theer gell o
mine.” As, however, she had “ got thro her
pleasurin’ and is come, I thinks I’ll take the
opportoonity to peg out ’’—which he did. It was
three weeks before Seba, still cavalierless, reached
Murboola. Her troubles must have made her
irritable. She found fault with everything the
men had done in her absence. Result, they de-
manded cheques, departing with blasphemy. That
night, strange to say, other men arrived to take
their places—which was suggestive of previous
rehearsal. It was a month before the Deserter
joined. For two years thereafter these twain
dwelt together as happily as it is given to mortals
to live. He had seemingly altered all old habits,
never stirring away from lonely Murboola, but
leaving Seba to transact all banking and other
business. Making no acquaintances, they were
no more widely known at the end of these two
years than before. Yet population was slowly
but surely closing round them, and they had
greatly prospered.
* * * *
Then the man was called away. News reached
them—news which took him swiftly and with
secrecy to Melbourne, thence to Sydney. There
he closed the eyes of a wretched woman who had
been battered to death by her—her husband.
The brute had escaped. Meanwhile a great dread
seized Seba—not apparently of loneliness, because
there were now married men at Murboola, whose
wives would have kept her company. But Seba
went, day and night, with pale lips and drawn
face. So haunted by this unspoken horror was
she that, for the first time for two years, she got
out, cleaned, loaded, and carried the pistol she
had bought to protect herself from Joad.
♦ * * *
Her terror was prophetic. The abomina-
tion she trembled at and expected befel. One
night a window was tried. The next minute it
was forced, and a man, or devil, dropped into the
room.
“ I have found you out at last, you ! ”
* * * *
Five days later the “deserter” returned.
He had not been an hour in the house before Seba
said :
“I’ll live in this vile place no more. I’ll sell it,
or burn it, or plough it with salt, but I’ll live here
never again.”
“ Well, that is strange ! ” replied the other. “ I,
or rather you, had a grand offer for it this morn-
ing. If you mean going, we could clear out in
two days.”
“ For God’s sake, yes,” she gasped.
* * * *
In the Spring of 1883 a Mr. and Mrs.
Sebath stayed at the Langham, London—“ estim-
able people who had made money in Australia.”
An old gentleman, who had a son in Victoria,
told them one day the story—then a little anti-
quated—of what the newspapers called the “Mur-
boola Mystery.” Briefly, this : Within a week of
the Joads leaving Murboola, the dead body of
Joad, strangely preserved, was found in a deepish
hole near the house. A gunshot wound in his
head and a revolver (one chamber discharged) in
his hand, clearly proved the case, said the doctors,
to be one of suicide. But—and here the essence of
the mystery—both Joads had, and without shadow
of doubt, been seen in Melbourne three hours
before Joad’s dead body was found hundreds of
miles away 1
* * * *
“ How remarkable,” drawled Mrs. Sebath,
with strangely-quivering lips.
J. EVISON.

SOAP MAKERS

TO HER MAJESTY

AND HIS ROYAL HIGHNESS THE
Prince of Wales.
Off the Grass.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They were boasting on “ The Greenhide” of their
nags of fancy breed,
And stuffing them with bran and oats to run in
Gumleaf Town,
But we hadn’t got a racehorse that was worth a
dish of feed,
So didn’t have a “ Buckley’s show ” to take the
boasters down.
For old Midnight was.in Sydney and we couldn’t
get him up
In time for Gumleaf Races if it had been worth
our while ;
The Chorus colt was far too light to win the Gum-
leaf Cup,
And we didn’t own a hackney that could finish
out the mile.
But we couldn’t watch them win it while we never
had a say, ‘
So we mustered up the horses, and we caught
old Myall King ;
lie’s as brave as ever galloped, but he’s twelve if
he’s a day,
And we couldn’t help but chuckle at the humor
of the thing.
But, though shaky in the shoulders, he’s the daddy
of them all,
He’s the gamest bit of horseflesh from the
Snowy to the Bree,
One of those that’s never beaten, coming every
time you call;
One of those you sometimes read about but very
seldom see.
He’s the don at every muster and the king of every
camp;
He’s the lad to stop the pikers when they take
you on the rush ;
And he loves the merry rattle of the stockwhip
and the tramp
Of the cockhorned mulga scrubbers when they’re
breaking in the brush.
He can foot the “Greenhide” brumbies if they
take a mile of start,
And if they get him winded in a gallop on the
plain
He’s as game as any lion, and he carries such a
heart
You can never say he’s beaten, for he’ll always
come again !
So we put up Jack the Stockman with his ten
pounds overweight,
And he lengthened out the leathers half-a-foot
and gave a smile:
“ I don’t suppose you’ll see us when they’re fairly
in the straight,
But we’ll make the beggars travel, take my
oath, for half-a-mile ! ”
And they started, and the old horse jumped away
a length in front,
And every post they came to gave the brown a
longer lead
Till it seemed that there was nothing else but
Myall in the hunt,
With his load of station honor and his weight
of mulga feed !
Then the bay mare Bogan Lily started out to cut
him down ;
She had travelled out five hundred miles to win
the Gumleaf Cup,
And she couldn’t well get beaten by a hack in
Gumleaf Town,
When she had to pay expenses for her owner’s
journey up.
So she started out to catch the old brown camp-
horse from the Bush,
And a furlong from the finish she could nose his
rider’s knee,
Then you should have heard the shouting of the
Bogan Lily push,
And the flinging of their hats up was a sight for
you to see !
But old Myall King had often been as nearly beat
before.
And he steadied off a little while the mare shot
out ahead,
Then he shook his ears and gripped the bit—you
should have heard us roar
As he came at Bogan Lily with his flanks a
streak of red !
And the little bay mare, beaten, gave him best
and threw it up,
And we heard her rider murmur as he saw the
brown horse pass
And Jack the Stockman drop his hands and win
the Gumleaf Cup—
“ Beat by a cripple of a camp-horse, off the
grass ! ”
Then we led him in a winner, and they cheered
him from the stand,
With the black sweat running channels from his
forearm to his foot,
And the white foam on his shoulder till you
couldn’t see the brand,
And the crimson bloodstains scattered over
spur and flank and boot.
So we carried off the honors of the meeting —and
the notes ;
And the men on Greenhide River, when they
see our fellows pass,
Will tell you this in whispers, “You can train
your nags on oats,
But be careful when you’re racing those d —d
scrubbers off the grass l”
W. H. OGILVIE.
Some time ago, at Zeehan (Tas.), a large
tree was blown down during a westerly gale. A
tent which stood in the line of fall just escaped by
being between the two main branches of the tree.
Only a little branch touched and tore the after
part, causing the balance to fall. The usual num-
ber of people rushed to the rescue of the man in
the tent, and were astonished to hear a voice from
beneath the canvas: “ None of yer darned jokes !
I’ll make yer pay for the blanky tent.”
Ed. Bulletin, —My own experience re
the “ N.S.W. Relief Works Swindle.” Eighty
men were engaged to thin out the Murray River
gum forests below Koondrook—wages, 6s. 6d. per
day and find themselves in tucker and cook.
Govt, found tools, utensils, etc. Their ration-bill
altogether ought not to be more than Bs. per week
per man (in local shearing-sheds the ration-bill
is less than that figure). The only men put on
at first were the unemployed from Sydney, who
travelled on free passes. Of the 80 first engaged
60 left within two months, mainly because they
wanted to get back to Sydney. Local men were
afterwards put on, as they considered 30s. per
week clear good wages for eight hours clean
work with the axe. But a local publican reaped
the biggest harvest.—Yours, &c., H.D.
The First Axiom.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Well, what do you want ? ” Harley asked. He
had just been interrupted once in a difficult
explanation of an arithmetical problem, and this
second interruption was a little too much for his
patience. Harley was not a model teacher. He
couldn’t keep in a groove, he was too versatile.
There was no even tenor in Harley’s way. He
could explain the knottiest problem in the most lucid
manner, propound the wisest doctrine, unravel
the mysteries of the causes that actuated men to
certain deeds in history, teach for an hour in a
manner that held his hearers breathless, stirred
their intellects to solve, or their minds to apply—
but then Harley would shut off steam and drift.
Then the school drifted too. Harley’s brilliancy
was intermittent, his genius displayed itself in
meteoric flashes. He was no born instructor,
he was an elucidator—for an hour —and his hours
were far apart. The teacher must keep on, keep
on, cause and effect, effect and cause, a continuous
stream, logical deduction, logical conclusion. This,
however, is not notes of a lesson on school-
management.
Harley looked impatiently at the'child before
him —a little girl with dirty, naked feet, unkempt
hair, and untidy, torn skirt. Harley hated dirt—
he was cleanly to a degree. Teaching was all very
well, he used to say, but the associations in the
public schools were trying, very trying. With a
superior class of children he did not object to the
work, but the dirt, the uncouth manner, the
rough and vulgar speech, these were too much for
Harley’s patience. He never recognised that he
was there to remove them, to raise the standard,
improve the conditions, and so to find pleasure
and reward in his work. “ Why don’t you work,
child ? ” said Harley fiercely. The class tittered ;
Harley scowled, and there was silence; the class
knew Harley—there was a cane resting against the
easel.
The girl held out a note. It was written on a
leaf of an exercise-book, and it was dirty. Peters
took it:
piece i have to keep this girl atome for lissy smith
Beating her every day she is Coming home from school
she wated yestday and pulled her hair and theumped her
on the back witch maid my girl sick
Yours truly
Mas. Ryan.
* Oh ! ” said Harley slowly and questioningly.
The girl twisted a dirty part of her skirt into a
corner, and began to bite it, pulling it in jerks
every now and then from between her teeth.
Harley took up his cane and tapped on the board.
The whole school came to silence and attention
m a moment. The teachers fell back from before
their classes, or looked up from their work among
the pupils.
“ Mr. Brown, send Lizzie Smith to me.”
“ Lizzie Smith, go to Mr. Harley.” Lizzie
Smith rose and came.
“ The school—resume work ! ” said Harley. The
school resumed. “Old Harley might have got
that youngster without stopping all the school. I
wonder how he’d like to be pulled up every now
and then in his work,” grumbled onepupil-teacher
to another near him. “He himself is always pull-
mg up, that’s why he doesn’t see it. As is the
teacher, so is the school! Don’t growl, go in for a
yo u don’t like it,” was the answer.
I here are worse than Harley.”
Wellj Lizzie Smith, have you been thumping
Mary Ryan ? ’ Harley asked severely.
Please, sir, no sir; I never done nothin’ to ’er.
Please, sir, she’s alius callin’ us names, sir—me
an’ me sister,” answered Lizzie Smith, looking at
Harley with vulgar resentment, shocked that he
should think of asking her such a question, accus-
ing her of such an offence.
“Please, sir, she did !” asserted Mary Ryan,
nodding her head expressively at Lizzie Smith.
Harley took a long breath and looked at them.
“ Get along, Mary Ryan.” Mary got. “And as
for you, Lizzie Smith, leave Mary Ryan alone,
that’s all, or you’ll get into trouble ; go back to
your class. I ’ll enquire further.” Lizzie, indig-
nant, went.
Harley’s explanation was spoiled, he set his class
a silent lesson, and drifted. Noon came, and the
school was dismissed. The subject for the after-
noon was Euclid. Harley had resumed, and he and
his class were once more intent. Lizzie Smith
came in, ten minutes late. She handed a note to
Harley. More interruption ! Harley thought the
note was for.being late. “Go to your place,’’said
Harley, putting aside the note unread.
“ Please, mother says to read what she’s writ.”
Harley stared. Lizzie Smith looked vindictive.
Harley took up the note again.
“ Sir, I rite these lines to you to let you know that what
Mrs. Ryan said to you in her note was untrue, that I can
pruve by Mrs. Murphy she had to take her Children
from the publick school an’ send them to the privet
threw Ryans, they 7 no Children can move for her for she
chase them across the paddick with a broom when she
drunk, and if you take any notice of her that got to be
brought home in a Chinaman’s cart drunk and beging
beating other pepoles' Children for what she says you
find yourself in a nice box, for I antend to make more to
do about if you go to the mistress she tell you what Mrs.
Murphy had to take her Children from school for
kindly fourdard me the not(e) that Mrs. Ryan sent to you
if you don’t wish to get yourself into any trouble and I
don’t wish to get you into trouble as I never had no trou-
ble with no fo (of) the niberous (neighbours) before she
came to live next door my children never been able to
look out the gate for fear of her chasing them.
Yours, MRS. SMITH.
Harley held the note abstractedly, pondering what
he should do. He had kept Mrs. Ryan’s note, he
took it from his pocket and compared it with the
other. Lizzie Smith stood contemplating him,
expectant. The boy who had been demonstrating
a proposition upon the board, noting Harley’s long
abstraction, took up again his proof. His words
broke on Harley’s ears—“ And since things that are
equal to the same thing are equal to one another,
by the Ist axiom; therefore “Stop!”
cried Harley, “ I didn’t tell you to resume.” “Go
to your place, Lizzie Smith.” “Go on, now,”
he said, turning again to the boy. The boy had
solved the problem.
A.G.A.
Other times, other manners. Down to the
middle of the 16th century it was the usual thing',
not only to pay counsel with money, but also to
provide them with meat and drink while they
were on their client’s business. In an old bill of
costs (1485) is found: “For breakfast at West-
minster spent on our counsel, one shilling and
sixpence.” That may have been very well in
those days ; but fancy suitors having to provide
meat and drink—especially drink—for some of the
members of the N.S. W. bar !
